in this book


2.1 Basics of REST

One of the modern architectures of applications is client–server

model. In this model, we have two diﬀerent roles. Server provides  
some kind of service to the clients who request that service. To  
simplify things, servers do any kind of data processing, clients

use processed data from servers.

Usually, we have much more clients than servers. Here is one  
real-world example of client-side architecture. We all know what is  
Facebook and most of us use it. We have a several ways to use

Facebook, via mobile app (Android, iOS) or via web browser (on  
our computer, laptop or mobile phone). Web and mobile  
Facebook applications represent clients.

Usually, the ﬁrst thing that we do in our Facebook applications is

login. When we login to Facebook client sends request to server,  
server accepts request, process data from request (check user  
name and password) and send response back to client. If  
username and password are correct response will be positive, if  
not error will be sent to client.

Server and clients must know how to communicate between  
themselves. Client must know how to prepare and send data and

what to expect as server response. These communications are  
usually referred as Application Programming Interface Its good  
practice to change initially deﬁned API at little as possible. Even a


small change requires changes on all applications that use that

API.

Representational State Transfer (REST) is became very popular in

the last years, so people use this term equally with term API, but  
these are not the same. REST represents software architecture  
(not a protocol as is usually though) that deﬁnes set of  
constraints that can be used for developing web server-side  
applications. In modern architectures, clients and server are  
distributed to the network and communicate via Internet, so most

modern servers are web servers.

The main idea of REST architecture is to use HTTP protocol to  
transfer new representation of resources from server to client. API  
that applies REST constants are called Restful API. Every API call  
is deﬁned with couple of aspects.

Uniform Resource Identiﬁer represents the ﬁrst aspect. This is a

simple string that can be used to determine resource that we  
want to work with.

Standard HTTP methods (GET, POST, PUT, PATCH and DELETE)  
are the second aspect. We will use diﬀerent methods to perform  
diﬀerent operations on a resource. If we want to read  
representation of a resource we use GET method. GET is often  
called “safe” method because the state of resource will remain  
unchanged.


Other methods can change state of a resource, but if we apply  
them multiple times to a resource, change will always be the  
same. For example, if we call API to set new Facebook password  
multiple times, the same password will always be set. These  
methods are called

There is a small guide on how to use idempotent methods:

Should be used for the creation of new resources.

Should be used to delete the resource. We can have two types of  
delete implementation on server side. We can completely delete  
resources or we can leave resources but change value of some  
state ﬂag to

PUT and Should be used for edit operations. PUT for update of  
whole resource, PATCH for update of one part of the resource.  
For example, if we have an API that change the whole user  
proﬁle (username, password, ﬁrst name, last name, …) we will  
use PUT. If we have API to change just password we will use  
PATCH.

If we use HTTP methods as described, this will allow us to have  
same URI for all APIs for same resource and to use method to  
decide which operation we will execute on resource. There is  
another approach, but he is less in the of the REST. We can use  
only one method (usually POST) and have unique URI to choose  
operation.


A media type represents third and the last aspect. It is used to  
identify format of content transferred through HTTP. We will use  
JSON Object for our API. JSON is simple, human-readable format  
which stores data in attribute-value pairs. It is very easy to  
convert from JSON to Go struct and vice versa. Here is an  
example of JSON object.

{  
"Name":"John",  
"Surname":"Doe",  
"Gender":"Male"  
}

We will see practical usage of all aspects in later chapter,  
especially in chapters where we will be talk about HTTP layer and  
handler. Now we will discuss about set of constraints deﬁned by  
the REST.

We mentioned REST constraints couple of times, now it is time  
to see a little bit more details about them. There are six basic  
constraints:

Client We already talk about this constraint. The main idea is to  
separate user interface (client often refereed as front end who  
request for service) and data processing and storage (server often  
refereed as back end who provides service).

When we want to create RESTful system it’s very important to  
enable communication between client and server. This means that


server has no need to store any context or additional information  
about client. All information needed for service are in request.  
This way of communication is called statelessness.

It is common for modern client applications to cache server  
response. This can improve the performance of system but also  
can cause problems. Cached data can create invalid requests and  
cause inconsistent data. To prevent that response must inform the  
client if it is cacheable or not.

Layered It’s good practice to create layered systems. Layered  
system can separate business, cache and security logic, for  
example. We can put load balancer between client and server.  
This will allow us to have multiple server applications and load  
balancer will delegate the job. Clients will send requests to load  
balancer, and here we can use security layer to verify requests, the  
cache layer to return cached responses and send request to one  
of servers. Application created by these guidelines is scalable, new  
server can be added without any interruptions in communication  
between client and server.

Code on This concern is optional. It allows server to send  
executable code to client. Usually, all kinds of scripts can be send  
to client. We will not have this functionality in our application.

Uniform The most important concern. If all requirements of this  
concern are met, each side (client and server) can be developed  
independently. Resource must be identiﬁed in request (usually in  
URI) and resource representation in response can be diﬀerent


from real resource. In our application resource is one row from  
database, but we will send JSON representation to the client.

When client holds representation of an resource he must have all  
information needed to perform or request from server all  
operations (resource modiﬁcation, resource deletion, …). Every  
client request must hold all required information to allow proper  
execution of requested service.

Server does not always have to send data, but client must use  
sent data to retrieve desired information. If we send resource that  
represents user proﬁle, one part of the proﬁle can be a proﬁle  
picture. Server can send image URL to client instead of real  
image.

If we follow these simple concerns, we can create simple, eﬃcient  
and scalable applications.


2.2 Go and REST Server Application

You can write REST Server Application in basically any

programming language. We will try to list few pros and cons of  
using Go programming language for REST Server Application.

Go has many advantages for development of server applications.  
Go has native support for server applications provided in

standard library. There are packages for web, cryptography, I/O  
handling, JSON serialization and JSON deserialization.

It is easy to deploy Go application. Just copy executable ﬁle to  
server, we do not need any support for the environment (JVM for

Java, for example). Executable ﬁle created by Go compiler it is  
usually a few MB, which is considerably less than other  
programming languages.

Because goroutines are light threads, Go application has better

execution time. As we mentioned before, Go is not object  
oriented. Without a large number of objects, memory  
consumption is less than other programming languages. One of  
the main advantages of Go concurrency is that we can easily  
create a new goroutine.

Go has no generics and this can be a problem. Without generics  
degree of code reusability is lower. This problem can be


bypassed, but requires additional coding. This can aﬀect

maintainability.

As we see in the previous chapter interfaces are implemented  
implicitly. It can be diﬃcult to tell, from simple looking, if  
something implements interface.

One of the main problems with Go is bad library support and  
small community. The Go community is growing day by day so  
this problem will probably become minor in near future. With  
bigger and stronger community many popular applications will

provide oﬃcial library for integration with Go.

Go is very fast and eﬃcient and with native support for web  
applications, it should be right choice. Some ﬂaws (generics) can  
discourage developers from using Go for REST Server  
Applications, but virtues give us powerful tool to develop  
application that is fast and eﬃcient. All Go can be easily avoided.


2.3 REST Server Application overview

It’s time to give a short overview and description of application

that will be developed in the next chapters. More speciﬁc details  
will be presented inside relevant chapters.

We will create REST Server Application that can be used in  
library. The book will be the main application entity. Each entity

will consist of unique identiﬁer name, author name, publisher and  
short description.

Also, each book will have small ﬂag to describe state. Book can  
be available or borrowed. If book is borrowed, no one else can

borrow that book. Also, we must have some indication who  
borrowed the book.

To keep things easy as possible we will assume that there is only  
one copy of same book. This will help us to avoid keeping track

of how many books we have in stock.

We will have two types of users, librarians and members, each  
with speciﬁc obligations. Each user will have unique identiﬁer,  
username, password, full name and role.

Member can see all available books, borrow the book and return  
the book to library. To keep things simple, when member call  
borrow API, no additional librarian approval will be required.


Also, there will be no deadline by which book should be returned.

Our goal is to become familiar with concepts of REST Server  
Applications so any unnecessary, any complications can lead to  
confusion.

Librarian can see list of all books (available and borrowed), also  
he can see who borrowed a book, can add new book, edit  
existing book and delete book from library. Both type of users,  
must login to system before they can use available API calls.  
More details will be presented in the chapter dedicated to

Handler.

Now, when we are familiar with our small project we can begin  
development.


2.4 Layers of Server Application

Before we start actual coding, we will present layers of Server

Application. Each layer can be observe as “box”. Each layer will  
process data and send it to next layer. In Figure we can see  
representations of layers:

Figure 2.1: Layers of REST Server Application

Client will send HTTP requests to Server Application. HTTP Layer  
will accept request and prepare data for next layer (authorization  
token, URL and request body). When everything is ready data can


be propagated to next layer. More details will be provided in

chapter dedicated to HTTP layer.

Handler will accept data from HTTP layer and check if  
authorization token is valid. In case of invalid token, handler will  
return error to HTTP layer, and client will be informed that API  
call was unsuccessful. If client provided valid token, handler will  
ﬁnd call core layer function that corresponds URL. If there is no  
corresponding function, the error will be returned.

Inside core layer, some validations will be performed. This layer

will check if all required ﬁelds are passed. If that is not a case,  
error will be propagated through upper layers to client. If  
everything required is passed, data will be passed to data layer.

In data layer, speciﬁc database query will be created and this  
layer will communicate with database. If database returns valid  
result, response will be prepared and propagated to upper layers  
to client. If database returns invalid result, error will also be  
propagated through upper layers to client.

This layered approach will help us to better understand all basic  
concepts of one simple REST Server Application. The same  
approach can be used for more complex applications. The  
principles are the same, the diﬀerences are in the details. If we  
master everything correctly, we can use this approach to develop  
all kinds of Server Applications.

Without further delay, let us get to work.


2.5 Folder structure

Unique folder structure will be used for our Server Application.

This structure presents good for all future Server Applications. We  
can see folder structure in Figure

Figure 2.2: Folder structure of Server Application

Folder library represents folder for our project. All other folders  
and ﬁles will be placed inside it. We can see two ﬁles, library.go  
and The go ﬁle is the main ﬁle of our project. Everything inside  
it is part of package main, also the main function is inside this  
ﬁle. We can start our application from that ﬁle with simple

command:

go run library.go


The toml ﬁle will hold our conﬁguration. Each line represents key-

value pair of one conﬁguration parameter. This will allow us to  
easily adopt our application to diﬀerent environments. Application  
will read conﬁguration parameters based on key, so we can set  
diﬀerent value for speciﬁc environment. Later we will discuss  
conﬁguration with more details.

Folder conﬁg will contain package with functions that will be used  
to read conﬁguration from toml ﬁle. Inside core folder is all

functions and structures that will be used in core layer, similarly  
data folder will contain all functions and structures that will be  
used in data layer.

Folder doc will contain documents that will describe how to use  
Server API calls. It’s good practice to write documentation, this

will help all potential users of our Server Application properly. We  
will see more examples and guides on how to write  
documentation in the next chapters.

Folder handler will contain handler package with all functions and  
structures that will be used in handler layer. Server folder will  
contain all parts of http layer and some other speciﬁc services  
(for example database service). We will see more details in next  
chapters.

Folder util will contain all designed to be used in all layers of  
application (like errors). Each tool will contain speciﬁc custom  
data types and functions.

Last folder values will be used for all kinds of constants. It is  
good practice to keep all constants on same place, because they


will probably be used in all layers of Server Application.

Now when we set base for our application we can write some  
code.


2.6 Initial conﬁguration of Server Application

As we mentioned before for our application is package main and

main() function. We will put our main() function in ﬁle Here is a  
for our function.

package main  
import ()

func main() {  
log.Println("Starting Library Server")  
// Main function code

log.Println("Library Server Stopped")  
}

The ﬁrst couple of notes. An import statement is not empty, here  
is just symbolic representation, IDE (Integrated development

environment) will automatically import all required packages.  
Package log will be used to display diﬀerent kinds of log  
messages to standard output.

Our main() function will start with log message Library and will  
end with log message Server Rest of code will come to the place  
of comment displayed in this shell.

First thing that needs to be executed in main() function is  
conﬁguration loading.


log.Println("Initializing conﬁguration")

err := conﬁg.InitConﬁg("library", nil)  
if err != nil {  
log.Fatalf("Failed to read conﬁguration: %v\n", err)  
}

First, we will display log messages to standard output. In function  
InitConﬁg() from conﬁg package conﬁguration ﬁle will be read and  
loaded into application. This functions returns error if somethings  
go wrong.

After function call we must check error. If error occurred, err  
object will have some value. In that case we must handle an  
error. We don’t need return after Fatalf() function from log  
package. Function will display message to standard output and  
stop application.

As we mentioned before, all conﬁguration is in library.toml ﬁle.

For now this ﬁle will be empty, we will add data as needed  
during application development.

In conﬁg folder, we will create ﬁle this will be our main ﬁle for  
conﬁg package.

package conﬁg  
import (  
"time"  
"github.com/spf13/viper"  
)


var (  
// InitConﬁg reads conﬁguration from TOML ﬁle  
InitConﬁg = initConﬁg  
)  
func initConﬁg(ﬁleName string, additionalDirs []string) (err error) {  
// Function code  
}

First, we must explain var statement. Here, we will declare all  
function exported from package. This pattern will be used in most  
of the ﬁles in our application. In declaration exported function  
will have assigned some local function.

We use this approach because it can be useful for testing  
(specially integration testing). We can create local mock function  
for test purpose and put that function in declaration instead.

In order to read conﬁguration ﬁle, we will use Viper. Viper is  
conﬁguration solution for Go application. It can read all kind of  
conﬁguration format (toml, yaml, json, …). In our application we  
will use toml. With Viper we can focus on our code and handle  
conﬁguration easily.

First, we must install Viper with this command:

go get github.com/spf13/viper

When Viper is installed we are ready to write code for This  
function receives two arguments, name of conﬁguration ﬁle and


additional directories where conﬁguration ﬁle can be found. In our  
application we will not have any additional directories, so we pass  
nil as second argument in main() function. Function returns one  
result, an error.

Let us take a look at initConﬁg() function body.

func initConﬁg(ﬁleName string, additionalDirs []string) (err error) {  
viper.SetConﬁgName(ﬁleName)  
viper.AddConﬁgPath(".")  
viper.AddConﬁgPath("$HOME")  
for _, dir := range additionalDirs {  
viper.AddConﬁgPath(dir)  
}  
//Read conﬁguration ﬁle from disk  
err = viper.ReadInConﬁg()  
if err != nil {  
return

}  
// Create conﬁguration  
viper.ConﬁgFileUsed()  
viper.WatchConﬁg()  
return  
}

First we use SetConﬁgName() function to pass name of  
conﬁguration ﬁle. If conﬁguration ﬁle doesn't have extension we  
must set proper type with SetConﬁgType() function. After that we  
must add all paths for Viper to search conﬁguration ﬁle in. In  
our example we add current directory (.) and home directory of


our project ($HOME). If we pass any additional directories we  
add then in for loop.

When search paths and ﬁle name are ready we call  
ReadInConﬁg() function. This function will ﬁnd and read  
conﬁguration ﬁle. If any error occurred we must handle it.

Viper provides ability to read conﬁguration while application is  
running. This way we don’t need to restart application each time  
when we change something in conﬁguration. If we call  
WatchConﬁg() function, Viper instance will know to load new  
conﬁguration.

To prevent any kind of misunderstands it’s good practice to call  
ConﬁgFileUsed() function before This function return conﬁguration  
ﬁle, this will assure that Viper instance uses the proper ﬁle.

After initConﬁg() we can create couple of functions that override  
Viper which returns value associated with the key from  
conﬁguration ﬁle. Here are functions for string, int and Duration  
(from time package).

func getConﬁgString(key string) string {  
return viper.GetString(key)  
}

func getConﬁgInt(key string) int {  
return viper.GetInt(key)  
}


func getConﬁgDuration(key string) time.Duration {  
return viper.GetDuration(key)  
}

We do not need to override Viper function but it can be useful.

If we create all our server application to follow same design, this  
initialization part will be same for all of them. We can pull out  
this conﬁguration part in separate module and use that module  
in all applications.

If we follow this approach we must include viper only in one  
place, inside conﬁguration module. It is good practice to include  
third-party libraries in one place.

Now, when we prepared conﬁguration we can create ﬁrst layer of  
our application, HTTP layer.


Conclusion

Now, we are familiar with basic concepts of REST architecture.

With this knowledge we can properly understand all parts of  
server side applications.

We see some pros and cons of Go and see basic for this kind  
of application. We are ready to develop fast and eﬃcient

applications.


Points to remember

REST is software architectural style used for client–server systems.

Go has native support for server side applications.

It is good practice to create layered application.

We should keep conﬁguration in dedicated ﬁle.

CHAPTER 3

HTTP Layer and Handler

In this chapter , we will see how can we develop two layers of  
our server application. HTTP Layer who accepts HTTP Requests

and Handler Layer who handles HTTP Requests.


Structure

In this chapter, we will discuss the following topics:

HTTP

Errors

HTTP Layer – Accepting HTTP Requests

Handler Layer – Handling HTTP Requests


Objectives

After studying this unit, you should be able to:

Understand the basic concepts of HTTP.

Learn how to create and use custom-made errors.

Learn how to create HTTP layer of server application.

Learn how to create Handler layer of server application.


3.1 HTTP

In this chapter, we will see how to create HTTP Layer for our

server-side application. Here is a good place to discuss about  
basics of HTTP. Hypertext Transfer Protocol is designed for

communication between client and server sides (originally, web  
browsers and web servers).

HTTP Request has speciﬁc structure:

Start which contains HTTP Method, request target (URI) and

HTTP Version (Go will handle version so we don’t need to worry  
about that).

HTTP which contains additional request information.

HTTP which contains data transmitted between client and server.

HTTP deﬁnes a set of methods that can be used to indicate

operation that will be performed on system resource. We will not  
use all methods in our application but we will present them all.

GET returns representation of system resource. It should be used  
only for reading and has no body.


HEAD ask for response identical to GET request. Response will be

returned without body.

POST is used to create new system resource.

PUT is used for update of system resource.

DELETE is used to delete speciﬁc system resource. This request  
has no body.

CONNECT is used to establish two-way communication channel  
between client and system resource. This request has no body.

OPTIONS is used to describe the communication options for the  
targeted system resource. This request has no body.

TRACE is used to perform a message loop-back test.

PATCH is used for partial modiﬁcations of system resource.

Most HTTP methods are Cacheable by design, so we do not need

to worry about that REST constraint.

There are multiple HTTP Header ﬁelds, these are some common,  
most used:

which contains authentication credentials (usually some token).


Content-Encoding is used to specify compression algorithm  
(“gzip”, “compress”, “deﬂate”).

Content-Type is used to indicate media type of the content  
charset=utf-8”, “text/html;

Content-Length is used to represent size of the resource.

We will use these HTTP Header ﬁelds in our application.

HTTP response has structure similar to request. Response starts  
with status line instead of start line. Start line contains protocol  
version (again Go will handle this for us), status code and status

text (textual description of status code).

HTTP provides dozens of status codes. We will use few in our  
applications.

200 Request was successful.

400 Bad Server could not understand and process request.

401 Client is not authenticated.

404 Not Requested system resource cannot be found.


500 Internal Server Server has encountered situation that cannot  
be handled.

Hypertext Transfer Protocol Secure is a combination of HTTP and  
Secure Socket Layer In this version of HTTP, safe channel is  
established between client and server.

Transport inside channel is encrypted, only sender and receiver  
know how to decrypt data. If we send some sensitive data inside  
request body (passwords, bank accounts,…) if we use HTTPS we  
do not need to additionally encrypt sensitive data. Protocol will  
do that for us.

SSL use asymmetric cipher with two keys to provide encrypted  
transport. Only server-side application knows about private key, on  
the other side all clients have the public key distributed. Public

key is generated based on private key.

To keep things simple as possible, data encrypted with the public  
key can only be decrypted with private key and vice versa.

Now when we are familiar with basic of HTTP, we can  
understand all problems that can occur during development of  
HTTP Layer.


3.2 Errors

Errors will be used in all layers of our application, so now it is a

good time to talk about them. Is considered to be a good  
practice to create our own errors. Everything related to errors will  
be in error.go ﬁle inside util folder.

First, we need to deﬁne some errors. We will use ﬁve diﬀerent

types of errors inside our application. Function New() from errors  
package will return an error that formats as a given text.

var (  
ErrBadRequest = errors.New("Bad request")

ErrInternal = errors.New("Internal error")  
ErrInvalidAPICall = errors.New("Invalid API call")  
ErrNotAuthenticated = errors.New("Not authenticated")

ErrResourceNotFound = errors.New("Resource not found")  
)

Each of these error will be used in proper situations:

ErrBadRequest will be returned when something is wrong with  
request itself (invalid request body). It will be returned to client  
as HTTP Status

ErrInternal will be returned if something bad is happened during  
processing of request (failed read from database). It will be


returned to client as HTTP Status

ErrInvalidAPICall will be returned when invalid API call is issued

(call of non-existing API). It will be returned to client as HTTP  
Status

ErrNotAuthenticated will be returned when user is not  
authenticated (authentication token is missing or invalid). It will  
be returned to client as HTTP Status

ErrResourceNotFound will be returned when API is called for non-  
existing system resource. It will be returned to client as HTTP  
Status

Now we can deﬁne error struct that will be sent to clients as  
JSON. This struct will have two ﬁelds, error code and cause.

type ErrorResponse struct {  
ErrorCode int  
Cause string  
}

Errors that we deﬁned before will be used to map our application  
errors to HTTP Status. But it is good practice to deﬁne our own  
error codes for internal usage in application. This will help us to  
distinguish errors because in some cases diﬀerent errors can  
return same HTTP Status and both returns HTTP Status Cause is  
a simple message that describes occurred error.


We can use any number for error code. But it’s good practice to  
codes and to leave gap between groups for the new ones. Here  
are the codes that will be used in our application:

const (  
ErrorCodeInternal = 0

ErrorCodeInvalidJSONBody = 30  
ErrorCodeInvalidCredentials = 201  
ErrorCodeEntityNotFound = 404  
ErrorCodeValidation = 500  
)

Each of these codes will be used in proper situations:

ErrorCodeInternal will be used when internal server error occurred.

ErrorCodeInvalidJSONBody will be used when request body  
contains invalid JSON.

ErrorCodeInvalidCredentials will be used when provided username  
or password is invalid.

ErrorCodeEntityNotFound will be used when required system  
resource does not exist.

ErrorCodeValidation will be used when value for a ﬁeld (provided  
in request body) is not valid.


Now we must create error struct that will be used in our  
application. This struct will contain three ﬁelds: cause and error  
type. Last ﬁeld, error type will be actual error that occurred in  
application. We will see the usage of this ﬁeld later in this  
chapter and next chapters.

type serverError struct {  
code int  
cause string  
errorType error  
}

In order for a struct to be used as an error, it must implement  
all methods of Error interface. This interface has only one  
method,

func (e serverError) Error() string {  
return e.cause  
}

Maybe you noticed that error struct starts with lowercase  
character, so it will not be exported. But how can we use it in  
our application?

We will export two functions, one will create new error, and other  
will use type assertion to collect data from error. We will need to  
export third function which will map errors to HTTP status.

var (


// MapErrorTypeToHTTPStatus maps errors to corresponding  
// HTTP status code  
MapErrorTypeToHTTPStatus = mapErrorTypeToHTTPStatus

// IsError returns underlying error type  
IsError = isError

// NewError creates new Error object  
NewError = newError  
)

Now let us take a closer look to implementations of these  
functions.

First function, MapErrorTypeToHTTPStatus() is simple. Function  
takes one argument, and use switch statement to map error to  
corresponding HTTP Status. Corresponding HTTP Status will be  
returned as function result.

func mapErrorTypeToHTTPStatus(err error) int {

switch err {  
case ErrBadRequest:  
return http.StatusBadRequest  
case ErrInternal:  
return http.StatusInternalServerError  
case ErrInvalidAPICall, ErrResourceNotFound:  
return http.StatusNotFound  
case ErrNotAuthenticated:  
return http.StatusUnauthorized  
default:  
return http.StatusInternalServerError


}  
}

The second one, IsError() is little bit trickier, but not too much.  
This function also takes error as only argument and returns four

values. The ﬁrst (boolean) result value will be true if provided  
error holds the concrete type otherwise it will be false. Other  
results values are error code, cause and error type respectively.

func isError(errorType error) (bool, int, string, error) {  
err, isError := errorType.(serverError)  
if !isError {  
return false, 0, "", errorType  
}

return true, err.code, err.cause, err.errorType  
}

As we can see, we use type assertion to check if error holds the

concrete type serverError. If that the case, function return true  
and all ﬁelds from serverError struct. If that is not a case, isError

will be false, and function will return false, zero values for integer  
and string and error provided as function argument.

The third function is quite simple. It takes four arguments, cause,  
error code, error type (one of the errors that we deﬁned at the

beginning of this chapter) and error (actual error that occurred).

The ﬁrst three arguments will be used to create new serverError  
and the fourth one will be used just for logging.


func newError(cause string, code int, errorType, err error) error {  
// Error logging

if err != nil {  
log.Printf("error: %v: %v", cause, err)

} else {

log.Printf("error: %v", cause)  
}

return serverError{code, cause, errorType}  
}

There is a reason why we use second error just for logging. For

example, if error occurs because of bad database query, we will  
not send that error to client, because we do not want to expose

our implementation and database design. We will log that  
message and use it to ﬁx a problem. Client will be informed that

some internal error occurred and he will know how to handle  
that.

Now we have everything that we need to handle all kinds of

errors. We are ready to create ﬁrst layer of our application –  
HTTP Layer.


3.3 HTTP Layer – accepting HTTP requests

HTTP Layer is a ﬁrst layer of our application. This layer will be

listening for HTTP Request and accepting them. Base for this  
layer will be HTTP server, small server (service), who is a part of  
Go native support. We will use it, and adapt it for our needs.

HTTP server will be started in main() function. We will add this

code in following library.go conﬁguration initialization:

var wg sync.WaitGroup  
wg.Add(1)

go func() {  
defer wg.Done()

log.Println("Starting HTTP Server")  
err := server.StartHTTPServer()  
if err != nil {

log.Fatalf("Could not start HTTP Server: %v\n", err)  
}

log.Println("HTTP Server gracefully terminated")  
}()  
wg.Wait()


We will use WaitGroup to create synchronization barrier. We will

wait until all goroutines are ﬁnished. In our case, we have just  
one goroutine, HTTP server, but there is a case when we can  
have more than one. Without wait group, main goroutine will  
ﬁnish and program will be terminated.

HTTP server will be started in new goroutine deﬁned inside  
anonymous function. We use defer to signal WaitGroup that  
routine is ﬁnished, as we mentioned before, it is good to use

defer for this kind of jobs.

Inside anonymous function we will display log message to  
standard output. Function StartHTTPServer() from server package  
will try to start HTTP server, we will see implementation details  
soon.

If error occurred, Fatalf() function will display message to  
standard output and stop application. When HTTP server is

terminated log message will be displayed to standard output.

We must prepare HTTP server conﬁguration. There are three  
parameters that we need to include in conﬁguration, server  
address, read timeout and write timeout.

Server address is TCP address where HTTP server listens for  
HTTP Requests. Read timeout is maximum duration for reading  
entire request, body included and write timeout is maximum  
duration before timing out response writing.


We need to add HTTP section inside library.toml ﬁle.

# HTTP server conﬁguration

[http]

server_address = ":8080"

read_timeout = "60s"  
write_timeout = "60s"

Because we will run our application on our local machines  
(computers) we just provide port for server address. HTTP server  
will be listening for requests on localhost:8080. Tag [http] is used

to section in toml ﬁle.

We must provide functions for reading HTTP conﬁguration in  
conﬁg package. Inside http.go ﬁle in conﬁg folder we will export  
three functions, one for each conﬁguration parameter.

var (  
// GetHTTPServerAddress returns server address  
// from http section in toml ﬁle

GetHTTPServerAddress = getHTTPServerAddress

// GetHTTPReadTimeout returns read timeout  
// from http section in toml ﬁle

GetHTTPReadTimeout = getHTTPReadTimeout


// GetHTTPWriteTimeout returns write timeout  
// from http section in toml ﬁle  
GetHTTPWriteTimeout = getHTTPWriteTimeout  
)

We will use functions deﬁned in conﬁg.go ﬁle to read

conﬁguration from toml ﬁle. We use section tag and key to read  
proper value from conﬁguration. Code below should be added to  
http.go ﬁle inside conﬁg folder.

func getHTTPServerAddress() string {  
return getConﬁgString("http.server_address")  
}

func getHTTPReadTimeout() time.Duration {  
return getConﬁgDuration("http.read_timeout")  
}

func getHTTPWriteTimeout() time.Duration {  
return getConﬁgDuration("http.write_timeout")  
}

Now when our conﬁguration is ready we can create http.go ﬁle  
inside server folder. StartHTTPServer() function will be only  
function in this ﬁle.

var (  
// StartHTTPServer starts listening to HTTP requests  
StartHTTPServer = startHTTPServer


)  
func startHTTPServer() (err error) {

// Function code  
return  
}

We will now look in detail body of startHTTPServer() function. We  
mentioned, couple of times, that Go provides native support for  
server applications. ServeMux is a part of that support, it  
represents HTTP Request multiplexer. It matches the incoming  
request against list of registered patterns and calls proper handler.

mux := http.NewServeMux()  
mux.Handle("/api/", newHandlerAPI())

This part of code will create new ServeMux and register the  
Handler for the given pattern. All our API calls will start with so  
we will use that to deﬁne pattern. Handler is an interface with  
only one method this method responds to HTTP Request.  
Function newHandlerAPI() will return new object of our Handler  
implementation.

When ServeMux is created we must create new Server object from  
http package and read conﬁguration from toml ﬁle.

server := http.Server{}

server.ReadTimeout = conﬁg.GetHTTPReadTimeout()  
server.WriteTimeout = conﬁg.GetHTTPWriteTimeout()


server.Addr = conﬁg.GetHTTPServerAddress()  
server.Handler = mux

Inside new goroutine, we will handle shutdown of HTTP Server.  
We will use channels to listen for operating system interrupt  
signal + C key combination). When the signal is received server  
will be shutdown. Shutdown error can occur so we must handle  
that situation.

go func() {  
// Listen to operating system's interrupt signal  
interrupt := make(chan os.Signal, 1)  
signal.Notify(interrupt, os.Interrupt, syscall.SIGTERM)  
<-interrupt

// Gracefully shut down the server when it happens  
if err := server.Shutdown(context.Background()); err != nil {  
log.Printf("Error shutting down. %v\n", err)

}  
}()

At the end, we call ListenAndServe() function. This function will

listen on TCP address provided in conﬁguration and call proper  
Handler when request is received. This function will always return

non null error, after Shutdown() it will return This is not an  
actual error so we must handle this situation.

err = server.ListenAndServe()

if err == http.ErrServerClosed {  
err = nil

}


Now it is time to see our implementation of Handler. This is an  
essential part of HTTP Layer. Code for this will be in serveAPI.go

ﬁle inside server folder.

Our implementation of Handler interface will be use struct called

It will contain two pools from sync package. Pool is set of

temporary objects that can be saved and retrieved. We will add  
the following code at the beginning of serveApi.go ﬁle from server

folder.

type handlerAPI struct {

requestPool *sync.Pool // holds: *handler.Request

buﬀerPool *sync.Pool // holds: *bytes.Buﬀer  
}

The ﬁrst Pool (requestPool) will be used for Request (deﬁned in

Handler Layer), the second one (buﬀerPool) will be used for all  
kinds of buﬀer. Buﬀers will be used for request body reading and

response writing.

Request deﬁned in Handler Layer ﬁle inside handler folder) is  
struct that contains all that we need in Handler Layer:

Authorization Header form HTTP Request.

HTTP Request Body.


HTTP Request URL.

HTTP Request Method (POST, PUT, GET, …).

HTTP Layer will prepare this information and send it to Handler

Layer. Here is a declaration of a struct.

type Request struct {  
Authorization string

Body io.Reader  
URL *url.URL

Method string  
}

We also need function to create new We already used this

function to register Handler for the giving pattern at the  
beginning of StartHTTPServer() function. It is simple function, we

just create new Pool objects. New speciﬁes function that will be  
called to generate a value when Get() is called. If function is not

provided nil will be returned. All code examples to the end of  
this section should be added to serveApi.go ﬁle from server

folder.

func newHandlerAPI() (api *handlerAPI) {  
api = &handlerAPI{}

api.requestPool = &sync.Pool{

New: func() interface{} {  
return new(handler.Request)


},  
}

api.buﬀerPool = &sync.Pool{

New: func() interface{} {  
return new(bytes.Buﬀer)

},  
}

return

}

We must implement ServeHTTP() function in order to our struct

handlerAPI implements Handler interface. This method receives

two arguments response writer and HTTP Request.

It is often useful to measure request duration, this value will be

used when request is logged. At the start of the function, we will  
just save current time in startTime variable.

When we capture time we must create new Context. Context is a

type from package context that can be used to carry deadline,  
signals and values across API boundaries and processes. We have

one simple rule to follow when we work with context. We should  
not store Context in struct, we should pass it to each function

that needs it.

A Background() function will return not nil empty Context. This  
Context is never canceled, has no values and has no deadlines. It

is a top-level Context for incoming requests.


func (handlerAPI *handlerAPI) ServeHTTP(writer  
http.ResponseWriter,

httpReqest *http.Request) {

// startTime is used to measure request duration  
startTime := time.Now()

// Prepare request context

ctx := context.Background()

authorization := httpReqest.Header.Get("Authorization")

requestBody, logRequestBody :=  
handlerAPI.getRequestBody(httpReqest.Body)

request := handlerAPI.requestPool.Get().(*handler.Request)

request.Authorization = authorization  
request.Body = requestBody

request.URL = httpReqest.URL  
request.Method = httpReqest.Method

// response for the request generated by core layer function

var response interface{}

// error that occurred during processing of the request  
var err error

defer func() {


// function code

}()  
response, err = handler.Handle(ctx, request)

}

After creation of Context, we prepare the request object for

Handler Layer. We read Authorization from HTTP Request Header,  
and read requestBody in getRequesBody() method. This method

accepts io.Reader and returns two results (both represent HTTP  
Request Body), io.Reader that will be used in core layer and

string that will be used for logging.

This function uses When we Get() new buﬀer from pool we use  
Reset() to reset the buﬀer to be empty. We copy request body into

the buﬀer and build string from buﬀer for logging. Buﬀer should  
be returned to Pool when we no longer need it. At the end of the

method, we return new Reader with string representation and  
string representation of request body.

func (handlerAPI *handlerAPI) getRequestBody(reader io.Reader)  
(io.Reader, string) {

buﬀer := handlerAPI.buﬀerPool.Get().(*bytes.Buﬀer)

buﬀer.Reset()

io.Copy(buﬀer, reader)  
body := buﬀer.String()

handlerAPI.buﬀerPool.Put(buﬀer)


return strings.NewReader(body), body  
}

We declared two variables response and err. Response will be

returned from Core Layer through Handler Layer, err represents

error which occurred during processing of the request. At the end  
of the ServeHTTP method we call Handle() function. This function

call will move HTTP Request processing to next layer, Handler

Layer.

Response will be prepared and handled inside defer anonymous

function. First, we will deﬁne two variables. The ﬁrst integer

variable httpResponseStatus will be used to represent HTTP

Response status that will be returned to client. The second string  
variable logResponseBody will represent string interpretation of

HTTP Response Body and it will be used for logging.

var httpResponseStatus int

var logResponseBody string

We will have small defer anonymous function that will be used

for logging of request and response. Here, we will use startTime

captured at the start of the HTTPServe() method to calculate

request duration. Also, we will return pooled value to

defer func() {

// Return pooled values back to the appropriate sync.Pool

handlerAPI.requestPool.Put(request)


//Log the request

duration := time.Now().Sub(startTime)

log.Printf("%v: status=%v method=%v uri=%v duration=%v",  
startTime, httpResponseStatus, httpReqest.Method,

httpReqest.RequestURI, duration)

log.Printf("request=%v", logRequestBody)

if response != nil {

log.Printf("response=%v", logResponseBody)

}

}()

Error handling is very important part of preparing a response for

the client. If there is no error, we will just assign HTTP Status

OK (200) to httpResponseStatus variable.

If error occurred we must handle two situations. If occurred error

is instance of error that we deﬁned in error.go we will assign  
error code and cause to ErrorResponse struct and return that as

response body to client. Function MapErrorTypeToHTTPStatus() will

be used to assign proper HTTP Response Status to

httpResponseStatus variable.

If occurred error is not instance of error that we deﬁned in we

will return HTTP Response with empty body and use  
MapErrorTypeToHTTPStatus() function to assign proper HTTP

Response Status to httpResponseStatus variable.


if err == nil {  
httpResponseStatus = http.StatusOK

} else {

isError, errorCode, cause, errorType := util.IsError(err)

if isError == true {  
response = util.ErrorResponse{

ErrorCode: errorCode,

Cause: cause,

}

httpResponseStatus = util.MapErrorTypeToHTTPStatus(errorType)

} else {

response = nil  
httpResponseStatus = util.MapErrorTypeToHTTPStatus(err)

}

}

Now we must prepare response that will be returned to client.

First, we get buﬀer from buﬀer pool and reset it.

responseBuﬀer := handlerAPI.buﬀerPool.Get().(*bytes.Buﬀer)

responseBuﬀer.Reset()

If response is not nil (some core functions can return nil  
response, for example for delete, we just need conﬁrmation that

operation is successful we can achieve that with HTTP Response

Status). If response is not an error, we should use gzip to

compress content and ﬁll HTTP Response Header. We will use  
makeResponseBody() method to write response body and prepare

string response representation for logging.


if response != nil {

shouldGZip := err == nil

logResponseBody = handlerAPI.makeResponseBody(shouldGZip,

responseBuﬀer, response)

if shouldGZip == true {

writer.Header().Set("Content-Encoding", "gzip")

}  
writer.Header().Set("Content-Type",

"application/json; charset=utf-8")

writer.Header().Set("Content-Length",  
strconv.Itoa(responseBuﬀer.Len()))

}

Let us take a closer look to makeResponseBody() method. If

response is equal to nil we just return empty string and return

from method. If response is not equal to nil, we need to write

response. Again we have two situations, in ﬁrst we will compress  
content with gzip, in second we just write response without

compression.

func (handlerAPI *handlerAPI) makeResponseBody(shouldGZip  
bool,

writer io.Writer, response interface{}) string {

if response == nil {  
return ""

}


respRawBody := handlerAPI.buﬀerPool.Get().(*bytes.Buﬀer)

respRawBody.Reset()

if shouldGZip == true {

gzipper := gzip.NewWriter(writer)

writeResponse(io.MultiWriter(respRawBody, gzipper), response)

gzipper.Close()  
} else {

writeResponse(io.MultiWriter(respRawBody, writer), response)

}

rawBody := trimEOL(respRawBody.String())

handlerAPI.buﬀerPool.Put(respRawBody)

return rawBody

}

Before we return string representation of response body and

return buﬀer to poll we must do one more thing. Encode()

method from json package will add \n (new line) character at the

end of each line. We should remove it.

func trimEOL(json string) string {

n := len(json)

if n > 0 && json[n-1] == '\n' {  
return json[:n-1]

}


return json

}

When we want to write response inside writeResponse() function

we will use type assertion in switch statement to decide what

todo. If provided response body is array of bytes we just use  
writer to write the response. In any other case, we must use

Encode() for JSON encoding. Encoder returned with NewEncoder()

will write encoded content.

func writeResponse(writer io.Writer, response interface{}) {

switch resp := response.(type) {

case []byte:  
writer.Write(resp)

default:

json.NewEncoder(writer).Encode(response)

}  
}

At the end of the function, we just use writer to write HTTP

Response Header and Body. When writing is ﬁnished we just  
return buﬀer to appropriate pool.

writer.WriteHeader(httpResponseStatus)  
writer.Write(responseBuﬀer.Bytes())

handlerAPI.buﬀerPool.Put(responseBuﬀer)


Now our HTTP Layer is ready and we can move to next layer,

Handler Layer.


3.5 Handler Layer – handling HTTP Requests

The Handler Layer can be observed as a tree, with call of Core

Layer function in each leaf. URI is an indicator of which function  
to call. So ﬁrst we need to deﬁne how URIs for our API call will  
look.

As we mentioned before there are two approaches. We can use

diﬀerent HTTP methods for diﬀerent operations and have same  
URI for all APIs for same resource or we can use only one  
method (POST) and have unique URI to choose operation.

Because ﬁrst approach is more in the of the REST, we will use  
ﬁrst approach.

So basically, to create Handler Layer we must know URI and  
HTTP method for each API. First, we must describe application

User must use username and password to login to our system.  
Login API will return user’s authorization token. Client will put  
authorization token in HTTP Request Header and call other APIs.

We do not need any token for login, so everyone can call Login  
API. We refer that kind of API as Username and password will be  
sent inside HTTP Request Body so we can use POST method. If  
we use something else, for example GET, username and password

will be a part of URI, so it will be to exposed.

Our open API can look like this:


POST: /api/open/login

When user is logged he can perform diﬀerent kind of operations.  
Member can get all available books and get a single book. We  
usually use GET for these operations. Additionally, member can

borrow or return a book. These operations are essentially the  
same, they only change book status, so we can have one API for  
both operations. These operations will change one part (ﬁeld) of  
the book resource, so we can use PATCH.

These operations will be available only for users with a role  
member and will be executed over book resource, so our Member  
API should look like this:

GET: /api/member/book/{book-id}  
GET: /api/member/book/all/?searchTerm={searchTerm}  
&oﬀset={oﬀset}&limit={limit}  
PATCH: /api/member/book

Method GET has no body so we need to send parameters in  
Here, we can see two approaches. In ﬁrst, when we have only

one parameter (bookID) we can just put that parameter at the  
end of In Get All Boos API, we can send more than one  
parameter. We will discuss about these parameters in Core Layer.

Librarian has much larger set of operations that he can perform.  
He can get all books and single book (same as member we will  
use GET here), he can create, update and delete book. There is a


certain for resource creation we use for resource update PUT and  
for resource deletion DELETE method. We will follow that  
standard and our Librarian API should look like this:

POST: /api/librarian/book  
GET: /api/librarian/book/{book-id}

GET: /api/librarian/book/all/?searchTerm={searchTerm}  
&oﬀset={oﬀset}&limit={limit}  
PUT: /api/librarian/book  
DELETE: /api/librarian/book/{book-id}

Now we have everything that we need, so we can develop  
Handler Layer. In a nutshell, Handler Layer consists of several  
switch statements. We have one function Handler() that is  
exported and used in HTTP Layer. All code examples from this  
section are related to Handler layer and should be placed in  
handler.go ﬁle from handler folder.

var (  
// handle all http requests  
Handle = handle  
)

func handle(ctx context.Context, request *Request) (interface{},  
error) {  
// Function code  
}


At the beginning of the function, we test if our URI starts with if  
that is not a case we will return Invalid API Call Error. Next  
expression will remove from string representation of URI, so we  
can use HasPreﬁx() function inside switch statements.

Function PrepareDbRunner() will create new Database Runner and  
put it in Context. We will discuss this when we talk about  
Database Layer. For now, we should know that we have Database  
Runner in Context and we can use it in layers of application.

if !strings.HasPreﬁx(request.URL.Path, "/api") {  
return nil, util.ErrInvalidAPICall  
}

uri := request.URL.Path[4:]  
ctx = dbserver.PrepareDbRunner(ctx)

We have a switch statement with three case statements. The ﬁrst  
case will handle Open API calls, the second Member API calls  
and the third one will handle Librarian API calls. If there is no  
matching case statement, default statement will return Invalid API  
Call Error.

Only users with a role member can call Member API and vice  
versa, only users with a role librarian can call Librarian API. We  
will call AuthorizeUser() function from Core Layer, with token  
received from Login API. If the user doesn't have appropriate role  
Not Authenticated Error will be returned.


switch {  
case strings.HasPreﬁx(uri, "/open"):  
return handleOpen(ctx, uri[5:], request)

case strings.HasPreﬁx(uri, "/member"):  
userRole, err := core.AuthorizeUser(ctx, request.Authorization)  
if err != nil {  
return nil, util.ErrNotAuthenticated  
}

if userRole != values.UserRoleMember {  
return nil, util.ErrNotAuthenticated  
}

return handleMember(ctx, uri[7:], request)  
case strings.HasPreﬁx(uri, "/librarian"):  
userRole, err := core.AuthorizeUser(ctx, request.Authorization)  
if err != nil {  
return nil, util.ErrNotAuthenticated  
}

if userRole != values.UserRoleLibrarian {  
return nil, util.ErrNotAuthenticated

}  
return handleLibrarian(ctx, uri[10:], request)  
default:  
return nil, util.ErrInvalidAPICall  
}

We have just one open API, login. If URI is equal to (we  
removed part in Handle() function) and HTTP Method is POST


we will call Login() function from Core Layer. In other case, we  
will return Invalid API Call Error.

func handleOpen(ctx context.Context, uri string, request *Request)  
(response interface{}, err error) {

if uri == "/login"&& request.Method == http.MethodPost {  
return core.Login(ctx, request.Body)  
}  
return nil, util.ErrInvalidAPICall  
}

Often we will have more than one resource in application, but  
here we will have only one, so we do not need additional switch  
for book and some potential other resources. At the start of

handleMember() function we will check if URI starts with If that  
is not a case Invalid API Call Error will be removed.

We will remove part from start of the URI. Now only parameters  
are contained in URI string variable.

func handleMember(ctx context.Context, uri string, request

*Request) (response interface{}, err error) {  
if !strings.HasPreﬁx(uri, "/book") {

return nil, util.ErrInvalidAPICall  
}

uri = uri[5:]


// Switch statement

}

We will use HTTP Method for conditional statement in switch. For  
PATCH Method BorrowOrReturnBook() Core Layer function will be

called.

For GET Method we have two options, get all books or get single  
book. Because we will always have something at the end of URI

for these API calls (“/all” and parameters for get all and bookID  
for get single book) if the current value of URI string is empty

we will return an Invalid API Call Error.

If URI starts with we will read parameters from URI and call

GetAllBooks() Core Layer Function. Because the response is not

same for members and librarians we will also send user role to  
Core Layer.

If URI not starts with we will call GetBook() function from Core  
Layer. Because the current value of URI variable is we must

remove slash from beginning.

switch request.Method {  
case http.MethodGet:

if uri == "" {  
return nil, util.ErrInvalidAPICall

}

if strings.HasPreﬁx(uri, "/all") {  
searchTerm, rowOﬀset, rowLimit, err := getParams(request.URL)


if err != nil {

return nil, util.ErrInvalidAPICall

}

return core.GetAllBooks(ctx, searchTerm, rowOﬀset, rowLimit,   
values.UserRoleMember)

}

return core.GetBook(ctx, uri[1:])  
case http.MethodPatch:

return nil, core.BorrowOrReturnBook(ctx, request.Authorization,   
request.Body)

default:  
return nil, util.ErrInvalidAPICall

}

In getParams() function we will use Query() method to read  
parameters from URI. This function will return map of the strings

slices. We use parameter names, go get proper values from map.  
Actual value will be in the ﬁrst element of slice (element with

index 0). All parameters are optional, so if we omit some  
parameters default (zero) values will be assigned. Sometime we

must convert from string to required type.

func getParams(uri *url.URL) (searchTerm string, rowOﬀset,  
rowLimit int, err error) {

params := uri.Query()


param, ok := params["searchTerm"]  
if ok {

searchTerm = param[0]  
}

param, ok = params["oﬀset"]

if ok {  
rowOﬀset, err = strconv.Atoi(param[0])

if err != nil {  
return

}

}

param, ok = params["limit"]  
if ok {

rowLimit, err = strconv.Atoi(param[0])  
if err != nil {

return  
}

}

return

}

Inside handleLibrarian() we will handle Librarian API. This function  
is similar with handleMamber() function, we will only have one

case statement for create, update and delete book. For delete  
book, we need to check if bookID is included at the end of the

URI, and we must remove slash.


func handleLibrarian(ctx context.Context, uri string, request

*Request) (response interface{}, err error) {  
if !strings.HasPreﬁx(uri, "/book") {

return nil, util.ErrInvalidAPICall  
}

uri = uri[5:]

switch request.Method {

case http.MethodPost:  
return core.CreateBook(ctx, request.Body)

case http.MethodGet:  
if uri == "" {

return nil, util.ErrInvalidAPICall  
}

if strings.HasPreﬁx(uri, "/all") {  
searchTerm, rowOﬀset, rowLimit, err :=

getParams(request.URL)  
if err != nil {

return nil, util.ErrInvalidAPICall  
}

return core.GetAllBooks(ctx, searchTerm, rowOﬀset, rowLimit,  
values.UserRoleLibrarian)

}

return core.GetBook(ctx, uri[1:])


case http.MethodPut:

return core.UpdateBook(ctx, request.Body)  
case http.MethodDelete:

if uri == "" {  
return nil, util.ErrInvalidAPICall

}

return nil, core.DeleteBook(ctx, uri[1:])  
default:

return nil, util.ErrInvalidAPICall  
}

}

Handler Layer is ready. Now we can deal with business logic in  
Core Layer.


Conclusion

We are familiar with basic concepts of HTTP and we created the

ﬁrst and second layers of our server application. HTTP Layer will  
accept HTTP Request and pass them to the second layer, Handler  
Layer, who will handle them and pass to Core Layer.


Points to remember

HTTP is transfer protocol used for client-server communication.

Custom-made error must implement Error interface.

Context is a Go data type that can be used to carry deadline,

signals and values across API boundaries and processes.

Handler is an interface that responds to an HTTP request, it’s a  
part of Go native support for server-side applications.

Handler Layer detect which function of Core Layer will be called.

Key terms

Cryptography protocol used for network traﬃc.
