CHAPTER 5

Data Layer and Database

In this chapter, we will see how to design, initialize and conﬁgure  
database. We will get familiar with common design and  
development practices in order to produce the most eﬃcient and

optimal solution. Data Layer, that will extract data from database  
and prepare it for HTTP Response, will also be presented in this  
chapter.


Structure

In this chapter, we will discuss the following topics:

Database Layer

Database Design

Creating a Database

Database Creation SQL Script

Initializing Database SQL Script

Database Conﬁguration and Initialization

DB Runner

Row Reader

Data Layer

Common Functions

User Functions


Book Functions

Where Next?

Documentation

API Testing

Break a Monolith into Microservices


Objectives

After studying this unit, you should be able to:

Design and initialize database.

Create Data layer of server application.

Write API documentation.

Prepare for implementation of some advanced architectural  
designs.


5.1 Database Layer

We will reverse an order of layers. Data Layer will execute queries

on database, so we must design and initialize database ﬁrst. This  
is a reason why we will talk about Database Layer before Data  
Layer. In some, cases, database design can be the ﬁrst step of

application development. Because our application is simple  
enough we had a luxury to postpone it as long as possible.


5.1.1 Database design

During the database design, several problems must be handled.

First of them is choice of database management system. For our  
application, PostgreSQL is a good solution. It will provide good  
performance, Go provide a good support and more importantly

PostgreSQL is open-source solution with large community and has  
no licensing issue (like MySQL).

Before we start to design our database, we will give an overview  
of the most important database concepts.

Collection of related data organized in table format. Each table is

consisting of columns and rows. Column represents one with  
information while row represents one set of related data. For  
example, table that represents RGB color model will have the

following columns: color name, red, green and blue. Set of values  
(“Red”, 255, 0, 0) can be one row.

Primary Field or a set of ﬁelds that uniquely identiﬁes each row  
in table. In table that represents bank customers, bank account  
can be used for primary key.

Foreign Field or set of ﬁelds that refer primary key from another  
table.


Database Structure that can be used to improve speed of read

operations. In database that represents all participants of  
international sport competition (Olympic games), we can search  
athletes by event. In order to do that as fast as possible, we  
should create index on event column. We must be very careful  
with indexes. Each index requires additional storage space.

Database Special procedure which will be run when speciﬁc action  
occurs in database. If we have students database, we can create  
trigger to update GPA when student pass an exam.

Now when we are familiar with basic concepts of database we  
can start to design. Our application is quite simple so we will  
have no problem to create optimal solution and designs for our  
needs.

Let’s start with table that represents book. In the previous  
chapter, we become familiar with our structures so we know that

we must have text columns for bookID, book name, author name,  
publisher and description. Because the description is only optional  
other columns cannot be NULL. BookID is unique for each book  
(row) and that qualiﬁes it as a good candidate for primary key.

We mentioned in the previous chapter that is a good practice to  
follow when system resource is created and updated. We can  
have two timestamps columns, created_at and updated_at for  
these information.


When someone borrows or returns a book this will aﬀect book  
status. One integer column is all that we need to handle this  
problem. We need one more column borrower_id to store  
borrower This column is nullable, because when book is available  
there is no point of storing any value for this column. When  
some value is stored, userID must be valid (must exist in the  
system). To ensure that this will be the case we will create the  
foreign key. Book table is now ready, on ﬁgure 5.1 we can see  
table representation, without foreign keys:

Figure 5.1: Book table

Here, we will learn one good trick. Our book can have one of  
two statuses available (integer value 1) and borrowed (integer  
value 2). We can validate status in our application, but someone  
can execute query directly on database and insert invalid value.  
We can prevent data with small database table with two columns,  
code and


First column, code will represent integer value of book status (1  
or 2). The second column is textual representation of book status  
or When we create foreign key on book_status column from the  
book table we can store only statuses that exist in referenced  
table. All tables that have this or similar purpose should have

enum in name in our case). In ﬁgure 5.2 design for  
enum_book_status table is presented:

Figure 5.2: enum_book_status table

We did not create User API, but still we will have some users in  
our system. All our users will be inserted in database during  
initialization and we need to design database table. Because user  
is a keyword in SQL, we will use library_user for table name.  
This table will have the following columns: username, password,  
full name, user role and authorization token. All columns are  
textual except role and userUID will be primary key.

We will use same trick with enum table for user roles. Column  
code will represent integer value of user roles (1 or 2), column  
user_role will be textual representation of those roles and Now we  
will just create foreign key on user_role column form library_user  
table, so only roles deﬁned in enum_user_role table can be


stored. In ﬁgure we can see design for library_user and  
enum_user_role tables:

Figure 5.3: library_user and enum_user_role tables

In ﬁgure we can see design of our database. Primary keys are  
bolded while the foreign keys are represented with inter-table lines.  
One small note, for type of ID columns, uuid is speciﬁed instead  
of text. This is a special textual data type used to store unique  
identiﬁers:


Figure 5.4: Database design


5.1.2 Creating a database

Our application will connect to database and manipulate data

from it. The ﬁrst, we must create database. For our needs, we  
can have it on our local machine (computer), but we must  
download postgreSQL from oﬃcial site

After installation, we can execute all scripts and commands in

terminal, but it’s easier to use speciﬁed tool. One of the most  
popular open-source postgreSQL tools is pgAdmin and we can  
download it from the oﬃcial site (www.pgadmin.org).

During installation we will be asked to set admin user name and

password, they will be used for database connection. When we  
ﬁnish all installations we can start Tool will be opened inside web  
browser.

First, we should create new server. In order to do that we should

go to Object | Create | Server New window will be opened and  
we can ﬁll required information. On General tab, we can use  
localhost for server name we can leave other ﬁelds intact:


Figure 5.5: Creating new server

On Connection tab we need to ﬁll couple of ﬁelds. Our database  
will run on our local machine so we can use localhost for Host  
name/address. Port 5432 is standardly used for databases and we  
can use it here. We should use username and password that we

set during installation and our database server is ready, we just  
need to click Save


Figure 5.6: Connection tab

Now we will create new database inside our server. We can go to  
Object | Create | Database and ﬁll database name on the General  
tab. We will use library_db for database name and click Save


Figure 5.7: General tab

When database is created we can go to Tools | Query Tool to  
open an editor where we can execute queues.


Figure 5.8: General tab

Now we can simply copy our scripts and execute them. We will  
execute scripts that will be created in the following sections.  
These scripts should be executed one by one, database creation  
script and then initialization script.

Editor is also very useful when we want to test queries and  
scripts that we will use inside Data Layer.


5.1.3 Database creation SQL Script

When we ﬁnalize database design, converting that design to SQL

scripts is next step. All scripts will be in dbscripts folder, sub-  
folder inside data folder. First script public_schema.sql will create  
all tables, indexes and triggers.

Database schema is a skeleton structure that deﬁnes how data is

organized. We can have more than one schema. If we have  
application that is used in couple diﬀerent countries, and one of  
them wants to have a separate set of data from other countries,

we can create schema for that country and allow only users from  
that country to use data from that schema. Public schema is for  
databases.

At the start of script we should set some options for timeouts,

encoding and some validations. For all timeout conﬁguration  
parameters 0 means that timeout is disabled. Other parameters  
turn oﬀ some validation that is not necessary for our system and  
set message level that will be sent to client.

SET statement_timeout = 0;  
SET lock_timeout = 0;  
SET idle_in_transaction_session_timeout = 0;

SET client_encoding = 'UTF8';  
SET standard_conforming_strings = on;  
SET check_function_bodies = false;


SET client_min_messages = warning;

SET row_security = oﬀ;

Extensions are PostgreSQL concept and are similar with packages

in Go. If we want to use functionality from speciﬁc extension, we  
must install that extension. Installing extension that is already  
installed can cause error, so we can use IF NOT EXIST to handle  
that situation.

We will use three extensions in our database. The ﬁrst extension  
plpgsql will allow us to use all functionalities of PostgreSQL. The

second extension uuid-ossp will be used to generate unique  
identiﬁers. We must store password in our database, it’s not safe  
to store them in plain-text form. The third extension pgcrypto will  
be used for password encryption.

CREATE EXTENSION IF NOT EXISTS plpgsql WITH SCHEMA  
pg_catalog;

CREATE EXTENSION IF NOT EXISTS "uuid-ossp" WITH SCHEMA  
pg_catalog;  
CREATE EXTENSION IF NOT EXISTS pgcrypto WITH SCHEMA  
pg_catalog;

Before we can create concrete tables we must select desired  
schema (public schema in our case). Without that command, we  
would have to write schema name before table name (separated  
with dot). Tablespaces allow database administrator to deﬁne ﬁle

(from ﬁle system) where representations of database objects will  
be stored. Empty string speciﬁes that default table space will be  
used.


SET search_path = public, pg_catalog;  
SET default_tablespace = '';

We should deﬁne database function that will set value of  
updated_at column to current data and time. This function will  
be triggered when base modify operation (insert or update)  
occurs.

\-- update updated at column

CREATE OR REPLACE FUNCTION update_updated_at_column()  
RETURNS TRIGGER  
LANGUAGE plpgsql

AS $$  
BEGIN  
NEW.updated_at = now();  
RETURN NEW;

END;  
$$;

First, we will create enum tables. For each column, we will deﬁne  
column name, column type and some speciﬁcs (is column  
nullable, unique and default value). Constraints are used to

specify rules for data in tables. We will use constraints to deﬁne  
primary and foreign keys.

\-- enum_user_role  
CREATE TABLE enum_user_role (

code integer NOT NULL,


user_role text NOT NULL,  
CONSTRAINT enum_user_status_pk PRIMARY KEY (code)  
);  
\-- enum_book_status  
CREATE TABLE enum_book_status (  
code integer NOT NULL,  
book_status text NOT NULL,  
CONSTRAINT enum_book_status_pk PRIMARY KEY (code)  
);

To deﬁne the primary key, we specify key name and column or  
set of columns that uniquely identiﬁes each row in table. We will  
use simple name convention for primary keys, we will just add  
_pk suﬃx to table name.

To generate unique identiﬁer for userID and authorization token  
we will use uuid_generate_v1mc() function from uuid-ossp  
extension. This function will use randomly generated MAC address  
and timestamp to generate unique values. We can also use  
uuid_generate_v1() function which will use real MAC address of  
computer. There is a way to use generated values and reveal of  
computer where values are generated, so uuid_generate_v1mc()  
function is safer solution.

\-- library_user  
CREATE TABLE library_user (  
user_id uuid NOT NULL DEFAULT uuid_generate_v1mc(),  
username text NOT NULL UNIQUE,  
user_password text NOT NULL,  
full_name text NOT NULL,


user_role integer DEFAULT 1,  
token uuid NOT NULL DEFAULT uuid_generate_v1mc(),  
CONSTRAINT library_user_pk PRIMARY KEY (user_id),

CONSTRAINT fk_library_user_user_role FOREIGN KEY (user_role)  
REFERENCES enum_user_role (code) MATCH SIMPLE  
ON UPDATE NO ACTION  
ON DELETE NO ACTION  
);

Member will be the default role for users. When we deﬁne foreign  
key we specify foreign key name, the column that serves as  
reference and a column that references. We should specify what  
to do if someone tries to update or delete value from referenced  
column that has matching row. Referential action NO ACTION  
will reject update and delete actions on referenced column. We  
will all have name convention for foreign keys, using fk_ as preﬁx  
to table name and name of the reference column as suﬃx.

With all practices that we used in previous tables it’s easy to  
create book table.

\-- book  
CREATE TABLE book (  
book_id uuid NOT NULL DEFAULT uuid_generate_v1mc(),  
book_name text NOT NULL,  
author_name text NOT NULL,  
publisher text NOT NULL,  
book_description text,  
book_status integer DEFAULT 1,  
created_at timestamp with time zone NOT NULL DEFAULT now(),


updated_at timestamp with time zone NOT NULL DEFAULT  
now(),  
borrower_id uuid,  
CONSTRAINT book_pk PRIMARY KEY (book_id),  
CONSTRAINT fk_book_book_status FOREIGN KEY (book_status)  
REFERENCES enum_book_status (code) MATCH SIMPLE  
ON UPDATE NO ACTION  
ON DELETE NO ACTION,  
CONSTRAINT fk_book_borrower_id FOREIGN KEY (borrower_id)  
REFERENCES library_user (user_id) MATCH SIMPLE  
ON UPDATE NO ACTION  
ON DELETE NO ACTION  
);

We will have operations that will use book_status to read books  
from database. To improve the speed of this operation we should  
create index on book_status column. Name convention for indexes  
is simple, table name and name of columns that will be used to  
create the index.

CREATE INDEX book_book_status

ON book (book_status);

We create function that will set the current time for value of  
updated_at column, but we must create trigger which will be

executed when update operation occurs. This trigger will run  
before update operation to set proper value for updated_at

column.

CREATE TRIGGER update_book_updated_at_column


BEFORE UPDATE

ON book  
FOR EACH ROW

EXECUTE PROCEDURE update_updated_at_column();

Script which will be used to create our database is completed.  
Now we need to put some data into our database.


5.1.4 Initializing database SQL Script

We should ﬁll our database with some initial data. We can create

new script init_public_schema.sql in the dbscript folder for this  
purpose. Our enum tables will be initialized with data that we  
deﬁned in previous chapters and during database design.

\-- enum_user_role

INSERT INTO enum_user_role  
VALUES  
(1, 'member'),

(2, 'librarian');

\-- enum_book_status  
INSERT INTO enum_book_status  
VALUES

(1, 'available'),  
(2, 'borrowed');

We don’t have a User API (for now), so we cannot create new  
users through API calls. In order to test and use our application,  
we need at least two users, one member and one librarian.

\-- library_user  
INSERT INTO library_user(username, user_password, full_name,  
user_role)

VALUES


('joe', crypt('joe', gen_salt('bf')), 'Average Joe', 1),

('smith', crypt('smith', gen_salt('bf')), 'John Smith', 2);

If we want (need), we can insert more users and the initial set of  
books for our application. But for now, this will be enough.


5.1.5 Database conﬁguration and initialization

Our database is ready, now we need to connect to it from

application. We will do that in main() function. We will add this  
part of code between conﬁguration initialization and HTTP Server.

log.Println("Initializing database")  
err = dbserver.InitializeDb()

if err != nil {  
log.Fatalf("Could not access database: %v\n", err)  
}

If we want to use postgreSQL in our application we must have

this in import statement of our main ﬁle

_ "github.com/lib/pq"

We will create new folder dbserver inside server folder. Here we  
will keep everything that we need to communicate with database

and perform read and write operations. We will use dbserver  
package in almost all layers of our application, so we cannot put  
dbserver functions in server package it will cause circular import  
and we must create separate package.

Before we see implementation of InitializeDb() function we need to  
prepare database conﬁguration. There are four parameters that we  
need to include in conﬁguration, connection string, maximum


number of idle connections, maximum number of open

connections and maximum lifetime of connection.

Inside the connection string, we should provide all relevant  
parameters in speciﬁc order (host, port, user, password and  
database name). We set these parameters when we create  
database in pgAdmin so we just need to format them in required  
way.

We need to add section inside library.toml ﬁle.

# Database conﬁguration

[database]

connection_string = "host=localhost port=5432 user=postgres   
password=postgres dbname=library_db sslmode=disable"  
max_idle_connections = 5  
max_open_connections = 20  
connection_max_lifetime = "60s"

Note: All key-values pairs in toml ﬁle take one line, but here we  
must broke it to multiple lines to make it more readable.

We must provide functions for reading Database conﬁguration in  
conﬁg package. Inside database.go ﬁle in conﬁg folder we will

export four functions, one for each conﬁguration parameter.


var (  
// GetDatabaseConnectionString returns connection string  
// from database section in toml ﬁle  
GetDatabaseConnectionString = getDatabaseConnectionString

// GetDatabaseMaxIdleConnections returns max idle connection

// from database section in toml ﬁle  
GetDatabaseMaxIdleConnections = getDatabaseMaxIdleConnections

// GetDatabaseMaxOpenConnections returns max open connections  
// from database section in toml ﬁle  
GetDatabaseMaxOpenConnections =  
getDatabaseMaxOpenConnections

// GetDatabaseConnectionMaxLifetime returns connection max  
lifetime

// from database section in toml ﬁle  
GetDatabaseConnectionMaxLifetime =  
getDatabaseConnectionMaxLifetime  
)

We will use functions deﬁned in conﬁg.go ﬁle to read  
conﬁguration from toml ﬁle. We use section tag and key to read  
proper value from conﬁguration.

func getDatabaseConnectionString() string {  
return getConﬁgString("database.connection_string")  
}


func getDatabaseMaxIdleConnections() int {  
return getConﬁgInt("database.max_idle_connections")  
}

func getDatabaseMaxOpenConnections() int {  
return getConﬁgInt("database.max_open_connections")

}

func getDatabaseConnectionMaxLifetime() time.Duration {  
return getConﬁgDuration("database.connection_max_lifetime")  
}

Inside dbserver function in ﬁle database.go we will have two  
exported functions, InitializeDb() which is used in main() function  
and PrepareDbRunner() which we mentioned in HTTP Layer.

var (  
// InitializeDb initializes database  
InitializeDb = initializeDb

// PrepareDbRunner creates db runner for master database

// and puts it in context

PrepareDbRunner = prepareDbRunner  
)

First, we will considered implementation details of InitializeDb()  
function. In this function, we will read conﬁguration from toml  
ﬁle and use it to initialize database handler.


func initializeDb() (err error) {  
connectionString := conﬁg.GetDatabaseConnectionString()  
maxIdleConnections := conﬁg.GetDatabaseMaxIdleConnections()  
maxOpenConnections := conﬁg.GetDatabaseMaxOpenConnections()  
connectionMaxLifetime :=  
conﬁg.GetDatabaseConnectionMaxLifetime()

dbHandler, err = initDbHandle("master", "postgres",  
connectionString, maxIdleConnections, maxOpenConnections,  
connectionMaxLifetime)

if err != nil {  
return  
}

return  
}

Database handler represents pool of connections safe for

concurrent use by goroutines. We will use database handler  
implementation DB from SQL package. We can deﬁne handler as  
local variable.

var dbHandler *sql.DB

In initDbHandle() we must check if non-empty database type and  
connection string are passed. If all checks are passed, we can  
open connection and set conﬁguration parameters from toml. At


the end of function, we will validate database handler. If  
validation fails, we must close connection.

func initDbHandle(  
name, dbType, connectionString string,  
maxIdleConnections, maxOpenConnections int,  
connectionMaxLifetime time.Duration  
) (dbHandler *sql.DB, err error) {  
if dbType == "" {  
return nil, errors.New("Database type is empty")  
}

if connectionString == "" {  
return nil, errors.New("Connection string is empty")  
}

dbHandler, err = sql.Open(dbType, connectionString)  
if err != nil {

return nil, err  
}

// Initialize connection pool

dbHandler.SetMaxIdleConns(maxIdleConnections)  
dbHandler.SetMaxOpenConns(maxOpenConnections)

dbHandler.SetConnMaxLifetime(connectionMaxLifetime)

err = validateDB(dbHandler)  
if err != nil {

dbHandler.Close()  
}


return  
}

When we validate database handler, we ping a connection (check

if connection is still alive and establish it if is necessary. After  
that we can check database time zone. It’s good to set time zone

to Coordinated Universal Time It’s good practice to store all  
timestamps in UTC format and convert it to user timezone when

create response if that is required. Time zone checking is optional  
if you don’t want to include this just leave out this part of code.

func validateDB(dbHandler *sql.DB) (err error) {

err = dbHandler.Ping()  
if err != nil {

return  
}

timeZone, err := readDatabaseTimeZone(context.Background(),

dbHandler)  
if err != nil {

return  
}

if timeZone != "UTC" {  
err = fmt.Errorf("Database 'timezone' must be set to 'UTC'.

Currently it's '%v'", timeZone)  
return

}  
return

}


Function readDatabaseTimeZone() is simple. It just executes the  
simple query that returns time zone set in database.

func readDatabaseTimeZone(ctx context.Context, dbHandler

*sql.DB) (timeZone string, err error) {

rowsTimeZone, err := dbHandler.QueryContext(ctx, "show  
timezone")

if err != nil {  
return

}

defer rowsTimeZone.Close()  
if !rowsTimeZone.Next() {

err = fmt.Errorf("No time zone")  
return

}

err = rowsTimeZone.Scan(&timeZone)

return

}

Now it’s ﬁnally time for us to see the implementation of  
PrepareDbRunner() function. This function will create new

Database Runner and put it in context. Database Runner is  
special struct that is used for interaction with database.


We can use createRunner() function to create new Database  
Runner and assign database handler to runner.

func createRunner(db *sql.DB) Runner {

run := new(dbRunner)  
run.db = db

return run  
}

In order to put value in context, we must create special

representation of context key. Inside value folder we will create  
new ﬁle context.go and put empty struct and variable inside.

// ContextKeyDbRunner is a key for context.Context to extract db

runner  
var ContextKeyDbRunner = contextKeyDbRunner{}

type contextKeyDbRunner struct{}

Now when we want to put Database Runner from context or get  
it from context we will use variable Function WithValue() from

context package will put provided key (variable value (Database  
Runner created with createRunner() function) into context.

func prepareDbRunner(ctx context.Context) context.Context {

return context.WithValue(ctx,  
values.ContextKeyDbRunner, createRunner(dbHandler))

}

Now we can talk about Database Runner with more details.


5.1.6 DB Runner

Database Runner is a special struct that implements interface for

database access. We will develop generic Runner interface, we will  
not use all interface methods, but it’s important to mention all of  
them here.

We will create struct dbRunner to implement database access

interface which we will call Runner. The struct will consist of for  
ﬁelds. The ﬁrst ﬁeld (db) will represent pool of connections (DB  
struct from package SQL) and we already see how to initialize

and prepare connection pool.

Database transaction can be deﬁned as set of multiple database  
operations that create a logical unit of work. We don’t have a  
need for transactions in our application, but it’s important

concept worth mentioning. Here is a typical example of  
transaction usage. We have a set of database operations, in that  
set we have two write operations. In order to successfully ﬁnish  
work and leave database in consistent state all operations must  
execute properly.

First write operation successfully write new data, but second fails  
from some reason (invalid query, data violation error). Without

transaction all operations will be executed separately and ﬁrst  
write will be visible and aﬀect database. Now database is not  
consistent and we have invalid data in it. If we put all operations


in transaction, when second write fails we can rollback transaction

and discard all changes made before error (in our example ﬁrst  
write) and return database in state before start of the transaction.

We need one ﬁeld in our struct to represent transactions. Inside  
SQL package, we have struct Tx that implements database  
transactions. Field txCount is a counter that we will use to see  
how many functions is executed in transaction (everything will be  
clearer when we see the implementation of the Runner interface).

Sometimes we don’t need pool of database connections, one

connection is enough. It’s expensive to allocate whole pool for  
just one connection, one struct ﬁeld to represent single  
connection will handle that situation. In package SQL, struct Conn  
is the implementation of single database connection.

type dbRunner struct {  
db *sql.DB

tx *sql.Tx  
conn *sql.Conn  
txCount int  
}

Interface Runner will have seven functions. We will see details of  
implementation for each of them.

// Runner is an interface for database access  
type Runner interface {


Transact(ctx context.Context, txOptions *sql.TxOptions, txFunc  
func() error) error  
Conn(ctx context.Context, connFunc func() error) error  
Query(ctx context.Context, query string, args …interface{})  
(rows *sql.Rows, err error)  
QueryRow(ctx context.Context, query string, args …  
interface{}) (row *sql.Row)  
Exec(ctx context.Context, query string, args …interface{})  
(res sql.Raesult, err error)  
Prepare(ctx context.Context, query string)

(stmt *sql.Stmt, err error)  
IsInTransaction() bool  
}

Method Transact() is probably the most complex. This method  
will execute function passed as a parameter in database  
transaction. Nested transactions are not supported but it’s  
possible to have multiple Transact() calls simultaneously. Only ﬁrst  
Transact() will create database transaction, other Transact() calls  
will be run inside database transaction created by ﬁrst

If the value of tx pointer is nil, we will start new database

transaction and update counter. Depending on whether we use  
single connection or connection pool we will call proper function  
and handle potential error. At the end of method, we will call  
function that is passed as a parameter.

func (run *dbRunner) Transact(ctx context.Context, txOptions   
*sql.TxOptions, txFunc func() error) (err error) {  
if run.tx == nil {


var tx *sql.Tx

if run.conn == nil {  
tx, err = run.db.BeginTx(ctx, txOptions)  
} else {  
tx, err = run.conn.BeginTx(ctx, txOptions)  
}

if err != nil {  
return err  
}

run.tx = tx  
run.txCount = 1

} else {  
run.txCount++  
}

defer func() {  
// Function body  
}()

err = txFunc()

return  
}

Every transaction can be ﬁnished by commit (successfully) or  
rollback (unsuccessfully). We will handle that situation inside


anonymous defer function. In case of error or panic we must  
rollback transaction and update counter. In order to handle panic,  
ﬁrst we must it, we can do that with recover() function. When we

rollback transaction we should re-panic (if panic caused  
transaction rollback) or return error (if error caused transaction  
rollback).

// Recover from panic  
p := recover()

//Rollback the transaction in case of error or panic  
if (err != nil || p != nil) && run.tx != nil {  
run.tx.Rollback() // ignoring error  
run.tx = nil  
run.txCount = 0  
}  
// Re-panic if was panicking  
if p != nil {  
panic(p)  
}

if err != nil {  
return  
}

If for any reason we enter anonymous function with value nil for  
tx pointer, this can be considered as fatal error. We don’t have  
any way to handle that situation so we will use panic to abort  
method and exit program.

if run.tx == nil {


panic("Transaction is already rolledback or committed")  
}

If all went well, we can decrease counter value. If counter reaches  
zero we can commit our transaction and set tx pointer value to  
nil. Some error can occur during commit and we need to handle  
them. If transaction failed because of the ﬁnished context, we  
should return context error instead of transaction error.

// Decrement tx counter and commit tx  
// if it's the last one in Transaction chain  
run.txCount--  
if run.txCount == 0 {  
err = run.tx.Commit()  
if err == sql.ErrTxDone {  
ctxErr := ctx.Err()  
if ctxErr == context.Canceled ||  
ctxErr == context.DeadlineExceeded {

err = ctxErr

}  
}

run.tx = nil

}

Method Conn() will execute function passed as a parameter. All

calls of this method will use same database connection. If we  
already have established connection or we are in transaction we


can just call passed function. If that is not a case we can try to

establish connection. If connection fails we can try one more time  
(in some cases some small network problem can cause this error

and we don’t want to return error immediately).

At the end of method we will have connection established and we  
can call function passed as a parameter. We will use anonymous

defer function to close connection.

func (run *dbRunner) Conn(ctx context.Context, connFunc func()  
error)

(err error) {

// If it in transaction or already using single connection

// just call the function

if run.tx != nil || run.conn != nil {  
return connFunc()

}

run.conn, err = run.db.Conn(ctx)

if err == driver.ErrBadConn {  
run.conn, err = run.db.Conn(ctx)

}  
if err != nil {

return

}

defer func() {

errClose := run.conn.Close()  
run.conn = nil

if err != nil {


err = errClose  
}

}()

err = connFunc()

return  
}

Now we will have set of wrapper methods. Each method will

wrap around some method from sql package. Depending on  
whether we are in transaction, we use single connection or we

use connection pool proper method from SQL package will be  
called.

Method Query() is a wrapper around Method QueryConext() will  
execute database query (typically is used for select) and return

database rows.

func (run *dbRunner) Query(ctx context.Context, query string,   
args …interface{}) (rows *sql.Rows, err error) {

if run.tx != nil {

rows, err = run.tx.QueryContext(ctx, query, args…)  
} else if run.conn != nil {

rows, err = run.conn.QueryContext(ctx, query, args…)  
} else {

rows, err = run.db.QueryContext(ctx, query, args…)  
}


return

}

Method QueryRow() is a wrapper around Method QueryConext()

will execute database query that is expected to return at most one

database row.

func (run *dbRunner) QueryRow(ctx context.Context, query string,  
args …interface{}) (row *sql.Row) {

if run.tx != nil {  
row = run.tx.QueryRowContext(ctx, query, args…)

} else if run.conn != nil {  
row = run.conn.QueryRowContext(ctx, query, args…)

} else {  
row = run.db.QueryRowContext(ctx, query, args…)

}

return  
}

Method Exec() is a wrapper around Method ExecContext() will

execute database query that doesn’t return database rows.

func (run *dbRunner) Exec(ctx context.Context, query string,   
args …interface{}) (res sql.Result, err error) {

if run.tx != nil {  
res, err = run.tx.ExecContext(ctx, query, args…)


} else if run.conn != nil {

res, err = run.conn.ExecContext(ctx, query, args…)  
} else {

res, err = run.db.ExecContext(ctx, query, args…)  
}

return  
}

Method Prepare() is a wrapper around Method PrepareContext()  
will prepare statement that can be used inside transaction.

Prepared statement is used to execute similar or same query  
repeatedly. This can be useful if we receive array of objects from

client and we want to insert them in database. All queries are  
similar but with diﬀerent values. We can create prepared

statement and execute them all.

func (run *dbRunner) Prepare(ctx context.Context, query string)  
(stmt *sql.Stmt, err error) {

if run.tx != nil {

stmt, err = run.tx.PrepareContext(ctx, query)  
} else if run.conn != nil {

stmt, err = run.conn.PrepareContext(ctx, query)  
} else {

stmt, err = run.db.PrepareContext(ctx, query)  
}

return


}

The last method IsInTransaction() will be used to check if runner

is currently in transaction. If value of counter is greater than zero  
runner is in a transaction.

func (run *dbRunner) IsInTransaction() bool {

return run.txCount > 0  
}

Runner that we implemented here is more complex than our  
needs, but we use it to discuss about some important concepts.

This runner can be used in basically every application and cover

most of problems that we can ﬁnd in development practice.


5.1.7 Row reader

In this chapter, couple of time is mentioned that we read

database rows. We should create simple interface for this  
operation. Interface will provide us with couple of methods that  
we can use for simple manipulation with database rows. This way

Data Layer functions will be simpliﬁed and more readable.

First, we will deﬁne struct that will be used to implement  
rowReader interface. When we read set of rows from database in  
addition to rows we must also know columns. We will need one

slice to represent values for one database row, and slice of  
pointer to that values. We need pointers in order to use Scan()  
method from SQL package. This method will copy columns of  
current row into values pointed by destination, parameter provided

as method parameter. Number of values must be the same as  
the number of columns in row. We will have one struct ﬁeld to  
handle potential errors.

type rowReader struct {  
rows *sql.Rows  
columns []string

values []interface{}  
valuePtrs []interface{}  
lastError error  
}


Interface RowReader will have couple of methods. Details of

methods implementation will be provided soon. Methods marked  
with RowReaderFxs will present set of methods to read values  
from single database row.

// RowReader is used to simplify reading of sql.Rows objects  
type RowReader interface {

ScanNext() bool  
Error() error  
RowReaderFxs

}

Method ScanNext() will iterate through rows (method Next() from  
SQL package) and copy row values into slice from rowReader  
struct (method Scan() from SQL package, as we mentioned  
before). If any error occur we must handle it by assign error to  
lastError ﬁeld from rowReader struct.

func (rr *rowReader) ScanNext() (hasMore bool) {  
if hasMore = rr.rows.Next(); hasMore {  
err := rr.rows.Scan(rr.valuePtrs…)  
rr.lastError = err  
if err != nil {  
hasMore = false  
}  
}

return  
}


Method Error() will return value of last error ﬁeld from rowReader  
struct.

func (rr *rowReader) Error() error {  
return rr.lastError  
}

We can read couple of diﬀerent data types from database.  
Depending on what we read (string, integer, timestamp of custom

struct) we will have special method to handle that situation. If we  
need to handle any additional situation we can add new method  
here.

// RowReaderFxs contains method to read values from single row  
in sql.Rows  
type RowReaderFxs interface {  
ReadByIdxString(columnIdx int) string  
ReadByIdxInt64(columnIdx int) int64  
ReadByIdxTime(columnIdx int) time.Time  
ReadAllToStruct(p interface{})  
}

We have three errors that may occur during reading of row  
values. If we try to read NULL as any type we should return  
Second error, will occur if we try to read wrong type (we want to  
read integer from database as string). When we try to read whole  
database row as Go struct we can ﬁnd unsupported type (we

have not covered situation when we try to read boolean, for


example). These errors will help us to handle all tricky situations  
during reading values from database row.

// Errors  
var (  
ErrorNullValue = errors.New("Null value encountered")  
ErrorWrongType = errors.New("Unable to convert type")  
ErrorUnsupported = errors.New("Unsupported type")  
)

First set of methods will be used to read single value from  
database. They are similar, we use switch statement to ﬁnd out  
type of value. If value type match the type we want we just return  
value. For slice of bytes we need to convert it to desired type. If  
value is NULL we will return In any other case we will return

func (rr *rowReader) ReadByIdxString(columnIdx int) string {  
switch value := rr.values[columnIdx].(type) {  
case string:  
return value  
case []byte:  
return string(value)  
case nil:  
panic(ErrorNullValue)

default:  
panic(ErrorWrongType)  
}  
}


To convert slice of bytes into integers we will use ParseInt()  
function from atoi package. This function receives three  
parameters, string that we try to convert to integer, base (2 for

binary number, 8 for octal number, 10 for decimal number, and  
so on) an bit size (0 for int, 8 for int8, 16 for int16, 32 for int32  
and 64 for int64).

func (rr *rowReader) ReadByIdxInt64(columnIdx int) int64 {  
switch value := rr.values[columnIdx].(type) {  
case int64:  
return value  
case []byte:

s := string(value)  
i, err := strconv.ParseInt(s, 10, 64)  
if err != nil {  
panic(ErrorWrongType)  
}  
return i

case nil:  
panic(ErrorNullValue)

default:  
panic(ErrorWrongType)  
}  
}

To convert string or slice of bytes to timestamp we will use  
Parse() function. It receives two arguments, time format and  
string that represents time.

func (rr *rowReader) ReadByIdxTime(columnIdx int) time.Time {


switch value := rr.values[columnIdx].(type) {  
case time.Time:  
return value  
case []byte:  
time, err := time.Parse(time.RFC3339Nano, string(value))  
if err != nil {  
panic(ErrorWrongType)  
}  
return time  
case string:  
time, err := time.Parse(time.RFC3339Nano, value)  
if err != nil {  
panic(ErrorWrongType)  
}  
return time  
case nil:

panic(ErrorNullValue)  
default:

panic(ErrorWrongType)  
}

}

Last method ReadAllToStruct() is combination of all previous  
methods. We iterate through row and read it column by column.

func (rr *rowReader) ReadAllToStruct(p interface{}) {  
var value reﬂect.Value

value = reﬂect.ValueOf(p)

if value.Kind() != reﬂect.Ptr {  
return


}

value = reﬂect.Indirect(value)

if value.Kind() != reﬂect.Struct {  
return

}

for columnIdx, columnName := range rr.columns {  
if rr.values[columnIdx] == nil {

continue  
}

column := value.FieldByName(columnName)  
if column == (reﬂect.Value{}) {

continue

}

switch column.Kind() {

case reﬂect.String:  
column.SetString(rr.ReadByIdxString(columnIdx))

case reﬂect.Int, reﬂect.Int8, reﬂect.Int16, reﬂect.Int32, reﬂect.Int64:  
column.SetInt(rr.ReadByIdxInt64(columnIdx))

default:

panic(ErrorUnsupported)  
}

}  
}


If we want to use RowReader in our application we must provide

function which will create and return it.

var (  
// GetRowReader returns RowReader interface

// that can be used to read data from sql.Rows  
GetRowReader = getRowReader

)

This method will create and initialize rowReader struct based on  
row parameter that function receives. We will use Column()

method to ﬁnd out column names. With that information we can  
calculate length of column slice and initialize value and valuePtrs

slices. Now we can initialize rowReader and return it for usage.

func getRowReader(rows *sql.Rows) (RowReader, error) {  
columns, err := rows.Columns()

if err != nil {  
return nil, err

}

n := len(columns)

rr := new(rowReader)  
rr.rows = rows

rr.columns = columns  
rr.values = make([]interface{}, n)

rr.valuePtrs = make([]interface{}, n)

for i := 0; i < n; i++ {


rr.valuePtrs[i] = &rr.values[i]  
}

return rr, nil

}

Our Database Layer is now ready. We only need one more layer,  
Data Layer, to connect our application into one coherent

functional module.


5.2 Data layer

Data Layer will except validated data from Core Layer, prepare

SQL queries and execute them. After query execution, values  
received from Database Layer will be prepared and send it to  
client through above layers.

All Data Layer functions will be part of data package, placed in

ﬁles inside data folder. All ﬁles in data folder can follow simple  
naming convention, they should be named after the table over  
which they execute queries.


5.2.1 Common functionsf

Some Data Layer functions can return same result type (string,

int, and so on). We can avoid code repetition if we create generic  
functions and separate them into special ﬁles. We will send query  
and potential parameters to generic functions and receive results.

These functions will be only used in Data Layer, so we don’t  
need to export them.

Our ﬁrst function will be used to receive strings from Database  
Layer. When we get Database Runner from Context we will use

Query() function to read rows from Database Layer. When we  
have rows in raw form we will need RowReader to convert them  
into desired data type.

With RowReader we can iterate through rows returned by Runner.

We only expect one result so there is no need to use for loop  
here. If we received more than one row from Database Layer only  
ﬁrst will be considered, others will be rejected.

We use ReadByIdxString() from RowReader to read a string from  
Database Layer. If any error occurs we handle it, and use defer to  
Close() method from sql package. This method will close the row  
and prevent any further enumerations.

func executeQueryWithStringResponse(ctx context.Context, query

string, params …interface{}) (result string, err error) {


dbRunner := ctx.Value(values.ContextKeyDbRunner).(dbserver.Runner)

rows, err := dbRunner.Query(ctx, query, params…)

if err != nil {  
return  
}

defer rows.Close()

rr, err := dbserver.GetRowReader(rows)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}  
if rr.ScanNext() {  
result = rr.ReadByIdxString(0)  
}

err = rr.Error()  
return  
}

The second and third functions are similar to the

executeQueryWith they use integer and timestamp reading  
methods from RowReader.

func executeQueryWithInt64Response(ctx context.Context, query  
string, params …interface{}) (result int64, err error) {  
dbRunner := ctx.Value(values.ContextKeyDbRunner).(dbserver.Runner)


rows, err := dbRunner.Query(ctx, query, params…)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

defer rows.Close()

rr, err := dbserver.GetRowReader(rows)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

if rr.ScanNext() {  
result = rr.ReadByIdxInt64(0)  
}

err = rr.Error()

return  
}

func executeQueryWithTimeResponse(ctx context.Context, query  
string, params …interface{}) (result time.Time, err error) {  
dbRunner := ctx.Value(values.ContextKeyDbRunner).(dbserver.Runner)

rows, err := dbRunner.Query(ctx, query, params…)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}


defer rows.Close()

rr, err := dbserver.GetRowReader(rows)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

if rr.ScanNext() {  
result = rr.ReadByIdxTime(0)  
}

err = rr.Error()  
return  
}

Our last common function will return number of rows aﬀected by  
query execution. We already see that we can use number of  
aﬀected rows to check if delete operation was successful (delete  
book).

We know that our result will not return any rows, so we will use  
method Exec() instead of When we execute query, we will read  
number of aﬀected rows and return it as response.

func executeQueryWithRowsAﬀected(ctx context.Context, query  
string, params …interface{}) (result int64, err error) {  
dbRunner := ctx.Value(values.ContextKeyDbRunner).(dbserver.Runner)


res, err := dbRunner.Exec(ctx, query, params…)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

result, err = res.RowsAﬀected()  
return  
}

With these common function we can easily implement our Data  
Layer functions for users and books. Let us look ﬁrst at  
implementation of user functions.


5.2.2 User functionsf

Our user functions will be placed in user.go ﬁle inside data folder.

We will deviate a little from our naming convention. Actual table  
name is but we use that name because user is keyword in  
PostgreSQL, so we will name our ﬁle user.go instead to keep ﬁle

name shorter.

We will have three simple functions, for user login, user  
authorization and to get userID for provided authorization token.

var (  
// LoginUser ﬁnd user with provided username and password

// and returns user's token  
LoginUser = loginUser

// AuthorizeUser returns user's role if user token exists  
// if token not exists, empty string will be returned

AuthorizeUser = authorizeUser

// GetUserID returns userID for provided token  
GetUserID = getUserID  
)

First function will be used for user login. This function will  
receiver username and password and return user’s authorization  
token. Result of this function is string so we will use


executeQueryWithStringResponse() common function to return the

proper data type.

This function will execute the simple SELECT statement. This  
statement will return selected columns from one (or more tables).  
This statement from our example has three parts:

we specify which columns we want to retrieve.

we specify from which table we will receive data.

we can specify conditions, only rows that match speciﬁed criteria  
will be returned.

In our example, we want to get token from library_user table  
where username and password are same as in HTTP Request.  
Passwords in database are encrypted so we must use crypt()  
function to encrypt passwords from HTTP Request in order to  
archive exact matches.

func loginUser(ctx context.Context, username, password string)  
(response string, err error) {

query := `  
select token  
from library_user

where  
username = $1  
and user_password = crypt($2, user_password)`


return executeQueryWithStringResponse(ctx, query, username,  
password)  
}

We use $NUMBER notation to pass parameters to query. Runner  
function which executes query will insert parameters in order in  
which we passed them (username as ﬁrst parameter, password as  
second). To be easier to remember, parameters come after the  
query in function call. If we provide more or less parameters than  
required, an error will occur.

Our second user function, authorizeUser() will return user role for  
provided authorization token. We use this in Handler Layer to  
decide if user can call the requested API. Here response is  
integer, so we will use executeQueryWithInt64Response() from  
common functions.

func authorizeUser(ctx context.Context, token string)  
(response int64, err error) {

query := `  
select user_role  
from library_user  
where token = $1`  
return executeQueryWithInt64Response(ctx, query, token)  
}


Our last function, is similar to the previous one. This function  
will return userID instead of authorization token.

func getUserID(ctx context.Context, token string)  
(response string, err error) {

query := `

select user_id  
from library_user  
where token = $1`  
return executeQueryWithStringResponse(ctx, query, token)  
}

All user functions are used for reading from database, but they  
are good to get us familiar with concept of Data Layer  
functionality. We will have more diverse operations talk about

book functions.


5.2.3 Book functions

We will have more variety of database operations in book

function than with user functions. For some functions than return  
speciﬁc data type (usually some struct), we cannot use one of  
common functions. We don’t need common functions for this

speciﬁc case, because it will usually happen once in Data Layer.  
Function body will be almost the same as body of common  
functions so we will point out only diﬀerences and important

implementation details.

When we return some complex struct to client, we must prepare  
it ﬁrst. Data Layer is location where we often do this. We use  
one common name for that structs, Data transfer objects By  
deﬁnition, in computer science, DTO is an object that is used to

encapsulate data and send it from one subsystem to another. In  
our case, subsystems are server and client applications.

There are some rules for naming structs that represent DTOs. We  
use object name (book, user, …) and add speciﬁc suﬃx. Suﬃx  
should be used when we return all columns from speciﬁc table.  
When we return list of system resources, we usually do not return

all details, we deal with the details when we return single system  
resource. We use suﬃxes and respectively.

If we want to use these structs in other layers of our application  
we must export them. We will have two types of structs, one for


the librarian, one for members. It’s good practice to keep all

DTOs in separate ﬁle, Here are DTOs that will be used in our  
application.

// BookEntity is struct used to describe book

// This struct contains all database columns converted to Go  
types  
type BookEntity struct {  
BookID string  
BookName string

AuthorName string  
Publisher string  
Description string `json:",omitempty"`  
Status int

CreatedAt time.Time  
UpdatedAt time.Time  
BorrowerID string `json:",omitempty"`  
}

// BookDetails is struct used to describe book  
// This struct is used in get single book API  
type BookDetails struct {  
BookID string

BookName string  
AuthorName string  
Publisher string  
Description string `json:",omitempty"`  
}

// BookInfoLibrarian is struct used to describe book


// This structure is used in librarian get all book API  
type BookInfoLibrarian struct {  
BookID string  
BookName string  
AuthorName string  
Publisher string  
Status int64

Borrower string `json:",omitempty"`  
}

// BookInfoMember is struct used to describe book  
// This structure is used in member get all book API  
type BookInfoMember struct {  
BookID string

BookName string  
AuthorName string  
Publisher string  
}

For some struct ﬁelds we use special tag `json:",omitempty"`. If  
some ﬁeld has default value (0, empty string, nil) and we use  
tag, this ﬁle will be omitted from JSON sent to the client in body

of HTTP response. Usually we omit optional ﬁelds, because they  
don’t need to have a value.

In book.go ﬁle inside data folder we will have set of functions for  
all kinds of manipulation with book resource. Here is the list of  
all functions.

var (


// CreateBook creates new Book  
CreateBook = createBook

// GetBook returns book  
GetBook = getBook

// GetAllBooksForMember returns list of books for member

GetAllBooksForMember = getAllBooksForMember

// GetAllBooksForLibrerian returns list of books for librarian  
GetAllBooksForLibrerian = getAllBooksForLibrerian

// UpdateBook updates book  
UpdateBook = updateBook

// DeleteBook deletes book  
DeleteBook = deleteBook

// GetBookStatus returns book status  
GetBookStatus = getBookStatus

// GetBorrowerID returns user ID of member who borrowed the  
book  
GetBorrowerID = getBorrowerID

// ChangeBookStatus changes book status  
ChangeBookStatus = changeBookStatus  
)


First function, createBook() will create new book resource in our  
system. This function will execute simple INSERT statement.  
Statement will create new row in database table. This statement  
from our example has three parts:

INSERT we specify table and column names.

we specify values that will be inserted into table.

We don’t need to specify all columns. If we omit any column,

NULL or default value (if deﬁned) will be inserted. For example,  
in our case, available is default status for the book, so when we  
create new value that represents that status will be inserted into  
book table.

This function will return all columns from database, but we can  
use ﬁelds that we received in request to populate DTO. We only  
need values for bookID and because these values are generated  
by speciﬁc database functions. RETURNING clause allows us to

retrieve values modiﬁed by

func createBook(ctx context.Context, bookName, authorName,  
publisher string, description util.NullString) (response *BookEntity,  
err error) {  
dbRunner := ctx.Value(values.ContextKeyDbRunner).(dbserver.Runner)

query := `  
insert into book(


book_name, author_name, publisher, book_description)  
values ($1, $2, $3, $4)  
returning book_id, created_at`  
rows, err := dbRunner.Query(ctx, query, bookName, authorName,  
publisher, description)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

defer rows.Close()

rr, err := dbserver.GetRowReader(rows)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}  
if rr.ScanNext() {  
response = &BookEntity{}  
response.BookID = rr.ReadByIdxString(0)  
response.BookName = bookName  
response.AuthorName = authorName

response.Publisher = publisher

response.Description = util.GetNullStringValue(description)  
response.Status = values.BookStatusAvailable

response.CreatedAt = rr.ReadByIdxTime(1)  
response.UpdatedAt = rr.ReadByIdxTime(1)

response.BorrowerID = ""  
}

err = rr.Error()


return

}

Next function, getBook() will execute simple SELECT statement in  
order to read single book from Database Layer. Statement is

similar to the SELECT statements we have seen before in user  
functions. Main diﬀerence is that this statement returns multiple

database columns.

We will return complex custom-deﬁned struct to the client. In  
order to read proper struct from database we will use

ReadAllToStruct() method from RowReader.

func getBook(ctx context.Context, bookID string)

(response *BookDetails, err error) {

dbRunner := ctx.Value(values.ContextKeyDbRunner).(dbserver.Runner)

query := `  
select

book_id as "BookID",  
book_name as "BookName",

author_name as "AuthorName",  
publisher as "Publisher",

book_description as "Description"  
from book

where book_id = $1`

rows, err := dbRunner.Query(ctx, query, bookID)  
if err != nil {


return

}

defer rows.Close()

rr, err := dbserver.GetRowReader(rows)

if err != nil {  
return

}  
if rr.ScanNext() {

response = &BookDetails{}  
rr.ReadAllToStruct(response)

}

err = rr.Error()

return

}

We need function to read list of books. This list will be diﬀerent  
for member and librarian users, so we must have two separate

Data Layer functions.

Function getAllBooksForMember() will return list of books to  
member user. Here we will have speciﬁc SELECT statement with

some new details. In WHERE clause, LIKE operator will be used.  
This operator will search columns for speciﬁc patterns.

We can use two wildcard characters to specify deﬁne pattern:


% (percent) represents zero, one and more characters

_ (underscore) represents single character

In our example, each book with contains provided pattern will be  
returned to user (for example we want all books that contain

term

Statements OFFSET and LIMIT are used for pagination. We  
already talk about pagination in Core Layer, here we can see SQL

implementation.

func getAllBooksForMember(ctx context.Context, searchTerm

string, rowOﬀset, rowLimit int) (response []*BookInfoMember,  
err error) {

dbRunner := ctx.Value(values.ContextKeyDbRunner).(dbserver.Runner)  
query := `

select  
book_id as "BookID",

book_name as "BookName",  
author_name as "AuthorName",

publisher as "Publisher"  
from book

where book_name like '%%' || $1 || '%%' and book_status = $2  
oﬀset $3

limit $4`


rows, err := dbRunner.Query(ctx, query, searchTerm,

values.BookStatusAvailable, rowOﬀset, rowLimit)

if err != nil {

return  
}

defer rows.Close()

rr, err := dbserver.GetRowReader(rows)

if err != nil {  
return

}

response = make([]*BookInfoMember, 0)  
for rr.ScanNext() {

book := &BookInfoMember{}

rr.ReadAllToStruct(book)  
response = append(response, book)

}

err = rr.Error()

return

}

We will (probably) have multiple rows response from Database  
Layer. In order to return all rows we must use for instead of if

in combination with ScanNext() function.


Librarian must know name of the user who borrowed the book.  
We have userID stored in book table and we can use that

information to discover name of the user. We can store name in  
two tables also, but name is something that is changeable and

not unique. User ID will be generated automatically by database  
function, cannot be changed by user and is unique. These ﬁelds

are the best candidates for storing this kind of information.

In order to connect user ID from book table and user name  
from library_user table we must perform JOIN operation. JOIN

will combine rows from two or more tables based on related  
column between them from book table and user_id from

library_user table).

func getAllBooksForLibrerian(ctx context.Context, searchTerm string,  
rowOﬀset, rowLimit int) (response []*BookInfoLibrarian, err error) {

dbRunner := ctx.Value(values.ContextKeyDbRunner).(dbserver.Runner)

query := `

select  
b.book_id as "BookID",

b.book_name as "BookName",

b.author_name as "AuthorName",  
b.publisher as "Publisher",

b.book_status as "Status",  
u.full_name as "Borrower"

from book b  
left join library_user u on u.user_id = b.borrower_id


where b.book_name like '%%' || $1 || '%%'  
oﬀset $2

limit $3`

rows, err := dbRunner.Query(ctx, query, searchTerm, rowOﬀset,

rowLimit)  
if err != nil {

return

}

defer rows.Close()

rr, err := dbserver.GetRowReader(rows)

if err != nil {

return  
}

response = make([]*BookInfoLibrarian, 0)

for rr.ScanNext() {  
book := &BookInfoLibrarian{}

rr.ReadAllToStruct(book)

response = append(response, book)

}

err = rr.Error()

return  
}


Function updateBook() will execute UPDATE statement which will

modify existing data in database. Statement from our example has

three parts:

we specify table name.

we deﬁne column name and new value for column.

we can specify conditions, only rows that match speciﬁed criteria

will be modiﬁed.

func updateBook(ctx context.Context, bookID, bookName,

authorName, publisher string, description util.NullString)

(response time.Time, err error) {

query := `

update book

set

book_name = $1,  
author_name = $2,

publisher = $3,

book_description = $4

where book_id = $5  
returning updated_at`

return executeQueryWithTimeResponse(ctx, query,

bookName, authorName, publisher, description, bookID)  
}


Function deleteBook() will execute DELETE statement. This  
statement will delete all rows that match criteria speciﬁed in

WHERE clause. Function will return number of aﬀected rows.

func deleteBook(ctx context.Context, bookID string)(

response int64, err error) {

query := `delete from book where book_id = $1`

return executeQueryWithRowsAﬀected(ctx, query, bookID)

}

We only need two functions for working with status. First

function, getBookStatus() will execute simple SELECT statement  
and return current book status.

func getBookStatus(ctx context.Context, bookID string)

(response int64, err error) {

query := `select book_status from book where book_id = $1`

return executeQueryWithInt64Response(ctx, query, bookID)  
}

The second function will set new book status, and update value

for If book is borrowed userID of borrower will be saved, in  
another case (when book is returned) borrwer_id will be set to

NULL.


func changeBookStatus(ctx context.Context, bookID string, status

int, userID util.NullString) (err error) {

dbRunner := ctx.Value(values.ContextKeyDbRunner).(dbserver.Runner)

query := `

update book

set

book_status = $1,  
borrower_id = $2

where book_id = $3`

_, err = dbRunner.Exec(ctx, query, status, userID, bookID)  
return

}

At last we need one more function, This function will execute

simple SELECT statement and return ID of a member who

borrowed the book.

func getBorrowerID(ctx context.Context, bookID string)

(response string, err error) {

query := `select borrower_id from book where book_id = $1`

return executeQueryWithStringResponse(ctx, query, bookID)  
}


Finally, our application is ﬁnished! But the development never

stops. There are always new opportunities for improvement.


5.3 Where next?

We have successfully developed small server-side application, but

our job is not quite done yet. We still need to prepare our  
application for usage. Even when we ﬁnish these, less developing  
task, we can ﬁnd additional optimizations and improvements for

our application.


5.3.1 Documentation

We want some client to use our application. Usually developers of

client application are not familiar with server application. We need  
to provide document where we will describe our API calls. Based  
on our documentation client applications will know how to create

HTTP Request and what to expect in HTTP Response.

Our documentation will probably be available on web. Markdown  
syntax is often used for text stylization for display on web.  
Generally, Markdown text is regular text with special characters

used for the implementation of diﬀerent styles.

Character # (hash) marks header. Number of hashes indicates  
level of heading. One hash is used for level 1 heading (largest),  
two hashes for level 2 heading, etc. If we want to bold our text

we can put it between pair of ** (double asterisk). We can put  
text between pair of * (asterisk) to mark it as italic. If we use  
single * (asterisk) at the beginning of the line this will be marked  
as one bullet in bulleted list.

Here is a documentation example for our Member API. At the  
start of document we will provide some information that is  
relevant to the entire API group. For each API we will provide

short description, HTTP Method, URI, request and response  
description. Optional characters are marked with ? (question  
mark).


# Member API

Only users with role member can call this API.  
Request must contains proper Token in Authorization Header.

## Get Book

Returns single book.

Method: GET

URI: /api/member/book/{book-id}

Response:  
* BookID (string)  
* BookName (string)  
* AuthorName (string)  
* Publisher (string)  
* Description (string?)

## Get All Books

Returns all available books that ﬁt search criteria.  
All parameters are optional.

Method: GET


URI: /api/member/book/all/?searchTerm={searchTerm}&oﬀset=  
{oﬀset}& limit= {limit}  
Response:  
* Data (BookData)  
* Meta (MetaData)

BookData is array of:  
* BookID (string)  
* BookName (string)  
* AuthorName (string)  
* Publisher (string)

MetaData:  
* SearchTerm (string?)  
* RowOﬀset (int?)  
* RowLimit (int)  
## Borrow or Return Book

Change book's status (depends on current status)

Method: PATCH

URI: /api/member/book

Request:  
* BookID

We can use online solutions to generate table of content for our  
documentation. On site https://ecotrust-canada.github.io/markdown-


toc/ table of content will be provided, based on passed document  
(we can omit general information when we past document). Here  
is generated table of content for Member API document.

\- [Get Book](#get-book)  
\- [Get All Books](#get-all-books)  
\- [Borrow or Return Book](#borrow-or-return-book)

We should have separate ﬁle for each API group. In our case, we  
will have three ﬁles inside doc folder: member.md and In ﬁgure  
we can see partial web view for document from our example.

Figure 5.9: Documentation web view


5.3.2 API testing

Before we publish our application for public usage, we should

perform some tests and check functionality. Postman is perfect  
platform for this task. In Postman we can execute all kind of API  
calls and check if our application works properly. It can be

downloaded from oﬃcial site

Here, we will display test of simple functional ﬂow. First we will  
call Login User API. Our application will run on our computer  
(localhost). In Postman we will enter proper URI select HTTP

Method (POST) and provide username and password in HTTP  
Request Body. If everything pass as expected, Authorization token  
will be received in HTTP Response. On ﬁgure 5.10 we can see  
properly prepared HTTP Request and received HTTP Response in

Postman.


Figure 5.10: HTTP Request and Response in Postman

We will use received token in HTTP Response Header for  
authentication. In our example we have member user so we can  
only call Member API. In ﬁgure we see the appearance of HTTP  
Header for Get All Books API.

Figure 5.11: HTTP Request Header in Postman

Postman is perfect platform for fast and simple API testing. We  
should always test our application before we submit it for mass


usage. When is possible we should provide special person who  
will only deal with the testing. One who develop application can  
overlook some situation, so it’s good to have a pair of fresh  
eyes.


5.3.3 Break a monolith into microservices

We ﬁnished our application, deploy it on some type of physical

server and prepare documentation. Client application can request  
API call and display system resources to end user. What can we  
do next?

We can improve existing functionality, for example, we can develop

proper User API, which will allow librarian or new type of user  
(admin) to add and remove users. Our current solution insert  
users directly into database and can be a problem to add new

users.

New functionalities can also be added. Maybe, in some point,  
members want to send messages to librarian with some kind of  
chat application. Good option is to have email service, which will

send information and updates to users so we can add that  
functionality.

More functionalities means bigger and robust application. This  
can cause a lot of problems. Code can be less readable and  
hard for maintenance. But luckily there is a way to avoid these  
problems.

Big, one-part application is often referred as monolith. We can  
break monolith into group of smaller applications that will


communicate between self and execute same task as original

application. These applications are known as microservices.

Breaking monolith into microservices ﬁll help us to avoid  
problems that we mentioned before, but can cause new problems  
typical for microservice architecture. We will discuss in details  
about these problems in next chapter.


Conclusion

We passed whole process of developing simple Server Side REST

Application. Principles that we learned here can be used for  
creation of all kind of server applications. For practice, you can  
create something similar to what we have done so far. It’s

important to understand concept of all layers to make it easier to  
follow material that will be presented in next chapters.


Points to remember

Database Handler is a pool of connections to database.

Database Runner is an interface for database access.

Row Reader is an interface for database rows manipulation.

Provide documentation with description of API calls.
