CHAPTER 6

Microservices

In the previous chapter we ﬁnished our REST Server Application.  
In this chapter, we will explain basic concepts of microservice  
architecture. This will include short history and some most

popular architectures. We will see some advantages and  
disadvantages of microservices over monolith and some common  
development practices.


Structure

In this chapter, we will discuss the following topics:

Basics of Microservices

History of Microservices

Microservice Architectures

Advantages of Microservices

Disadvantages of Microservices

Development Practices


Objectives

After studying this unit, you should be able to:

Understand the concepts of microservices.

Learn how to prepare breaking of existing monolith application to

microservices.


6.1 Basics of microservices

Microservice architecture is style that emerges and becomes

popular in the last ten to ﬁfteen years. Now is widely spread and  
most of the implemented it in their products. In this chapter, we  
will go over some basic concepts related to microservices.

Microservices are based on so-called Unix philosophy one thing

and do it Unix OS has set of small commands, each command  
is in charge of one job (copy, move, show, …). There is a way  
how to combine these small commands in order to execute

complex tasks. Microservices are similar to Unix commands.

Microservice architecture is architectural style that represents  
collection of services (individual modules). Individual service is  
highly maintainable and testable, organized around speciﬁc

business logic, usually owned by a small development team and  
can be deployed independently of the rest of the system.

Whole system is loosely coupled. In loosely coupled systems, each  
component makes use of little or no knowledge of the deﬁnitions  
of other components. Each component has her own knowledge  
and only use knowledge of other components when is really  
necessary.

Microservice can also be deﬁned as single function module, with

well-deﬁned interfaces and operations. The principle where every


module has responsibility over single part of system functionality

is known as Single Responsibility Principle

There are several characteristics of microservice architecture:

Multiple System is divided into several components. Each

component can be redeployed individually, so when problem  
occurs, we just need to ﬁx component and redeploy it, instead of  
redeploy of entire system.

Built for Every microservice is organized around single business  
functionality.

Simple Individual microservice will receive request, process it and  
generate response. We already implemented simple routing in our  
Library Server Application, so we don’t have to worry about this  
characteristic.

Database is distributed, each service will manage its unique  
database.

Failure Microservices must be motorized. With good monitoring,  
we can handle or even prevent failures. This can add a little bit  
of complexity to system.

New functionality can be easily added into microservice

architecture, without aﬀecting other functionalities.


These characteristics make microservice architecture ideal for large  
and complex systems where we need to have rapid, frequent and  
reliable deploy.


6.2 History of microservices

Term similar to microservices was ﬁrst used in 2005 by Peter

During conference presentation, Rogers used term to describe the  
direction in which web application development should move.  
Concept presented on that presentation is almost identical to

what we know today as microservices.

Historically, all software system from past can be categorized as  
monolithic systems (shorter monolith). Monolith application is  
single-tiered software solution where user interface and data

access are combined into single program

Monolith is not quite elegant and good solution. If we need to ﬁx  
one small problem we need to redeploy entire application. Over  
time a ﬁner solution was reached. In Service oriented architecture

program consists of several services connected to Enterprise  
Service Bus Bus is used for communication between services


Figure 6.1: Software architectures through time Application(App),

Service(S), Microservice(MS), Database(DB)

In SOA architecture, ESB represents bottleneck. All  
communications go through bus, but you probably have some

services that don’t need to communicate with each other.  
Microservices emerged as improvement of SOA. Bus is removed


and microservices communicate with each other when there is a  
need for it. Also, database is distributed between microservices

Netﬂix, online streaming provider, encountered constant problems.  
It becomes hard to handle rapidly increasing data and store it in  
the current data centers. They decided to take a risk and broke  
monolith application into microservices.

It was not an easy task. When completed, Netﬂix was able to

handle problems more eﬃcient and quickly. In order to share the  
knowledge and help others, they developed set of tools and tips  
that can help developers to easily break monolith to  
microservices.

After Netﬂix, many decided to take that step and move from  
monolith to microservices, including Amazon and eBay. Takes to  
the Netﬂix, microservices becomes standard in development of

large and complex applications.


6.3 Microservice architectures

With microservices there are no exact architectures. Everything that

follow characteristics and some development practices, that we  
will introduce later in this chapter, can be considered a  
microservice architecture.

In literature and all kind of articles, we can ﬁnd term Netﬂix

architecture. This is not the actual architecture created by  
following strict rules. Any microservice architecture created by  
following Netﬂix recommendations and using Netﬂix tools belongs

to the group of Netﬂix architectures. One of characteristics of  
Netﬂix architectures is usage of NGIX (web server that can be  
also used as email service, load balancer and HTTP cache) as  
central part of delivery system.

We can diﬀerentiate microservice architectures by decomposition.  
We have couple of decomposition categories.

Business capability (noun Single microservice will handle single  
system resource. If we want to broke our server application, we  
will have two microservices to handle book and user resources.

Domain Single microservice will handle single subdomain of  
application. In our example, book storage can be one subdomain  
(creating, editing and deleting books), interactions between

members and books can be second subdomain, and so on.


Use Single microservice will handle speciﬁc use case, like user  
authentication, sending an email or renting a book.

When we decide how to decompose we should choose model that

best suits our needs.


6.4 Advantages of microservices

Microservice architecture has a lot of advantages over monolith

architecture. Here, we will talk about some main advantages and  
give additional explanations.

As we mentioned before, microservice architecture consists of  
multiple small modules (services). Often we don’t need all system

functionalities. With monolith we don’t have a choice, we must  
deploy whole application. With microservices we can deploy only  
modules that we really need, and we can combine modules

according to requirements. This is known as modularity.

Each microservice contains small set of system functionality. It’s a  
lot easier to maintain small simple service than large monolith  
application. Less functionality also means less of code, code is

less complex, new team members can easily understand it and  
get involved into developer faster.

Smaller set of functionalities is easier to testing. We need less  
tests for individual microservice than monolith applications and  
each microservice can be tested separately.

With all these advantages (easier to maintain, understand and  
test), we need small team for each microservice. In articles, these  
teams are often referred as because one pizza should be enough

for lunch for whole team. Also, we can have distributed teams


for individual microservices. Teams only need to agree on ways of

communication and API calls.

We create system with small amount of communication and

dependencies between microservices or we have some failure  
recovery mechanism, if one microservice fails, whole system will  
continue to function normally. We just need to recover fallen  
microservice and all system functionality will become available.

What with situation when we change something in code? With  
monolith structure we must redeploy whole application. With

microservices we just need to redeploy microservices aﬀected with  
the code changes. Microservices can be deployed independently  
from each other.

Note: If code change aﬀects API used for inter-service  
communication, this will aﬀect couple of microservice. In that  
cases, we must redeploy all aﬀected microservices.

In software system, some functionalities are used more than  
others. Microservice architecture possess ﬁner scalability than  
monolith. We monolith if we want to scale our system we will  
deploy new instance of application. With microservice architecture,  
we can deploy new instance of speciﬁc microservices. For  
example, our user authentication service is overloaded, we will  
just deploy new instance of authentication service.

During development of application, we must integrate with some  
third-party service (for example with some payment providers).


With microservices we can accomplish this by creating service  
which will be used as between our application and third-party  
service. With this service we can API familiar to our system to  
API used in communication with third party software. This is  
much easier than importing new part of integration code into  
monolith application.

We can observe singular microservice as one small independent  
application. We can use that fact and have diﬀerent technology  
stack for individual microservices. We can have one microservice  
written in C++ and using some of NoSQL databases (MongoDB,  
Apache Casandra) and other written in Java and using  
PostgreSQL database. This is considered bad practice and many  
developers and system architects discourage this solution.

With microservices, we have many new possibilities that we would  
not have with classic monolith application. Like most things in  
life, microservices are not perfect. They can bring new challenges  
and problems.


6.5 Disadvantages of microservices

Most of the disadvantages of microservice architecture steam

from the fact that it is a set of small software modules. Modules  
must work together to provide complete functionality and perform  
all kind of complex tasks.

It’s simple task to test individual microservice, but if we need to

test inter-service communication, that can be problem. We need  
all necessary microservices in our test suite, but still network  
latency can cause communication problems and additionally

complicate testing.

We can have use case that aﬀects more than one microservice.  
This requires changes and redeploy of all aﬀected microservices.  
As we mentioned, usually, smaller teams are in charge of

individual microservices. Teams can be distributed around the  
world, so these changes require additional communication and  
synchronization between teams.

We have same problem of redeploy if we have dependent  
microservices. If we redeploy one of them we must redeploy all of  
them. When we have dependency we must track which versions of  
microservices are compatible. Incompatible versions can cause

failure of entire system.


If one of dependent services fails, whole system will stop working

properly. We must implement really eﬃcient fault tolerance system.  
If we have large number of dependent service, maybe our  
microservice organization is not good. We should try to  
functionalities of microservices. If this still does not provide  
satisfactory solution, perhaps microservice architecture is not  
applicable on our application.

We can break our monolith application into, basically, inﬁnite  
number of microservices. Is this really good practice? Maybe not

so much. If we have too many services it becomes hard to  
manage and monitor all of them. When it comes to the number  
of services we should ﬁnd the right measure.

Scalability is one of the main advantages of microservice  
architecture, but here we can have one big issue. Individual  
microservice can have a database, if we scale it, we must take  
care of data consistency. Each of them must have identical  
database copy.

Log can help us to detect and ﬁx potential problems. Each  
service will have they own logs. If we want to have whole picture,  
we must read logs from all microservices. Collecting and  
centralizing logs can be very hard and complex job. Often,  
communication or network errors can prevent collection of logs  
from some microservices, without all information we cannot  
properly reconstruct problem.

If design of our application has most of disadvantages listed here  
and not utilize advantages of microprocessor architecture, maybe


we should change design. In some cases, there is no need to  
develop our applications according to the principles of  
microservice architecture. Each application is diﬀerent and unique  
and architecture that gives good results in one, does not have to  
give good results in other.


6.6 Development practices

We will discuss some good practices for developing microservices.

Beginners will ﬁnd some useful tips and guidelines here. More  
experienced developers can use these practices to check if their  
current solution is eﬃcient and can check whether it follows

microservice principles.

System should be decentralized. Each microservice should have  
separate data storage (database). Shared database can be  
problematic. First when we scale our application, what if we need

to scale only one microservice that shares a database? Sure, we  
can provide some workaround for that problem, but that solution  
is usually ineﬃcient:

Figure 6.2: Inter-service communication


If two microservices shares database and one of them changes

database schema, this change can break other. In ﬁgure we can  
see example of microservice communication through a shared  
database.

We will certainly have some use case when one microservice  
needs data from other microservices database. In that situations  
we must create API for communication between microservices.  
First microservice will send API request to second microservice  
and receives required data from database

API must be stable and compatible. If this is a case, redeploy of  
one microservice will not aﬀect other microservices. It is not  
always possible to keep API unchanged. New functionality can  
require a change in the API.

API for inter-service communication and for communication with  
client-side application should be separated. Often we can use

diﬀerent protocols. This is useful in situations when we change  
API, if client and inter-service are not separated, change will aﬀect  
other microservices and client-side application. This will cause  
that more than one team must change their application in order  
for the system to function properly again. If API is separated,  
only one side will be aﬀected with changes (client application or  
other microservices).

All microservices will have similar functionalities, like logging,

error handling, and so on. It’s good practice to standardize these  
functionalities. For example, if each microservice has its own way


of logging, it can be really complicated to understand information  
collected from diﬀerent microservices logs.

We should try to use same design patterns for all microservices.  
We use design for our server side application. We will try to keep  
that design pattern for individual microservice which will be

created by breaking current version of application.

It’s consider a good practice to have separated microservices for  
cross-cutting concerns. Cross-cutting concerns are aspects of the  
programs that aﬀect other concerns. Authentication and  
authorization are examples of some most common cross-cutting  
concerns.

We should wrap our applications in containers. Containers are  
software units with code and all dependencies packaged inside.  
They allow us to easily run our application in diﬀerent computer

environments. Containers can help us to automate deployment  
process and execute everything quickly and reliably. We will  
present containers with more details in future chapters.

With can deploy more than one microservice per operating system  
(container). This is good practice for microservices that often

communicate. If we have them on one place, we can prevent  
communication problem caused by network latency.

Multiple microservices per operating system approach have some  
disadvantages. All microservices deployed in same operating

system must have same technology stack. If we want to scale one


of microservices, we cannot do that independently. We must scale  
all microservices deployed together on same operating system.  
These disadvantages are, probably, main reason why approach  
one microservice per operating system is preferable in practice.

One of more interesting discussion, when we talk about  
microservices, are related to number of code lines. Often we can  
ﬁnd teams that have limit in code lines per microservice. We can  
ﬁnd claims that somebody created microservice with 250, 100 or  
even 10 lines (the last one is probably urban legend). It’s  
consider bad practice to limit number of lines per microservice.  
Real limit for number of lines should be enough, enough lines to  
cover all functionalities that we need in individual microservice.

Load balancing is term used to describe process in which a set

of task are distributed over set of system resources. When we  
have more than one instance of microservice without load  
balancing one of them will be overloaded and scaling will not  
give the desired results. We can create our own load balancer or  
use some of the existing solutions.

Some of the most popular load balancing algorithms are as  
follows:

One of the simplest algorithms. Task will be distributed to system  
resources in rotation. If we have three microservices ﬁrst task will  
be distributed to ﬁrst microservice, second task to second  
microservice, third task to third microservice, forth task to the  
ﬁrst microservice, and so on.


Fixed Each system resource will have weight value assigned.  
Resource with the highest weight will receive all tasks. If resource  
with the highest weight value fails, all tasks will be delivered to  
next highest weight resource.

Source IP This algorithm combines source and destination IP to  
generate unique hash key. Key will allocate task to a particular  
system resource. If session is broken key will be used for  
direction to resource which is previously used.

We mentioned failure couple of times in this chapter. When  
several microservices communicate together, we can expect that  
one of them will fail in one moment. Until fallen microservices  
are turned on, other should continue to work as normal as  
possible.

Here are some of most used failure handling techniques:

Application design pattern that puts application elements into  
isolated pools. If one element from pool fail, another will  
continue to function. Pattern is named after the ship’s hull. If  
one section of hull is damaged, only that section will be ﬁlled  
with water. This will prevent sinking and ship will continue to  
function.

Circuit Application design pattern which will prevent application to  
perform action that will certainly fail. When is safe to retry,  
pattern will allow application to perform action. Pattern is named


after the electrical switch designed to protect electrical circuit from  
damage caused by overload or short circuit.

Asynchronous Exchange of data between two or more parties  
where all recipients are not required to response immediately.  
Email communication is real life example of asynchronous  
communication. When somebody sends us an email we can  
response whenever it suits us.

We will design system with information how long we are willing  
to wait for the response. Communication will fail only when the  
timeout expires.

At the end, this is more advice than practice. This is especially  
true for start-ups. When small company or start-up develop

application they don’t need to go with microservices in ﬁrst  
application version. First version should have only basic  
functionalities and can be monolith application that will be

broken into microservices later.

These are some of most useful practices that can help anyone to

handle some of most common problems. All of these practices  
follow microservice principles. We will see application of some of

them in next chapter. Of course, we will not include all of them,  
but it’s good to be aware of all these practices.


Conclusion

We learned basics of microservice architecture. With all subjects

covered in this chapter, we should have an idea how to  
implement this knowledge into our current and future projects.

In the next few chapters we will see how to break monolith  
application that we developed in previous chapter and add new

functionalities (microservices). Microservices will continue to evolve  
in the future. We must continue to follow their development and  
constantly improve our applications.


Points to remember

Microservice architecture is ideal for large and complex systems

when we need rapid, frequent and reliable deployment.

Choose decompose model that best suits your needs.

We should try to utilize all advantages of microservice  
architecture.

If we have large number of dependent microservices we should  
try to functionalities of microservices.

Following good practices can help us to create eﬃcient

microservice architecture.

Key terms

Microservice Architectural style which represents collection of

services (individual modules) bounded together to provide desired  
functionality.

Single-tiered software solution where user interface and data  
access are combined into single program.

Service oriented architecture Program consisting of several services

connected to Enterprise Service Bus which is used for  
communication.

Cross-cutting Aspects of the programs that aﬀect other concerns.

Software units with code and all dependencies packaged inside.

Load Term used to describe process in which a set of task is  
distributed over set of system resources.

Failure Design that helps to overcome the problems that arise  
when one participant in communication fails.


CHAPTER 7

Microservices in Go

In this chapter, we will see why Go programming language is a  
good choice for developing Microservices architecture. We will see  
how to break monolith applications created in the previous

chapters into Microservices.


Structure

In this chapter, we will discuss the following topics:

Go and Microservices

How to break Monolith into Microservices

Microservice architecture overview

Go Modules

Breaking database

Breaking application layers

Initial conﬁguration of Microservices

HTTP Layer

Handler Layer

Core Layer

Data Layer


Database Layer

Documentation


Objectives

After studying this unit, you should be able to:

Understand the concepts of Go modules.

Understand Microservice architecture that will be developed in this

book.

Learn how to break monolith application to microservices.


7.1 Go and Microservices

In the previous chapter, we have seen why Go programming

language is good choice for REST Server Applications. If we  
observe Microservice architecture as set of small REST Server  
Application, all Go advantages that we mentioned before are

applicable for Microservice architecture. Go also have couple of  
additional advantages that can be used speciﬁcally for  
Microservices.

We already mentioned that we do not need any additional

environment and dependencies for Go application. We just need  
to copy the executable ﬁle and potentially conﬁguration ﬁle to the  
server machine and start applications. If we have monolith  
application developed in Java we will need JVM to execute that

application. If we break that application into microservices we will,  
potentially, need JVM for each microservice. This will cause  
additional usage of system resources (memory) and we want to  
avoid that.

We will have some common functionality for each microservice. If  
we must write the same code for all common functionalities like

error handling, logging or conﬁguration reading we will have a lot  
of code repetition. Go programming language has a concept that  
can help us to overcome this problem.


Go modules represent collection of Go packages. We just need to

import Go modules and we can use them in our projects. This  
will allow us to have same error codes and error types (Internal  
Error, Validation Error, …), to use same logging functions or use  
same functions for reading of conﬁguration ﬁles.

We must recognize these common functionalities and start  
breaking monolith applications with extraction of them into couple  
of modules. Go modules are good concepts, because we can add  
new modules for additional functionalities when we need them.


7.2 How to break Monolith into Microservices

Here we will see how to break REST Server Application that we

developed in previous chapters into microservices. We will perform  
this in couple of phases:

Design overview of our application microservice architecture.

Moving common functionalities into Go modules.

Creating a separate database for each microservice.

Breaking layers of monolith application into equivalent layers of  
microservices.

Now we have a plan for how to perform our complex task, let us  
start with the ﬁrst one.


7.2.1 Microservice architecture overview

We will create overhaul design for our microservice architecture.

Our monolith application is quite simple so it will be easy to  
decide the function of microservices. Couple of monolith  
decomposition ways has been mentioned in the previous chapter.

In this case, we will go with business capability approach; single  
microservice will handle single system resource.

Our system has two resources, user and book, so we will create  
two microservices, marked as User MS and Book MS in Figure

User microservice will handle something that we can call User  
API (Open API from our server application). Book microservice  
will handle Book API consisting of Member API and Librarian API  
from the server application. Now we will have two small REST

Server Application and client will send HTTP Requests to the one  
in charge of them.

Now we have one small problem. User microservice will handle  
all user-related data (user ID, user role, …) that we need in the  
book microservice for permission checking and book borrowing.  
Book microservice will not have an access to user Microservices

database (if we follow good development practices described in  
the previous chapter).

We can create communication channel between microservices  
where they can exchange data. Communication channel API will


be separated from standard HTTP API (User API and Book API).

In Figure we can see the complete microservice architecture for  
our system:

Figure 7.1: Microservices architecture


7.2.2 Go Modules

The newest versions of Go programming languages forces usage

of modules. We can put a group of packages that we need for  
certain functionalities into one module and include that module  
into other projects (modules).

If we examine our REST Server Application we can ﬁnd three

functionalities that will be common for all microservices:

Reading and initialization of conﬁguration.

Database functionalities (row reader, database runner, …).

Errors (same error types and codes for all microservices)

We can use that knowledge to create Go modules. All modules  
will have same root folder that we can call gomodules. This  
folder will be for import of our common modules into other  
modules. We will use these sub-modules for all small modules  
inside gomodules folder.

We already have all code that we needed for our modules, we

will just copy ﬁles from our monolith application with small  
adjustments.


Inside gomodules, we will create sub-folder conﬁgmodule for our

ﬁrst sub-module. We will copy conﬁg.go ﬁle from REST Server  
Application conﬁg folder. Inside conf.go ﬁle we have InitConﬁg()  
function that will read conﬁguration from toml ﬁle and couple of  
functions for reading of speciﬁc type from the conﬁguration. In  
our monolith application, we use these functions inside same  
package, but now they will be a part of new module, so we must  
export them.

var (

// InitConﬁg reads conﬁguration from TOML ﬁle  
InitConﬁg = initConﬁg

// GetConﬁgString reads string from conﬁguration  
GetConﬁgString = getConﬁgString

// GetConﬁgDuration reads time.Duration from conﬁguration  
GetConﬁgDuration = getConﬁgDuration

// GetConﬁgInt reads int from coniguration  
GetConﬁgInt = getConﬁgInt  
)

We should change package name from conf to conﬁg module  
(ﬁrst line of conﬁg.go ﬁle). Our ﬁrst sub-module is ready.

For our second sub-module, we will create new sub-folder,  
databasemodule, inside gomodules folder. We will move these ﬁles  
from our monolith application into databasemodule sub-folder:


util/nullString.go

server/dbserver/rowreader.go

server/dbserver/runner.go

We only need to change package name inside all ﬁles to  
databasemodule and our second sub-module is ready.

For our third sub-module, we just copy util/error.go ﬁle into newly  
created errormodule subfolder and change package name from util  
to errormodule (ﬁrst line of error.go ﬁle). In Figure 7.2 we can see  
ﬁle structure of our Go modules.

In order to create Go module, we must execute this command in  
modules root folder.

go mon init gomodules

This command will create go.mod ﬁle with the following content:

module gomodules

go 1.13


All sub-modules will be considered as part of module. When we  
include gomodules into other modules, all sub-modules  
(conﬁgmodule, databasemodule, and errormodule) will be  
available, so we do not need to execute go module init command  
in all sub-folders of gomodules folder:

Figure 7.2: File structure of Go modules

When we start breaking layers of our application we will see how  
to import modules that we created here.


7.2.3 Breaking database

All microservice projects will have same folder (package) structure

as REST Server Application project, except for one small change.  
We moved all functionalities from package util into gomodules so  
we don’t need it anymore. All database script ﬁles will be placed

inside data/dbscripts folders of our microservice projects.

First, we will database from our monolith application. User  
microservice will handle everything related to users which mean  
that tables librarian_user and enum_user_role will be placed into

user database

Figure 7.3: User Microservices database

Book microservice will handle everything related to books. We will  
use book and enum_book_status tables designed in the previous

chapters. Table library_user is not in same database as book, so


we must remove foreign key from borrower_id column. Everything

else will stay same

Figure 7.4: Book microservice database

Note: We already described and give explanation for all code that  
we developed in the previous chapters. In this chapter, we will  
just give instructions how to refactor code for individual  
microservices.

New code parts will be properly described and explained.

Both microservices will have public_schema.sql script ﬁle for  
database creation. We will mostly reuse scripts that we created for  
monolith applications. At the start of both scripts, we will set

options for timeouts, encoding, validation and install extensions,  
same as we do for monolith application.


SET statement_timeout = 0;  
SET lock_timeout = 0;  
SET idle_in_transaction_session_timeout = 0;  
SET client_encoding = 'UTF8';  
SET standard_conforming_strings = on;

SET check_function_bodies = false;  
SET client_min_messages = warning;  
SET row_security = oﬀ;

CREATE EXTENSION IF NOT EXISTS plpgsql WITH SCHEMA  
pg_catalog;  
CREATE EXTENSION IF NOT EXISTS "uuid-ossp" WITH SCHEMA  
pg_catalog;

SET search_path = public, pg_catalog;  
SET default_tablespace = '';

Extension pgcrypto will be used for password encryption. We have  
passwords only in user microservice so we do not need this line  
in public_schema.sql for book service (this will be only diﬀerence  
in this section of the script).

CREATE EXTENSION IF NOT EXISTS pgcrypto WITH SCHEMA  
pg_catalog;

For user microservice public_schema.sql script we will use queries  
from monolith application to create library_user and  
enum_user_role tables.


\-- enum_user_role  
CREATE TABLE enum_user_role (  
code integer NOT NULL,  
user_role text NOT NULL,  
CONSTRAINT enum_user_status_pk PRIMARY KEY (code)  
);

\-- library_user  
CREATE TABLE library_user (  
user_id uuid NOT NULL DEFAULT uuid_generate_v1mc(),  
username text NOT NULL UNIQUE,  
user_password text NOT NULL,

full_name text NOT NULL,  
user_role integer DEFAULT 1,

token uuid NOT NULL DEFAULT uuid_generate_v1mc(),  
CONSTRAINT library_user_pk PRIMARY KEY (user_id),  
CONSTRAINT fk_library_user_user_role FOREIGN KEY (user_role)  
REFERENCES enum_user_role (code) MATCH SIMPLE  
ON UPDATE NO ACTION  
ON DELETE NO ACTION  
);

For the book microservice public_schema.sql script, we will use

queries from monolith application to create book and  
enum_book_status tables, we just need to remove the foreign key  
from borrower_id that referenced user_id from library_user table  
with all indexes.

\-- enum_book_status


CREATE TABLE enum_book_status (  
code integer NOT NULL,  
book_status text NOT NULL,

CONSTRAINT enum_book_status_pk PRIMARY KEY (code)  
);

\-- book

CREATE TABLE book (  
book_id uuid NOT NULL DEFAULT uuid_generate_v1mc(),  
book_name text NOT NULL,  
author_name text NOT NULL,  
publisher text NOT NULL,  
book_description text,  
book_status integer DEFAULT 1,

created_at timestamp with time zone NOT NULL DEFAULT now(),  
updated_at timestamp with time zone NOT NULL DEFAULT  
now(),  
borrower_id uuid,  
CONSTRAINT book_pk PRIMARY KEY (book_id),  
CONSTRAINT fk_book_book_status FOREIGN KEY (book_status)  
REFERENCES enum_book_status (code) MATCH SIMPLE  
ON UPDATE NO ACTION  
ON DELETE NO ACTION  
);

CREATE INDEX book_book_status  
ON book (book_status);


We need to deﬁne database function which will set value of  
updated_at column to current data and time and trigger which  
will be executed when update operation occurs. We can completely  
reuse queries created for monolith applications.

\-- update updated at column  
CREATE OR REPLACE FUNCTION update_updated_at_column()  
RETURNS TRIGGER  
LANGUAGE plpgsql  
AS $$  
BEGIN  
NEW.updated_at = now();  
RETURN NEW;  
END;  
$$;

CREATE TRIGGER update_book_updated_at_column

BEFORE UPDATE  
ON book

FOR EACH ROW  
EXECUTE PROCEDURE update_updated_at_column();

Both microservices will also have init_public_schema.sql ﬁles for

initialization of database. We can completely reuse queries from  
monolith application. For user microservice, we should insert data

into library_user and enum_user_role table.

\-- enum_user_role  
INSERT INTO enum_user_role

VALUES


(1, 'member'),

(2, 'librarian');

\-- library_user  
INSERT INTO library_user(username, user_password, full_name,

user_role)  
VALUES

('joe', crypt('joe', gen_salt('bf')), 'Average Joe', 1),  
('smith', crypt('smith', gen_salt('bf')), 'John Smith', 2);

Book table initially will be empty so we just need to initialize

enum_book_status table for book microservice database.

\-- enum_book_status

INSERT INTO enum_book_status

VALUES  
(1, 'available'),

(2, 'borrowed');

Now when we have all scripts that we need, we can use them in  
as we described in Chapter to create databases for our

microservices (we can call them user_db and Now we can start

7.2.4 Breaking application layers

Now, we have databases for both microservices, our next step is

to break all layers of REST Server Application. We will follow the  
same order that we followed during creation of the ﬁrst  
(monolith) version of our application. Before we start with the

ﬁrst layer (HTTP Layer) we will deal with initial conﬁguration of  
microservices.


7.2.4.1 Initial conﬁguration of microservices

We can almost completely reuse the main() function from our

REST Server Application. For each microservice we will create new  
ﬁles for the main package and and conﬁguration and Code from  
our monolith application will be referred as for easier

understanding.

When we copy code of main() function we need to adjust it a  
little bit. In original code, we have two lines used for logging at  
the beginning and end of function body.

log.Println("Starting Library Server")

log.Println("Library Server Stopped")

These messages are no longer suitable for our microservices and  
we can change them. In the main() function for user microservice  
original lines of code will be replaced with:

log.Println("Starting User Microservice")  
log.Println("User Microservice Stopped")

Similarly these lines with be raplaces inside main() function for  
book microservice with:

log.Println("Starting Book Microservice")


log.Println("Book Microservice Stopped")

Call of InitConﬁg() function is a part of main() function. That  
function is no longer a part of conﬁg package, we moved it to

gomodules. In order to use gomodules, we must import them. In  
order to import gomodules, we should add these lines in go.mod  
ﬁles in both microservices.

require gomodules v0.0.0  
replace gomodules => ../gomodules

The ﬁrst command indicates module that we want to import into

our module. The second command will map required module to  
ﬁle path on our computer represents relative path to gomodules)  
if we use modules from our local machine. Path can be diﬀerent  
in your projects.

When we successfully imported modules into our microservice  
projects, we just need to change call of In original code we  
called function from conf package and conﬁguration was placed  
into library.toml ﬁle.

err := conﬁg.InitConﬁg("library", nil)

In our microservices, we will call function from who is a part of

gomodules. Conﬁguration for user microservice will be saved in


err := conﬁgmodule.InitConﬁg("userservice", nil)

Book microservice will have conﬁguration in bookservice.toml ﬁle.

err := conﬁgmodule.InitConﬁg("bookservice", nil)

First step in breaking our monolith application is completed.  
Now, we move to the next step, breaking of HTTP Layer.


7.2.4.2 HTTP layer

Most of the original code used for HTTP layer of REST Server

Application can be reused for HTTP layer of microservices.  
Everything connected to HTTP layer from main() function (starting  
of HTTP server and synchronization with WaitGroup) can be

completely reused.

Inside conﬁg folder in ﬁle we will put all functions that we need  
to read HTTP section of toml ﬁle. We can reuse original code of  
monolith application from http.go ﬁle in conﬁg folder, with small

adjustments. Functions for reading of speciﬁc type from  
conﬁguration are moved to gomodules. Here is an original  
function for reading HTTP server address:

func getHTTPServerAddress() string {

return getConﬁgString("http.server_address")  
}

Function getConﬁgString() is not in conﬁg package anymore, we  
must adjust this part of code and call it from gomodules.

func getHTTPServerAddress() string {  
return conﬁgmodule.GetConﬁgString("http.server_address")  
}


When we adjust all microservice functions from conﬁg package we

can completely reuse original code from ﬁle http.go (placed in  
server folder) in our microservices.

We have one more ﬁle in server folder related to HTTP Layer. In  
ﬁle serveAPI.go we have methods for listening and accepting  
HTTP Requests. We can copy the whole content of original ﬁle  
and perform small changes in one part of ServeHTTP() method.

This method contains an error handling part. Here is original  
code for that part.

if err == nil {  
httpResponseStatus = http.StatusOK  
} else {  
isError, errorCode, cause, errorType := util.IsError(err)  
if isError == true {  
response = util.ErrorResponse{

ErrorCode: errorCode,  
Cause: cause,  
}

httpResponseStatus = util.MapErrorTypeToHTTPStatus(errorType)  
} else {  
response = nil  
httpResponseStatus = util.MapErrorTypeToHTTPStatus(err)  
}

}


We moved everything related to errors in gomodules and we don’t  
have util package anymore. We just need to call functions from  
errormodule which is a part of gomodules.

if err == nil {  
httpResponseStatus = http.StatusOK

} else {  
isError, errorCode, cause, errorType := errormodule.IsError(err)  
if isError == true {  
response = errormodule.ErrorResponse{  
ErrorCode: errorCode,  
Cause: cause,  
}

httpResponseStatus = errormodule.  
MapErrorTypeToHTTPStatus(errorType)  
} else {  
response = nil  
httpResponseStatus = errormodule.MapErrorTypeToHTTPStatus(err)  
}  
}

Note: All changes apply to both microservices (user and book).  
HTTP layer will be identical for both of them.

As we can see, almost all HTTP layer code from monolith  
application can be reused in microservices. Can we move it then,  
to gomodules? Well not really. In this case, we will have two  
microservices with identical HTTP layers but this will not be  
always a case.


We can create microservice that will not communicate via HTTP,  
so we will not need HTTP layer. In our application HTTP  
Response will return JSON, but we can return other structures  
(xml, csv, etc).

We can use only one HTTP method (usually POST) and have

unique URI to choose operation we will execute on resource. If  
we use this approach we can block all non-POST HTTP Request  
in HTTP layer instead of Handler layer. For all these reasons it is  
better not to put functionality of HTTP later into gomodules.

One very important thing: If we are going to run all microservices  
on same machine we must choose diﬀerent ports for each  
microservice. We will run our application on our local machine so  
we will choose port 8080 for user service and port 8082 for book  
service. We intentionally skipped port 8081, in next chapter we will  
see why.


7.2.4.3 Handler layer

In Handler layer of our monolith application, we already have all

code that we needed for our microservices. We just need to  
rearrange it and put in a suitable microservice. Inside handler.go  
ﬁle from handler folder we can ﬁnd all code that we need.

Let’s start with Handler layer of user microservice. We will have

Request struct, handle() and handleOpen() functions from original  
code. User microservice will handle Open API from monolith  
application, so we will leave “open” case statement and default

statement in switch statement from handle() function.

switch {  
case strings.HasPreﬁx(uri, "/open"):  
return handleOpen(ctx, uri[5:], request)

default:  
return nil, errormodule.ErrInvalidAPICall  
}

For Handler layer of the book microservice, we will need  
constants from values package. We can copy and reuse entire  
consts.go ﬁle from values folder of monolith application. Book  
microservice will handle Member API and Librarian API of original

application so (besides Request struct) we will have and  
getParams() functions from original code.


As we do for user microservice we will do small changes on

switch statement from handle() function. We will leave and case  
statements and default statements. We will try to authenticate user  
in all remaining case statements so we can move that part of the  
code before switch statement.

userRole, err := core.AuthorizeUser(ctx, request.Authorization)  
if err != nil {  
return nil, errormodule.ErrNotAuthenticated  
}

switch {  
case strings.HasPreﬁx(uri, "/member"):  
if userRole != values.UserRoleMember {  
return nil, errormodule.ErrNotAuthenticated  
}

return handleMember(ctx, uri[7:], request)

case strings.HasPreﬁx(uri, "/librarian"):  
if userRole != values.UserRoleLibrarian {  
return nil, errormodule.ErrNotAuthenticated  
}

return handleLibrarian(ctx, uri[10:], request)  
default:  
return nil, errormodule.ErrInvalidAPICall  
}


Here, we will meet our ﬁrst big problem. Function AuthorizeUser()  
will return user role, but that information is in user microservice!  
In next chapter we will see how to rewrite this function to retrieve  
data from other microservice.

Before we can move to next layer we must do one more thing.

Errors are now part of gomodules so we must use variables from  
them. We must have (in both microservices)  
errormodule.ErrNotAuthenticated and errormodule.ErrInvalidAPICall  
instead of util.ErrNotAuthenticated and

Handler layer is ready. Real challenges will occur in next layers,  
so let’s move on.


7.2.4.4 Core layer

Reusing monolith application code for user microservice will be

quite straightforward task. We can reuse complete original code  
placed in user.go ﬁle from core folder. In authorizeUser() function,  
we use constant UserRoleUnknown to check if user exists.

Originally, that constant is placed in value package. Because this  
will be only constant from values package that will be used in  
user microservice, we do not need to create new package. We can

just add constant deﬁnition before deﬁnition of login() function.

const UserRoleUnknown = 0

Reusing original code for book microservice will be little bit more  
complex job. In function getAllBooks() when we get list of the  
books for the librarian, each borrowed book will have borrower

name attached to it. After breaking of monolith application,  
member names are in user microservice. Here, we must insert  
function that will retrieve borrower names from external source  
(user microservice). We will create this function in next chapter.

Function NewNullableString() is moved from util package to  
gomodules, more precisely the sub-module called We must change  
all occurrences of util.NewNullableString() function to

Inside function borrowOrReturnBook() we will call getUser()

function. This function is now part of user microservice. In next


chapter, we will see how to rewrite this function to communicate

and collect data from user microservice.

Before we can move to Data layer we must ﬁx one small thing in

both microservices. Error creating function was previously part of  
util package. Now the function is a part of errormodule, sub-  
module of gomodules. All occurrences of util.NewError() should  
be replaced with

Breaking original REST Server Application created a lot of  
problems that needs to be ﬁxed by microservice communication.

No matter how hard we try, inter-service communication is  
sometimes inevitable, and we must handle it.


7.2.4.5 Data layer

Similarly to Core layer, we can completely reuse original code for

Data layer of user microservice. We can just copy ﬁle user.go  
from data folder of monolith application into user microservice.  
We don’t need entire content of common.go ﬁle, only two

functions, executeQueryWithStringResponse() and

For book microservice we need three functions  
executeQueryWithRowsAﬀected() and  
executeQueryWithInt64Response() from original common.go ﬁle.

We mentioned, during braking of Core layer, that we cannot get

borrowed name, because that information is now on user  
microservice. In original code, function getAllBooksForLibrerian()  
used query with JOIN statement.

select

b.book_id as "BookID",  
b.book_name as "BookName",  
b.author_name as "AuthorName",  
b.publisher as "Publisher",  
b.book_status as "Status",  
u.full_name as "Borrower"

from book b  
left join library_user u on u.user_id = b.borrower_id  
where b.book_name like '%%' || $1 || '%%'


oﬀset $2

limit $3

We cannot perform JOIN with table from another database; we

must remove it from this query. Instead of full_name from  
library_user table, we will return borrower_id from book the table  
and use that information to get full names in Core layer.

select  
book_id as "BookID",  
book_name as "BookName",

author_name as "AuthorName",  
publisher as "Publisher",  
book_status as "Status",  
borrower_id as "BorrowerID"  
from book  
where book_name like '%%' || $1 || '%%'  
oﬀset $2  
limit $3

We will preserve original functionality, but in order to store  
information about borrower we must add BorrowerID ﬁeld in  
BookInfoLibrarian struct. We will have special JSON tag `json:"-"`  
for this ﬁeld. With this ﬂag we can use ﬁeld in our microservice  
and it will be omitted in HTTP response.

type BookInfoLibrarian struct {

BookID string  
BookName string  
AuthorName string


Publisher string  
Status int64  
BorrowerID string `json:"-"`  
Borrower string `json:",omitempty"`  
}

Same as in Core layer we should replace all occurrences of  
util.NewNullable String() to In both microservices, we will use  
Runner. Now moved from dbserver package to databasemodule  
we need to follow this change and replace dbserver.Runner with

Data layer of monolith application uses ContextKeyDBRunner  
struct from the values package. Data layers of user and book  
microservice will also use that struct. We can reuse original code  
in both microservices for that purpose.

With some small adjustments, we successfully broke Data layer.

Only one layer left, Database layer.


7.2.4.6 Database layer

Database layer becomes a lot less complex when we moved

Runner, RowReader, and NullString to databasemodule. There are  
couple small modiﬁcations left and our Database layer for both  
microservices will be ready.

First we need to read conﬁguration from database section of toml

ﬁle. We can reuse original code of monolith application from the  
database.go ﬁle in the conﬁg folder, with small adjustments.  
Similarly to HTTP layer, we must change the call of functions for

reading speciﬁc type from conﬁguration. Originally, those functions  
were part of conﬁg package, now they are part of gomodules  
(more precisely part of conﬁgmodule, sub-module of gomodules).

All original code related to database layer (call of InitializeDb()

function and error handling) from the main() function can be  
completely reused without any changes.

We can completely reuse original code placed in package dbserver  
(located in the server/dbserver/database.go ﬁle) with one small  
change. A struct dbRunner is now in databasemodule, and it’s  
not exported. Function createRunner() will try to reference  
unexported struct and this will cause error. The easiest, and most

elegant solution, is to move createRunner() function to  
databasemodule and export it. Than we can replace all calls of  
createRunner() with databasemodule.


There is only one small thing, we should change database name  
in conﬁguration (toml) ﬁle for both microservices. User and book

microservices will now use user_db and book_db database  
respectively instead of

All layers of original monolith application are now divided on two  
smaller microservices. We have just to do one very important  
thing, before we can try to solve communication problems  
between microservices.


7.2.4.7 Documentation

Each microservice should have API documentation. We did not

changed system functionality so we already have all  
documentation that we need; it just needs to be moved to proper  
microservices.

In original documentation we have three documentation ﬁles:

open.md

librarian.md

member.md

User microservice will handle Open API so we can copy (or  
move) ﬁle open.md to doc folder of user microservice. Librarian  
API and Member API will be the responsibilities of the book  
microservice. We just need to copy ﬁles librarian.md and  
member.md to doc folder of book microservice and our  
documentation is ready.

This was a simple but important task. Now someone who uses

our microservices will now how to call certain API to get desired  
response.


Conclusion

We ﬁnished one very important and complex task. A lot of

problems are covered in this chapter that can help in breaking  
any kind of monolith application. If we initially have a good  
organization of original application, breaking that application to

smaller microservices should be relatively easy to do.

In next chapter, we will try to handle problem of communication  
between microservices. This communication should be as small as  
possible, but sometimes it is inevitable.


Points to remember

Move all common functionalities into Go modules.

Choose the best suitable decomposition methods.

Reduce inter-service communication to minimum.
