CHAPTER 8

Microservice Communication

In this chapter, we will see how microservices can communicate  
with each other. Diﬀerent communication “ techniques ” will be  
presented and we see practical implementation for each of them.


Structure

In this chapter, we will discuss the following topics:

Communication methods

Protocol buﬀers

Message queuing protocols

gRPC

Proto ﬁle

gRPC Server

gRPC Client

Development practices

RabbitMQ

RabbitMQ Producer

Report microservice overview


RabbitMQ Consumer


Objectives

After studying this unit, you should be able to:

Understand how microservices can communicate.

Understand the concepts of protocol buﬀers.

Understand the concepts of message queuing protocols.

Apply communication techniques in real-life problems.


8.1 Communication methods

As we could see in the previous chapter, microservices sometimes

have to interact with each other. In this chapter, we will see how  
to implement inter-service communication solutions. Client-side  
applications use HTTP to communicate with our microservices.

We can use the same approach for inter-service communication,  
one microservice can send HTTP Requests to other microservice  
and wait for response.

It is good practice to separate API for external usage (client-side

applications) and for inter-service communication. To achieve that  
(and to show some other communication methods) we will use  
something diﬀerent from HTTP. We will present two popular  
communication methods: protocol buﬀers and message queuing

protocols.


8.1.1 Protocol buﬀers

Protocol buﬀers are the mechanism for serialization of structured

data. Developed by Google for internal usage, designed to be  
faster than XML, nowadays is one of the most preferable ways of  
inter-service communication.

Protocol buﬀers are language and platform neutral, so it can be

used for communication of applications written on diﬀerent  
program languages and deployed on diﬀerent platforms. How this  
is archived?

Inside special ﬁle (proto ﬁle) we describe data structures called

messages to deﬁne how structured data will be serialized. Every  
message will have ﬁelds consist of names and integers used to  
identify the ﬁeld. In proto ﬁles, services that can be invoked by

the sender (client) or recipient (server) of data structure.

Here is an example of one simple message, used to describe  
personal information for the user.

message PersonalInformation {  
string Name = 1;  
string Surname = 2;  
string Address = 3;  
int64 ID = 4;

}


We will use protoc to compile proto ﬁle and create binary ﬁles.  
We can create binary ﬁles for speciﬁc platform and language and  
distribute it to sender (client) and recipient (server). Both sides

will have binary ﬁles created from the same proto ﬁle and will  
know how to interpretate serialized data. In this way, we archive  
language and platform neutrality. If we compile message from our  
example, the sender and receiver will know that information  
tagged with 1 represents name, tagged with 2 represents surname,  
etc.

In Figure we can see the graphical interpretation of data  
serialization:

Figure 8.1: Data serialization


8.1.2 Message queuing protocols

Message queuing protocols are mechanisms created to support

sending and receiving messages between distributed systems.  
Senders and receiver do not communicate directly. Sender will put  
message in queue, receiver will pick message from queue

Figure 8.2: Message queuing protocols

Message queuing protocols are good for handling fail-overs. If  
microservices try to communicate directly and one of them is not  
available, communication will fail and the system will not work  
properly. Sender will send a message to queue and continue his

work. If receiver is not currently available, message will wait in  
queue to be picked. When receiver is recovered, he can pick  
message from queue.

If sender is not available, receiver will check queue for messages,  
queue will be empty, receiver will continue to work and check for  
messages later. Recovered sender will publish messages and  
receiver can pick them up.


In this approach of message queuing, multiple receivers can use

same queue but only one will process message. There are  
solutions that can handle situations where multiple receivers will  
receive each message from queue. Receiver will receive message  
from queue in same order in which they are received. This  
mechanism is often referred as publish-subscribe, where senders  
are referred as publishers and receivers are referred as  
subscribers.

RabbitMQ is one of the most popular solutions, we will see how

to use it for inter-service communication. Apache Kafka is a  
typical representative of publish-subscribe mechanism.


8.2 gRPC

A gRPC is protocol buﬀer solution, developed by Google. We will

see how to use this solution in order to ﬁx communication  
problems that occurred during breaking of monolith application.

Note: People usually think that gRPC is shorter for Google  
Remote Procedure Call. This is not true! gRPC is actually short

for “gRPC Remote Procedure Call”!

Before we can go with implementation we need to deﬁne services  
that will be handled by gRPC and deﬁne communication roles of  
our microservices. As we already know, book microservice needs

information from user microservice, so user microservice will be  
gRPC server (receiver) and book microservice will be gRPC client  
(sender).

Book microservice must authorize user through user microservice,

ﬁnd out user ID based on authorization token and ﬁnd out full  
name of user based on user ID. We can accomplish this with  
three gRPC methods:

AuthorizeUser

GetUserID


GefUserFullName

Now we are ready to communication barrier between our

microservices.


8.2.1 Proto ﬁle

Inside proto ﬁle, we will describe all services and messages.

Usually is a practice to put this ﬁle on gRPC Server. In our case,  
user microservice will represent gRPC server, so we will store it  
there. Inside server folder we will create folder usergrpc and put

ﬁle usergrpc.proto inside.

The ﬁrst line of proto ﬁle speciﬁes syntax that will be used. We  
will use proto3 syntax. Without this line, compiler will assume  
that we use proto2 syntax. The second line is a package

declaration, this line will help us to prevent naming conﬂicts if  
we use compiled proto ﬁle in multiple projects.

syntax = "proto3";

package usergrpc;

After package declaration, we can have import statements as we

have in regular go packages. In our case, we do not have a need  
for imports and will use standard data types and custom-deﬁned  
messages. Third line we will have go_package option. This option  
will deﬁne import path of package which will contain compiled  
code (binary ﬁle). In our case, binary ﬁle will be in usergrpc  
package.

option go_package = ".;usergrpc";


Now we can deﬁne service that will be exported by gRPC server.  
Basically, service represents simple interface, with couple of

methods. Each method will be declared with keyword rpc at the  
start, method name, request message as method argument and  
response message as method response.

// Interface exported by the server  
service UserGrpc {  
// Authorize user

rpc AuthorizeUser(AuthorizeUserRequest)

returns (AuthorizeUserResponse){}

// Get user ID  
rpc GetUserID(GetUserIDRequest) returns (GetUserIDResponse) {}

// Get user's full name  
rpc GetUserFullName(GetUserFullNameRequest)  
returns (GetUserFullNameResponse) {}  
}

We already know how to deﬁne message, now we should to see

what we need in each message. In AuthorizeUser() method will  
return user role for speciﬁed authorization token. We will have  
one ﬁeld in AuthorizationToken and one ﬁeld in

message AuthorizeUserRequest {  
string AuthorizationToken = 1;  
}


message AuthorizeUserResponse {  
int64 UserRole = 1;  
}

Method GetUserID() will return user ID based on provided  
authorization token. Message GetUserIDRequest will be same as  
GetUserIDResponse will have just one ﬁeld,

message GetUserIDRequest {

string AuthorizationToken = 1;  
}

message GetUserIDResponse {  
string UserID = 1;

}

Our last method, GetUserFullName() will return full name of user  
for speciﬁed user ID. Similar to previous methods, both messages  
will have one ﬁeld. GetUserFullNameRequest will have ﬁeld called  
UserID and GetUserFullNameResponse will have ﬁeld called

message GetUserFullNameRequest {  
string UserID = 1;  
}

message GetUserFullNameResponse {  
string FullName = 1;  
}


Now we must use protoc compiler to generate binary ﬁle. We can  
download oﬃcial protocol buﬀer release from After installation, we  
need to add protoc to system environment. In order to generate  
binary ﬁles for Go programming language, we should install  
protocol buﬀers plugin.

go install google.golang.org/protobuf/cmd/protoc-gen-go

With all dependencies installed we can execute this command to  
compile proto ﬁle and generate binary ﬁle.

protoc -I=source_path --go_out=plugins=grpc:destination_path  
source_directory/ﬁle.proto

Source path represents location where proto ﬁle is stored while  
destination path represents location where binary ﬁle will be  
saved. In order to generate binary ﬁle that can be used in Go  
application, we must add plugins=grpc before destination folder.  
Here is an example of protoc command executed from server  
folder:

protoc -I=usergrpc --go_out=plugins=grpc:usergrpc

usergrpc/usergrpc.proto

If we now take a look in the usergrpc folder we will see ﬁle We  
will use this ﬁle on gRPC server and gRPC client to implement  
inter-service communication.


8.2.2 gRPC server

Simple gRPC server will listen for gRPC requests. Similarity to

HTTP server we will start gRPC server from main() function of  
our user microservice (code is almost identical). Starting of gRPC  
server will be executed in new goroutine, started by calling oﬀ

anonymous function.

go func() {  
defer wg.Done()

log.Println("Starting gRPC Server")

err := server.StartGrpcServer()  
if err != nil {  
log.Fatalf("Could not start gRPC server: %v\n", err)  
return  
}

log.Println("gRPC server gracefully terminated")  
}()

Now we have two goroutines to synchronize on barrier, one for  
HTTP server and one for gRPC server. We must change value of  
Add() method parameter from 1 to 2.


wg.Add(2)

Before we see implementation details of StartGrpcServer() function

we can prepare conﬁguration. We only need one conﬁguration  
parameter, which represents address of our gRPC Server. Address  
usually have two parts, host (usually IP address) and port.

We will run our gRPC server on our local machine, so we can  
live host part of address empty. For port, we will use port 8081.  
Now we can see why we only used even ports (8080 and 8082)  
for HTTP server of our microservices and why we skipped port

8081 then. It is a good practice to use even ports (starting from  
8080) for HTTP servers and odd ports (starting from 8081) for  
gRPC servers.

We need to add gRPC section inside userservice.toml ﬁle.

# gRPC conﬁguration

# Connection string is in Go gRPC driver format:  
# :

[grpc]

connection_string = ":8081"

We must provide the function to read gRPC conﬁguration in  
conﬁg package. Inside grpc.go ﬁle in conﬁg folder we will export


function This function will use functions from conﬁgmodule to  
read connection_string from gRPC section of toml ﬁle.

var (  
// GetGrpcConnectionString returns connection string  
//from grpc section in toml ﬁle

GetGrpcConnectionString = getGrpcConnectionString  
)

func getGrpcConnectionString() string {  
return conﬁgmodule.GetConﬁgString("grpc.connection_string")  
}

Function StartServerGrpc() will be placed in grpc.go ﬁle from  
server folder.

var (  
// StartGrpcServer starts listening to gRPC requests

StartGrpcServer = startGrpcServer  
)

func startGrpcServer() (err error) {  
// Function code

return  
}

Outside function we will deﬁne empty struct userGrpcServer which  
will implement interface (service) deﬁned in proto ﬁle.


type userGrpcServer struct{}

At the beginning of startGrpcServer() function, we will read  
conﬁguration from toml ﬁle and call Listen() function from net  
package. This function will return Listener on which gRPC server  
will listen for requests. Errors that can occur during call of

Listen() functions will be handled.

address := conﬁg.GetGrpcConnectionString()

lis, err := net.Listen("tcp", address)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

Function NewServer() from grpc package will return new gRPC  
server with nor registered service who does not start to listen for  
request. We will pass that server and userGrpcServer object as  
parameters of RegisterUserGrpcServer() function. Now our server  
will have registered service, service that we deﬁned in proto ﬁle.

server := grpc.NewServer()  
usergrpc.RegisterUserGrpcServer(server, &userGrpcServer{})

Were we can insert part of the code that will listen for system  
interrupt signals and gracefully shut down gRPC server when  
interrupt occurs. This code will be executed in separated  
goroutine. We have almost identical code segment in our HTTP


server, shutting down of the server is the only operation that is  
diﬀerently executed here.

go func() {  
// Listen to operating system's interrupt signal  
interrupt := make(chan os.Signal, 1)  
signal.Notify(interrupt, os.Interrupt, syscall.SIGTERM)  
<-interrupt

// Gracefully shut down the server when it happens  
server.GracefulStop()  
}()

At the end of function, we will pass the Listener object to server  
with Serve() method call. Now our gRPC server will listen for  
requests.

server.Serve(lis)

Now we can see implementation of interface (service) deﬁned in

proto ﬁle. As we already mentioned we will use userGrpcServer  
struct from implementation. All three functions will be the same,  
we will extract data from gRPC request and pass it to Core Layer  
functions. We will pack response from Core Layer in gRPC  
response and send it back to gRPC client.

func (s *userGrpcServer) AuthorizeUser(ctx context.Context,  
request *usergrpc.AuthorizeUserRequest)  
(response *usergrpc.AuthorizeUserResponse, err error) {


userRole, err := core.AuthorizeUser(ctx, request.AuthorizationToken)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

response = &usergrpc.AuthorizeUserResponse{UserRole: userRole}  
return  
}

func (s *userGrpcServer) GetUserID(ctx context.Context,  
request *usergrpc.GetUserIDRequest)  
(response *usergrpc.GetUserIDResponse, err error) {

userID, err := core.GetUserID(ctx, request.AuthorizationToken)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

response = &usergrpc.GetUserIDResponse{UserID: userID}

return  
}

func (s *userGrpcServer) GetUserFullName(ctx context.Context,

request *usergrpc.GetUserFullNameRequest)  
(response *usergrpc.GetUserFullNameResponse, err error) {

fullName, err := core.GetUserFullName(ctx, request.UserID)  
if err != nil {

return


}

response = &usergrpc.GetUserFullNameResponse{FullName:  
fullName}

return  
}

In our proto ﬁle, we did not mention context and error in

method declaration, only messages. In this case, context  
represents context passed from gRPC client and Go will know

how to handle this. Go will also handle errors, if any error  
occurs on gRPC server that error will be returned to gRPC client.

We already have Core Layer function for user authorization. This

function is the heritage from monolith applications. But here we

have one small problem. In situation, when we trigger Core Layer  
with HTTP requests, we will prepare database runner in Handler

Layer. When we handle gRPC request we will Handler Layer. In  
order for our function to work as expected, we must prepare

database runner at the beginning of function.

ctx = dbserver.PrepareDbRunner(ctx)

Our second function getUSerID() will prepare database runner,

validate provided authorization token and call Data Layer function.  
If Data Layer function returns empty string, the user is not found

and error will be returned.

func getUserID(ctx context.Context, token string)  
(response string, err error) {


ctx = dbserver.PrepareDbRunner(ctx)

token = strings.TrimSpace(token)

if token == "" {  
cause := "Invalid value for token parameter"

err = errormodule.NewError(  
cause, errormodule.ErrorCodeValidation,

errormodule.ErrBadRequest, err)  
return

}

userID, err := data.GetUserID(ctx, token)  
if err != nil {

cause := "Failed to get user"  
err = errormodule.NewError(

cause, errormodule.ErrorCodeInternal,  
errormodule.ErrInternal, err)

return

}

if userID == "" {  
cause := "User not found"

err = errormodule.NewError(  
cause, errormodule.ErrorCodeEntityNotFound,

errormodule.ErrResourceNotFound, err)  
return

}

response = userID


return

}

The third function, getUserFullName() will also prepare database

runner, validate provided user ID and call Data Layer function. If

Data Layer function returns empty string, the user does not exist  
and error will be returned.

func getUserFullName(ctx context.Context, userID string)  
(response string, err error) {

ctx = dbserver.PrepareDbRunner(ctx)

userID = strings.TrimSpace(userID)  
if userID == "" {

cause := "Invalid value for userID"  
err = errormodule.NewError(

cause, errormodule.ErrorCodeValidation,  
errormodule.ErrBadRequest, err)

return  
}

fullName, err := data.GetUserFullName(ctx, userID)

if err != nil {

cause := "Failed to get user's full name"  
err = errormodule.NewError(

cause, errormodule.ErrorCodeInternal,  
errormodule.ErrInternal, err)

return


}

if fullName == "" {  
cause := "User not found"

err = errormodule.NewError(  
cause, errormodule.ErrorCodeEntityNotFound,

errormodule.ErrResourceNotFound, err)  
return

}

response = fullName

return  
}

We have two of tree Data Layer functions that we need to

complete gRPC server. Functions AuthorizeUser() and GetUserID()  
are inherited from Data Layer of monolith application. We only

need one additional function, This function will execute simple  
database query which will, for provided user ID, return user’s full

name.

func getUserFullName(ctx context.Context, userID string)  
(response string, err error) {

query := `  
select full_name

from library_user  
where user_id = $1`

return executeQueryWithStringResponse(ctx, query, userID)


}

Just a small reminder, all functions must be exported in order to  
use them in other packages. Our gRPC server is ready, now, we

only need a client.


8.2.3 gRPC client

In server book microservice project, we will create new sub-folder

called Here we will put everything related to gRPC communication  
with user microservice. Folder grpcserver will have one sub-folder,  
This folder will contain copy of ﬁle generated with protoc

compiler (ﬁle usergrpc.pb.go from user microservice). Without this  
ﬁle gRPC client does not know how to communicate with gRPC  
server.

We will start gRPC client from main() function. This is more

straight forward task than starting gRPC server. Because client  
doesn’t have to constantly listen and wait for request we do not  
need goroutine and barrier synchronization, gRPC clients will  
initiate communication when needed. Inside main() function we

will only have call of StartUserGrpcClient() function and error  
handling. We can put this code segment after database  
initialization.

log.Println("Starting User gRPC Client")  
err = grpcserver.StartUserGrpcClient()  
if err != nil {

log.Fatalf("Could not start User gRPC Client: %v\n", err)  
}

In conﬁguration we need only one parameter, address of gRPC  
server. Same address format (host:port) that we used for gRPC


server will be used for gRPC client. Our gRPC server will run on

port 8081 of our local machine, so we will put that address. We  
will add gRPC section inside bookservice.toml ﬁle.

# Grpc server conﬁguration  
[grpc]  
user_grpc_connection_string = ":8081"

We must provide function to read gRPC conﬁguration in conﬁg  
package. Inside grpc.go ﬁle in conﬁg folder we will export function  
This function will use functions from conﬁgmodule to read

user_grpc_connection_string from gRPC section of toml ﬁle.

var (  
// GetUserGRPCConnectionString returns user grpc connection  
string  
// from grpc section in toml ﬁle  
GetUserGRPCConnectionString = getUserGRPCConnectionString

)

func getUserGRPCConnectionString() string {  
return conﬁgmodule.GetConﬁgString(  
"grpc.user_grpc_connection_string")  
}

Inside grpcserver folder we will create new ﬁle usergrpcclient.go  
that will contain everything related to gRPC client. In order to use  
service deﬁned in proto ﬁle, we need client variable at the start of  
this ﬁle. Interface UserGrpcClient is generated by protoc compiler.


var userGrpcClient usergrpc.UserGrpcClient

We will place function StartUserGrpcClient() in usergrpcclient.go  
ﬁle. At the start of function, we read conﬁguration from toml ﬁle  
and call function WithInsecure() from grpc package. This function  
will return dial option that disables transport security for this  
client connection. We will use this option and address required  
from conﬁguration to create client connection with call of Dial()  
function from grpc package.

var (  
// StartUserGrpcClient starts gRPC client for user grpc  
StartUserGrpcClient = startUserGrpcClient  
)

func startUserGrpcClient() (err error) {  
address := conﬁg.GetUserGRPCConnectionString()

opts := grpc.WithInsecure()  
clientConn, err := grpc.Dial(address, opts)

if err != nil {  
return  
}  
userGrpcClient = usergrpc.NewUserGrpcClient(clientConn)  
return  
}


At the end of the function, we will pass connection as the  
parameter of NewUserGrpcClient() function (from code generated  
by protoc compiler) to initialize gRPC client. We will use this  
object to send request to gRPC server.

In same ﬁle, we will put functions that will use service described  
in proto ﬁle. All function will use same logic, create gRPC  
request, send it to server and unpack server response.

func AuthorizeUser(ctx context.Context, authorizationToken string)  
(userRole int64, err error) {  
request := &usergrpc.AuthorizeUserRequest{  
AuthorizationToken: authorizationToken,  
}

response, err := userGrpcClient.AuthorizeUser(ctx, request)

if err != nil {

return  
}  
userRole = response.UserRole  
return  
}

func GetUserID(ctx context.Context, authorizationToken string)  
(userID string, err error) {

request := &usergrpc.GetUserIDRequest{  
AuthorizationToken: authorizationToken  
},


response, err := userGrpcClient.GetUserID(ctx, request)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}  
userID = response.UserID

return  
}

func GetUserFullName(ctx context.Context, userID string)  
(fullName string, err error) {  
request := &usergrpc.GetUserFullNameRequest{UserID: userID}  
response, err := userGrpcClient.GetUserFullName(ctx, request)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

fullName = response.FullName  
return  
}

With this gRPC client functions, we can ﬁx problems that  
occurred during breaking of monolith application. In original code  
for our Handler Layer, we used AuthorizeUser() function from  
Core Layer. As we mentioned before this function is now part of  
user microservice. Now we can use AuthorizeUser() function from  
usergrpc package instead.


userRole, err := grpcserver.AuthorizeUser(ctx, request.Authorization)  
if err != nil {  
return nil, errormodule.ErrNotAuthenticated  
}

Original code of borrowOrReturnBook() function used GetUserID()  
that is now part of user microservice Data Layer. Here we will  
use GetUserID() function from usergrpc package.

userUID, err = grpcserver.GetUserID(ctx, token)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

Original code of getAllBooks() function from Core Layer contains

this code segment:

if userRole == values.UserRoleMember {

books, err = data.GetAllBooksForMember(ctx, searchTerm,  
rowOﬀset, rowLimit)

} else {  
books, err = data.GetAllBooksForLibrerian(ctx, searchTerm,

rowOﬀset, rowLimit)  
}

In original code, we performed table JOIN in Data Layer to return

names of users that borrowed a book (in GetAllBooksForLibreria()

function). User tables are not available from book microservice so  
we can use GetUserFullName() function from usergrpc package. In


order to keep code readable we will put call of Data Layer

function and retrieving of names in separate function.

Our if statement will now look like this:

if userRole == values.UserRoleMember {

books, err = data.GetAllBooksForMember(ctx, searchTerm,

rowOﬀset, rowLimit)  
} else {

books, err = getAllBooksLibrarian(ctx, searchTerm, rowOﬀset,  
rowLimit)

}

In getAllBooksLibrarian() function, we will call Data Layer function.

For each book that has an ID set, we will call GetUserFullName()

function from usergrpc package to get the full name of user.

func getAllBooksLibrarian(ctx context.Context, searchTerm string,

rowOﬀset,rowLimit int) (books []*data.BookInfoLibrarian, err error)  
{

books, err = data.GetAllBooksForLibrerian(ctx, searchTerm,

rowOﬀset, rowLimit)  
if err != nil {

return  
}

fullName := ""

for _, book := range books {  
if book.BorrowerID != "" {


fullName, err = grpcserver.GetUserFullName(

ctx, book.BorrowerID)  
if err != nil {

return  
}

book.Borrower = fullName  
}

}  
return

}

Now our microservices can communicate with each other. If we  
start both microservices and check API calls we will see that our

microservice architecture provides same functionality as original  
monolith application.


8.2.4 Development practices

Let us return to the example that we used to describe the

structure of gRPC message.

message PersonalInformation {  
string Name = 1;  
string Surname = 2;

string Address = 3;  
int64 ID = 4;  
}

We used generated ﬁle with protoc compiler and distributed that

ﬁle to our gRPC clients. After a while we decided that we do not  
need information about address. When we remove that ﬁled, our  
instinct will tell us to change tag of ID ﬁeld from 4 to 3. This is

actually not a good practice!

If we do not provide new version of generated ﬁle to some of  
gRPC clients, they will expect address string with tag 3, but they  
will receive integer ID. Clients with old version of generated ﬁle  
will no longer be able to communicate with gRPC server.

If we leave tags intact, gRPC server will not sent anything with  
tag 3, gRPC client will know how to handle that situation and  
they can still communicate, they will have compatible versions of

generated ﬁles.


For same reason, when we add new ﬁeld we should tag it with  
next number in sequence. If we removed address ﬁeld and add,

for example, phone number, we will use tag 5 for phone number.

message PersonalInformation {  
string Name = 1;  
string Surname = 2;  
int64 ID = 4;

string Phone = 5;  
}

Obviously, if we remove the largest tag, next time when we add  
new ﬁeld we will probably use removed tag. To avoid that  
situation we can put message in comment for us and other  
developers to use a “larger” tag.


8.3 RabbitMQ

Some communication does not require an immediate response.

We can have a simple report microservice for our library  
application. Someone will like to see how many books are  
borrowed each day. Report microservice can gather information

from book microservice once a day (after working hours, for  
example).

Book microservice can leave data in queue and continue to work  
undisturbed. Our report microservice can have a cron job that

will be triggered every day at the same time, for example at 8  
pm, collect and process data from queue.

Before we can start, we must download and install RabbitMQ. We  
can ﬁnd all versions and installation guides on It is good

practice to enable RabbitMQ dashboard so we can access our  
queues in web browser (all instructions can be found on oﬃcial  
site). Locally, dashboard will be available on localhost:15672 with  
a user named guest and default password of guest


Figure 8.3: RabbitMQ dashboard

In order to use RabbitMQ with Go programming language we  
must execute this command:

go get github.com/streadway/amqp

Now we have everything that we need to create RabbitMQ  
producers and consumers in Go. In our system book,  
microservice will produce messages, report microservice will

consume messages.


8.3.1 RabbitMQ producer

We will start RabbitMQ publisher inside main() function of user

microservice. We can put this code after starting of gRPC client.

log.Println("Starting RabbitMQ Publisher")  
err = rabbitmq.StartRabbitMQPublisher()  
if err != nil {

log.Fatalf("Could not start RabbitMQ Publisher: %v\n", err)  
}

We must prepare conﬁguration before we can write  
StartRabbitMQPublisher() function. We only need one conﬁguration

parameter, which represents RabbitMQ URL. Connection string  
usually has this form:

ampq://username:password@host:port/

We will run RabbitMQ on our local machine, so we will use

localhost as host and port 5672. We will use the same username  
and password that we use to access RabbitMQ dashboard  
(guest:guest).

We need to add RabbitMQ section inside bookservice.toml ﬁle.

# RabbitMQ conﬁguration


[rabbitmq]

connection_string = "amqp://guest:guest@localhost:5672/"

We must read RabbitMQ conﬁguration from toml ﬁle. Inside  
rabbitmq.go ﬁle in conﬁg folder we will export function This

function will use functions from conﬁgmodule to read  
connection_string from RabbitMQ section of toml ﬁle.

var (  
// GetRabbitMQConnectionString returns connection string from

// rabbitMQ section in toml ﬁle

GetRabbitMQConnectionString = getRabbitMQConnectionString  
)

func getRabbitMQConnectionString() string {  
return conﬁgmodule.GetConﬁgString("rabbitmq.connection_string")  
}

We will need new sub-folder, rabbitmq, in the server folder. Here,  
we can put everything related to RabbitMQ communication.  
Function StartRabbitMQPublisher() will be placed in  
rabbitmqpublisher.go ﬁle from rabbitmq folder.

var (

// StartRabbitMQPublisher starts rabbitMQ publisher  
StartRabbitMQPublisher = startRabbitMQPublisher  
)


func startRabbitMQPublisher() (err error) {  
// Function code  
return  
}

Outside function we will deﬁne two variables, they will be used

for RabbitMQ channel and queue.

var channel *amqp.Channel  
var queue amqp.Queue

At the beginning of StartRabbitMQPublisher() function, we will  
read conﬁguration from toml ﬁle and call Dial() function from  
amqp package. This function will return TCP Connection object.  
All errors that should be handled.

address := conﬁg.GetRabbitMQConnectionString()

conn, err := amqp.Dial(address)  
if err != nil {  
return

}

Method Channel() will open a unique, concurrent server channel  
to process messages. Method QueueDeclare() is indempodent  
method, queue will create if it does not already exists. This  
method accepts queue name and diﬀerent options. We will use  
“default” conﬁguration for reports.


channel, err = conn.Channel()  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

queue, err = channel.QueueDeclare("books", false, false, false,  
false, nil)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

Where we will use same part of the code that we used for HTTP  
and gRPC servers. Inside new goroutine, we will listen for system  
interrupt signal and gracefully shut down RabbitMQ publisher  
interrupt occurs. When interrupt occurs connection and channel  
will be closed.

go func() {  
// Listen to operating system's interrupt signal  
interrupt := make(chan os.Signal, 1)  
signal.Notify(interrupt, os.Interrupt, syscall.SIGTERM)  
<-interrupt

// Gracefully shut down the publisher when it happens

conn.Close()  
channel.Close()  
}()


Inside rabitmqpublisher ﬁle, we will have one more function, This  
function will create new message and send it to queue.

var (  
// SendMessage will send message to queue  
SendMessage = sendMessage  
)

We will use the Publishing struct from amqp package. Each  
message will have Content Type (plain text in our case) and Body.  
All reports can be created with only one information, ID of  
borrowed book, so we will store that information inside message  
body.

Method Publish() will be used to send message to queue. We will  
provide queue name and message as the second and ﬁfth  
parameter, other parameters are used to handle situations when  
message is not delivered. With conﬁguration all undelivered  
messages will be lost. For our needs, this is enough so we will  
use it.

func sendMessage(bookID string) (err error) {  
message := amqp.Publishing{  
ContentType: "text/plain",  
Body: []byte(bookID),  
}

err = channel.Publish("", queue.Name, false, false, message)  
if err != nil {  
return


}

return  
}

Now we just need to call SendMessage() function every time when  
someone borrow a book. In borrowOrReturnBook() function from  
core package at the end of if statement where we check current  
status of book we will add call of SendMessage() function from  
rabbitmq package.

if status == values.BookStatusAvailable {  
userUID, err = grpcserver.GetUserID(ctx, token)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

newStatus = values.BookStatusBorrowed

err = rabbitmq.SendMessage(request.BookID)  
if err != nil {

// Just log error, no rollback  
log.Fatalf("Failed to send message: %v\n", err)

err = nil  
}

}

If, for some reasons, sending message to queue fails, there is no  
need to return error and rollback entire transaction. This data is


not sensitive and system will work even if message is not sent.

For that reason we will just log potential error and move on.

Now when we now how to publish messages we should learn  
how to consume messages from queue.


8.3.2 Report microservice overview

Before we create RabbitMQ Consumer we will see overview of our

report microservice. With knowledge collected in previous chapters  
we will have all information required to create whole microservice.  
We will see only speciﬁc details that we have not seen in other

microservices.

Report microservice will have two APIs:

GET /api/report/daily?startDate={startDate}&endDate={endDate}  
GET /api/report/book

The ﬁrst API will return how many books have been borrowed on  
each day from date range. The second API will return how many  
times single book has been borrowed. Only user with role  
Liberian can call Report API, so we will use gRPC to Authenticate  
user (we saw earlier in this chapter how to do this).

Note: Dates must be provided in speciﬁc format: YYYY-MM-DD  
(four digits for year, two digits for month and two digits for day).

Example: 1989-05-27 (May 27, 1989)

As we can see our microservice will accept only HTTP GET  
methods. We can add HTTP method check in HTTP Layer or at


the start of Handler Layer and reject all other HTTP methods.

if request.Method != http.MethodGet {

return nil, errormodule.ErrInvalidAPICall  
}

For date range (start and end date) we will use Date struct from  
civil package. This Date contains only date part of timestamp  
(year, month, and day). Inside Handler Layer, for our Daily Report  
API, we will parse parameters from URI. If one of the dates is  
not provided we should return ErrBadRequest from

func getParams(uri *url.URL) (startDate, endDate civil.Date, err  
error) {  
params := uri.Query()  
param, ok := params["startDate"]  
if ok {  
startDate, err = civil.ParseDate(param[0])

if err != nil {  
return  
}  
} else {  
err = errormodule.ErrBadRequest  
return  
}

param, ok = params["endDate"]

if ok {  
endDate, err = civil.ParseDate(param[0])  
if err != nil {


return  
}  
} else {  
err = errormodule.ErrBadRequest  
return  
}

return  
}

When we work with dates, we should try to validate them. Start  
date and end date cannot be in the future. Also, start date must  
be before end date. If all conditions are met, our date range is  
valid and we can proceed to Data Layer.

today := civil.DateOf(time.Now())

if startDate.After(today) {  
cause := "Invalid value for start date"  
err = errormodule.NewError(cause,  
errormodule.ErrorCodeValidation, errormodule.ErrBadRequest, err)  
return  
}

if endDate.After(today) {  
cause := "Invalid value for end date"  
err = errormodule.NewError(cause,  
errormodule.ErrorCodeValidation, errormodule.ErrBadRequest, err)

return


}

if startDate.After(endDate) {  
cause := "Start date must be before end date"  
err = errormodule.NewError(cause,  
errormodule.ErrorCodeValidation, errormodule.ErrBadRequest, err)  
return  
}

In order to use our start date and end date in database queries  
we should “convert” them from civil.Date to Fortunately package  
civil provide us appropriate method. All timestamps should be in  
UTC timezone so we will provide it as method parameter.

start := startDate.In(time.UTC)  
end := endDate.In(time.UTC)

Report microservice will have simple database with only one table  
Table will have only three columns:

unique identiﬁer for each row (primary key).

ID of borrowed book.

timestamp when book has been borrowed:


Figure 8.4: Report microservice database

For each report type, we will have speciﬁc struct that will be used  
for response. Both API calls will return array of structures. Every  
day of daily report will be represented with struct that contains  
date (in text form, without time part) and number of books  
borrowed on that day. Each book from books report will be  
represented with struct that contains two ﬁelds, bookID and how  
many times that book has been borrowed.

// DailyReportData is struct used in daily report  
type DailyReportData struct {  
ReportDate string  
BookCount int  
}

// BookReportData is struct used in book report  
type BookReportData struct {  
BookID string  
BookCount int  
}


We will use two functions from Data Layer get report data from  
database. First function getDailyReport() will execute simple query  
that will group all rows by date and count number of books  
borrowed on that day. In order to get only date part from  
timestamp, we must convert timestamp to date and that to text  
in our query. If we left text part oﬀ our row reader will not know  
how to parse that data so we must convert it to text.

func getDailyReport(ctx context.Context, startDate, endDate  
time.Time)  
(response []*DailyReportData, err error) {

dbRunner := ctx.Value(values.ContextKeyDbRunner)  
.(databasemodule.Runner)  
query := `  
select  
report_date::timestamp::date::text as "ReportDate", count(*) as  
"BookCount"  
from report  
where report_date >= $1 and report_date <= $2

group by report_date::timestamp::date::text`

rows, err := dbRunner.Query(ctx, query, startDate, endDate)  
if err != nil {

return  
}

defer rows.Close()


rr, err := databasemodule.GetRowReader(rows)  
if err != nil {

return  
}

response = make([]*DailyReportData, 0)

for rr.ScanNext() {  
report := &DailyReportData{}

rr.ReadAllToStruct(report)  
response = append(response, report)

}  
err = rr.Error()

return  
}

Our second function getBooksReport() will execute a little bit

simpler query. This query will group all rows by bookID and count  
how many time each book has been borrowed.

func getBooksReport(ctx context.Context) (response

[]*BookReportData, err error) {  
dbRunner := ctx.Value(values.ContextKeyDbRunner)

.(databasemodule.Runner)  
query := `

select  
book_id as "BookID",

count(*) as "BookCount"  
from report

group by book_id`  
rows, err := dbRunner.Query(ctx, query)

if err != nil {


return

}

defer rows.Close()  
rr, err := databasemodule.GetRowReader(rows)

if err != nil {  
return

}  
response = make([]*BookReportData, 0)

for rr.ScanNext() {  
report := &BookReportData{}

rr.ReadAllToStruct(report)

response = append(response, report)  
}

err = rr.Error()  
return

}

Now when we are familiar with our report microservice we can

create RabbitMQ Consumer.


8.3.3 RabbitMQ Consumer

As we are already accustomed to, we will start from main()

function of our report microservice. We can put this part of code  
before or after starting of HTTP server:

go func() {  
defer wg.Done()

log.Println("Starting RabbitMQ Consumer")

err = rabbitmq.StartRabbitMQConsumer()  
if err != nil {  
log.Fatalf("Could not start RabbitMQ Consumer: %v\n", err)

}

log.Println("RabbitMQ Consumer gracefully terminated")  
}()

Now we have two goroutines thaw will be synchronized on barrier

so we must increase value of WaitGroup counter to 2.

wg.Add(2)

Conﬁguration part is same as conﬁguration of RabbitMQ  
Publisher, because publisher and consumer will use same queue.


We just need to provide function (in conf package) to read

connection string from toml ﬁle.

var (  
// GetRabbitMQConnectionString returns connection string  
// from rabbitMQ section in toml ﬁle  
GetRabbitMQConnectionString = getRabbitMQConnectionString  
)

func getRabbitMQConnectionString() string {

return conﬁgmodule.GetConﬁgString("rabbitmq.connection_string")

}

Everything related to RabbitMQ consumer will be placed into  
folder sub-folder of server folder. Function  
StartRabbitMQConsumer() will be similar with function  
StartRabbitMQPublisher() from book microservice.

At the start of the function, we will collect conﬁguration, create  
TCP connection and open server channel to process messages. At  
the end of the function, we will call local function

func startRabbitMQConsumer() (err error) {  
address := conﬁg.GetRabbitMQConnectionString()

conn, err := amqp.Dial(address)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}


channel, err = conn.Channel()  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

queue, err = channel.QueueDeclare("books", false, false, false,  
false, nil)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

consumeMessages()  
return  
}

Function consumeMessages() can be described as After  
initialization same job will be executed in scheduled time. To  
archive this we will use Tickers from time package. Ticker  
represents channel that will periodically send values until  
something stops it.

At the start of the function we must prepare our context and put  
database runner in it.

ctx := context.Background()  
ctx = dbserver.PrepareDbRunner(ctx)


Ticker will send values periodically, but we want it to be at the  
same time every day. Let us assume that out library works till 5  
pm. We can set ﬁrst execution at 5 pm on a day when  
microservice is started and repeated same job in 24h periods.

Method Truncate() will round down current time to a multiple of  
provided duration. In our case duration is 24h (1 day). If current  
time is 2020-08-25 45:32:15, Truncate() method will return 2020-08-  
25 00:00:00. Our job will be triggered at 5 pm, so we must add  
17 h. If “truncated” time is passed for current day we will add 24  
h.

// Position the ﬁrst execution  
ﬁrst := time.Now().Truncate(time.Hour * 24).Add(time.Hour * 17)  
if ﬁrst.Before(time.Now()) {  
ﬁrst = ﬁrst.Add(time.Hour * 24)  
}

Function After() from time package will wait for the duration to  
elapse and then sends the current time to channel. In other  
words, when time for ﬁrst execution arrives, we will receive data  
on channel and start our job. We will create Ticker object with nil  
channel, (receiving from nil channel blocks forever), proper  
channel will be set after ﬁrst execution.

ﬁrstC := time.After(ﬁrst.Sub(time.Now()))  
ticker := &time.Ticker{C: nil}


We can use same that we used to stop HTTP Server to stop  
Ticker. When interrupt occurs Ticker be stopped.

interrupt := make(chan os.Signal, 1)  
signal.Notify(interrupt, os.Interrupt, syscall.SIGTERM)

Select statement will be blocked until variables can be received  
from one of the channels. If we receive variable from channel  
ﬁrstC (ﬁrst execution), we will set execution period for Ticker and  
consume messages from queue. After ﬁrst execution we will only  
receive variables through Ticker’s channel. In that case we will  
consume messages and wait for next variable. In case when  
interrupt occurs we will stop our Ticker and gracefully shut down  
publisher.

select {  
case <-ﬁrstC:  
ticker = time.NewTicker(time.Hour * 24)  
err = getMessages(ctx)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

case <-ticker.C:  
err = getMessages(ctx)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

case <-interrupt:


ticker.Stop()  
return  
}

Function getMessages() will use method Consume() to receive  
messages from queue. For each message, Data Layer function

CreateReport() will store received data to database.

func getMessages(ctx context.Context) (err error) {  
messages, err := channel.Consume(queue.Name, "", true, false,  
false, false, nil)  
if err != nil {  
return  
}

for message := range messages {  
err = data.CreateReport(ctx, string(message.Body))  
if err != nil {

return  
}

}  
return

}

Inside Data Layer, function CreateReport() will execute simple query  
to store bookID received from queue. For the date when book is

borrowed current time will be set (default value for report_date  
ﬁeld must be set to


Our system is complete. If we want we can use RabbitMQ to

exchange all kinds of diﬀerent information between our  
microservices.


Conclusion

We are familiar with diﬀerent methods of inter-service

communication and we should be able to implement them into  
our applications. Some methods are suitable for certain group of  
communication problems than others. If we are not sure what

method to use we should choose one that we are most  
comfortable with.

In some situation, we can create test applications and tries  
diﬀerent methods for same communication functionality. That we

can measure performance or do diﬀerent kind of metrics to select  
best method.

We have to deal with one more thing, how to deploy and scale  
our application.


Points to remember

Create separate API for client-side applications and for inter-

service communication.

Choose communication method that best suits your needs.

Always create “cron jobs” for tasks that will be repeated  
periodically.
