9.1 Microservice deployment

We have completed development of our microservice architecture

for library system. If we want someone to use our system we  
must it in some place where client applications can access it.  
Our local machine is good for testing, but it is not the best

place for hosting a web server.

There is a couple of reason why we should not use our local  
machine for hosting. In order to access our microservices via  
HTTP, we should expose our IP address and our ports to world,

and this is not a good idea. Also, hardware conﬁguration of our  
local machine is not suitable for this type of work. Maybe our  
CPU and memory are not enough to support big amount of  
HTTP Requests and data processing.

Our microservice application should be available all the time, but  
when we turn oﬀ computer and we will have to do it sometime  
(as a result of power outage or hardware malfunction). When our  
machine is down, service is not available and we can be in a  
problem.

The best solution is to ﬁnd some dedicated hosting service and  
deploy our application there. Usually hosting is not free and price

depends on hardware conﬁguration that we from service provider.


When we choose where to host our application we can deploy it

to hosting server. By deﬁnition, deployment is set of activities that  
make system available for use. Most people think that only  
process of putting application to host server is deployment, but  
deployment includes couple additional activities.

Usual activities related to deployment are:

First activity which comes after development. When development  
is ﬁnished, and all tests are passed and our application is stable  
we can expose it to world. Version chosen for release is often

called

Creating form of application that can be executed manually or  
automatically. Usually, we use some script, command, or service  
to create executable ﬁle. For our example, go build command will  
create executable ﬁle.

Starting executable component for the ﬁrst time.

Shooting down executable component.

Removal of the system that is no longer in use.

Replacement of executable component with newer version of same  
component.


Version Allows user to track and ﬁnd updates for software. When  
we visited websites to download some software solution, usually,  
on download page we can ﬁnd links to download newest and  
oldest version of that software.

We know what deployment process is and how to host  
application. Now we may run into a problem. We created  
executable ﬁles for Windows, but our hosting provider provides  
only Linux machines. Yes, we can rebuild our application, but  
now we should perform tests and all required pre-deployment  
actions again. Fortunately, modern deployment solutions oﬀer us  
practical way to handle this problem. In this chapter, we will see,  
with more details, some of the most popular and most used  
solutions.


9.2 Scaling microservice

In microservice architecture, it is common that one of the

microservices handle more job than others (for example all users  
will call authentication microservice) and soon that microservice  
will become bottleneck of our system. This problem can be

solved easily, we should deploy couple of instances of  
microservice.

Scalability can be described as a property of a system to handle  
increasing amount of work by adding new resources. In our case,

if we add new instance of microservice we will scale our system  
and increase system performance.

If we scale our microservice, client applications and end-users will  
not notice any diﬀerence. With unscaled microservice, all HTTP

Requests will directly hit microservice Now we scale our  
microservice by adding one more instance of that microservice,  
but now we have two diﬀerent hosting addresses, so each  
instance will have diﬀerent URI. How client-side applications will  
now which instance is more unburdened?

We can put component called load balancer as entry point for  
our scaled microservice. Load balancer will use same URI as

original unscaled microservice. Load balancer will use some  
balancing algorithm to distribute HTTP Request to microservice  
instances as evenly as possible


Figure 9.1: Unscaled (a) and scaled (b) microservice

Scaling of microservice carries certain risks. If microservice has a  
database, we must ensure consistency of a data. If we create two  
instances of microservices both databases must contain same  
data. If something changes data in database of the ﬁrst instance,  
that change should be propagated to all instances. Modern  
scaling solutions can provide us with tools that can help us to  
ensure that our system contains consistent data.

We can use some type of measurement to deﬁne type of scaling.  
In our example, we scaled our microservice to handle more HTTP  
Request. This type of scalability is often referred as Load  
scalability, we scale up our microservice to handle heavier loads.


Other popular scalability types are:

Administrative System will be scaled to allow increasing number  
of users to access system. Scaling of authentication microservice  
is a good example for this type of scalability.

Functional System will be scaled to add new functionality without  
inﬂuencing current activities. If we deployed our library system  
without report microservice and we add it later, this can be

considered as functional scalability.

Geographical System can scale to increase eﬀectiveness based on  
geographical location. Let us assume that we have our system  
deployed on host service in Paris. After analysis, we found out  
that 50% of our users are from South Korea. We can host one  
more instance of our system in Seoul. Now our Korean users can  
access our system faster and more eﬃciently.

In this chapter, we mostly used term scaling to denote scale up  
of system. It is important to note that we can also scale down  
our system. Some of online game developers discovered that their  
servers are overloaded during weekends. They ﬁxed that problem  
with simple scaling trick. They decided to scale up system on

Friday and to scale down system on Monday.

Now, when we are familiar with concept of scaling we can see  
some modern solutions and tools.


9.3 Docker

Docker is a platform that uses virtualization to deliver software in

software units with code and all dependencies packaged inside  
called containers. We should always Dockerize our application.  
Dockized application is application conﬁgured to run in a

container.

Before we can move ahead we must describe diﬀerences between  
Docker and Virtual Machine, people often think that these terms  
represent same thing, but that is not correct.

In order to use Virtual Machine, on our host server we must

have Hypervisor, software or hardware designed to create and run  
virtual machines. Then we need to install Operating System on  
created Virtual Machine. Now we can install our application to

Virtual Machine

With Docker, situation is a little bit simpler. Our host servers  
must contain Operating System with Docker installed. We can  
create and run Docker containers on that machine. Inside docker  
container, we have everything that we need to run our application  
We are not dependent on host’s server Operating System, we can  
create our application to run on wherever we want, we can host

Linux containers on Windows OS, we can even run Docker  
container inside Virtual Machine!!!:


Figure 9.2: Virtual Machine and Docker Application (App), Operating

System (OS), Virtual Machine (VM), Docker Container (DC)

There is a how to get from the code to the Docker Container.  
When we ﬁnish development of our code, next step is to create  
Docker Image. Docker Image represents ﬁle, used to executed  
code in Docker Container. To create Docker Image From our code,

we use conﬁguration ﬁle which contains information on how to  
create Docker Image called Dockerﬁle.

With ready Docker Image we can put it on Docker Registry,  
server-side application used to store and distribute Docker  
Images. Last step in Docker ﬂow is creation and launch of  
Docker Container.

Essentially, we can observe Docker Images as stopped Docker  
Container and vice versa, Docker Container is running Docker  
Image.


There are two types of Docker Containers:

Stateless Does not read or save state. If something stops  
container, next time when container is started, there is no way to  
state before stopping. Application will start execution from  
beginning.

Stateful Read and save state. If something stops container, on  
next start container will state and continue application execution  
from place where it interrupted.

Before we can see how to Dockerize our microservices, we should  
see some basic Docker commands.


9.3.1 Basics of Docker

We can download Docker from the oﬃcial website For Windows

and Mac OS we will install tool called Docker Desktop, for Linux  
we just need to install Linux Engine. On oﬃcial website, we can  
ﬁnd guide how to install Docker for all Operating Systems.

After successful installation, we can execute version command to

check if everything go well.

docker version

If Docker is correctly installed, command should provide input  
similar to this:

Client:  
Version: 19.03.11  
API version: 1.40  
Go version: go1.13.12

Git commit: dd360c7  
Built: Mon Jun 8 20:23:26 2020  
OS/Arch: linux/amd64  
Experimental: false

With info command, we can get host information.


docker info

As we mentioned before, Docker Images are usually stored on

Docker Registry. In order to use images, we can pull them from  
registry and store it locally. We need to provide name of the  
desired image. Image name is usually two-parter consists of  
registry name and image version (tag).

docker pull image_name

We can list all locally stored images with image command and  
remove images that we do not need any more with remove  
command.

docker images  
docker rmi image_name

When we have prepared Dockerﬁle for our application we can use  
it to build Docker Image. Build command has two interesting  
options. With -f option, we can provide path to Dockerﬁle and  
with option -t we can provide Docker Registry where image will  
be stored and tag.

docker build -f path_to_dockerﬁle -t repository_name:tag

With Docker Image we can create and run Docker Container with

run command.


docker run image_name

Command ps will show us list of all running containers. If we  
add option -a to command we will see all containers that are  
currently running and all containers which were created and  
started.

docker ps  
docker ps -a

Created container can be stopped, started and removed when we  
no longer need it. For each command we must provide container  
name as parameter.

docker stop container_name  
docker start container_name  
docker rm container_name

Now when we are familiar with basics of Docker we can use that  
knowledge to Dockerize our microservices.


9.3.2 Microservices Dockerization

In order to Dockerize our application, we will create Docker ﬁle

for all of our microservices. All of our Dockerﬁles will be similar  
so we will cover Docker ﬁle for user microservice.

If we exclude comments, Docker ﬁle must start with FROM  
instruction. This instruction will specify parent image from which

we will build our image. Most of the images are stored on  
Docker Hub, oﬃcial Docker Registry. For our image we will use  
oﬃcial Go programming language based on Alpine Linux. Alpine

Linux is a security-oriented, lightweight Linux distribution so it is  
convenient for small size images with basic functionalities.

# Start from golang alpine base image  
FROM golang:alpine

Note: If we want to create base image, FROM instruction will not

have speciﬁed image, we will use word scratch instead.

We can assume that our code is completely on pour machine, it  
is not hosted to any online Git Repository, so we must copy our  
gomodules to our container in order to build our Go application.  
Instruction WORKDIR will create new working directory inside  
container and instruction COPY will copy code from our machine  
to container.


# Set the Current Working Directory inside the container

WORKDIR /gomodules  
# Copy the gomodules source code to the Working Directory  
# inside the container  
COPY gomodules/ .

Now, we can copy source code oﬀ user microservice with same  
set of instructions.

# Set the Current Working Directory inside the container  
WORKDIR /user-ms

# Copy the source code to the Working Directory inside the  
container COPY user-ms/ .

Instruction RUN will execute any command and commit the  
results. We use Apline Linux, so our RUN instruction can execute  
Linux commands. We will use it to download all dependencies  
and build executable ﬁle for our microservice.

# Download all dependencies.  
# Dependencies will be cached if the go.mod and go.sum  
# ﬁles are not changed  
RUN go mod download

# Build the Go app  
RUN go build -o userservice userservice.go

We can create new working directory and put executable and  
conﬁgurations ﬁle inside. We do not need source code any more


so we can delete directories that contain it.

# Set the Current Working Directory inside the container  
# for builded application  
WORKDIR /app

# Copy binary from user-ms and conﬁguration to main folder  
RUN cp /user-ms/userservice .  
RUN cp /user-ms/userservice.toml .

# Delete source code and gomodules  
RUN rm -r /gomodules  
RUN rm -r /user-ms

User Microservice will listen on ports 8080 and 8081 for HTTP  
and gRPC requests. Instruction EXPOSE will inform Docker that  
container listens on the speciﬁed network ports.

# Export necessary ports  
EXPOSE 8080 8081

Instruction CMD speciﬁes what command will be run inside the  
container. In our case, we want to start our microservice, so we  
will put executable ﬁle in our instruction. Only one CMD  
instruction should be in Dockerﬁle. If we have more than one,  
only the last one will take eﬀect.

# Command to run when starting the container  
CMD ["/app/userservice"]


Now when our Dockerﬁle is completed we can use docker build  
command to create new image. We will host our Docker Images  
on our local machine instead of online Docker Registry, so we  
can just provide image name, for repository name. We will use  
value 1.0.0 for initial tag and increase it for newer versions.

docker build -f user-ms/Dockerﬁle . -t serservice:1.0.0

If build is successful, we should see our images when we execute  
docker images command.

We can still use databases that we created in previous chapters,  
we just need to assign host value to network parameter of docker  
run command in order to access local machine databases from  
docker container. Parameter -p will be used to publish container’s  
ports to host. Without this parameter client application cannot  
access our container. Docker image will be the last parameter of  
docker run command.

docker run –network=host -p 8080:8080 -p 8081:8081  
userservice:1.0.0

If everything passed well, we should repeat same job for other  
microservices. Managing all container can become serious  
problem. Fortunately, there is an eﬃcient solution for container  
management problem.


9.4 Kubernetes

Kubernetes (commonly stylized as k8s) is system for orchestration

of containers. It can be used for application deployment, scaling  
and container management. Originally, Kubernetes is designed by  
Google now a days by Cloud Native Computing Foundation. It

supports most of the container solutions but most often is used  
in combination with Docker.

Kubernetes is wide and complex subject, one section of a chapter  
is not big enough to cover all topics. Here we will cover only

basics necessary to cover topics of this chapter, deployment and  
scaling. Here are some basics Kubernetes terms:

Physical or virtual working machine that run containerized  
applications.

Set of nodes. We must have at least one node in cluster.

Virtual cluster. Multiple virtual clusters can be hosted on same  
physical cluster.

Smallest and simplest object that run containers. Usually, one  
pod runs one container. Pods are managed by deployment.


Set of replica pods that run the same application. Deployment

will use RS (ReplicaSet) to ensure that conﬁgured number of  
pods are running. Pod can be created or deleted to maintain  
speciﬁed number of RS members.

Object that menages replicated application running in cluster.  
Each replica is represented by pod distributed among nodes of a  
cluster. Typically, deployment manages pods without local state  
(stateless). For stateful pods we should use StatefulSet.

Abstract used to expose set of pods (deployment) as network

application. Without service our application will not be accessible.

Now, when we are familiar with basic concepts of Kubernetes we  
should see some basic Kubernetes commands.


9.4.1 Basics of Kubernetes

We can use Kubernetes on our local machine, but ﬁrst we must

install couple tools. Minikube is a tool that allows us to run  
Kubernetes locally. Minikube will create learning environment, one  
cluster with one node. All guides for installation of Minikube are

available on oﬃcial Kubernetes website

In order to run command on Kubernetes cluster, we must install  
special tool, Kubectl. With Kubectl we can deploy applications and  
manage Kubernetes resources (pods, service, …) and view logs.

When we install all required tools we can check it with version

command for both tools. If everything passed according to plan,  
both command will return text with printed version.

minikube version  
kubectl version

If both tools are properly installed, we can start Minikube and  
start executing Kubectl commands on our Kubernetes cluster.

minikube start

Note: In order to use local Docker images, minikube must use  
same Docker deamon as our local Docker. On Linux system


command eval($minikube docker-env) should ﬁx problem.

On Windows situation is a little bit complicated. This set of

commands should ﬁx problem:

minikube start -vm-driver=hyperv  
minikube docker-env  
&minikube -p minikube docker-env | Invoke-Expression

With get command, we can list Kubernetes resources. After a get  
we should specify which resource we want to list. Here are some  
examples (rs is shorten for ReplicaSet):

kubectl get nodes  
kubectl get pods  
kubectl get rs  
kubectl get deployments  
kubectl get services

Describe command will display detailed state of one or more  
resources. If we provide resource name state will be displayed for  
speciﬁed resource in other case state for all resources of speciﬁed

type will be displayed. In other words, resource name is optional  
parameter.

kubectl describe nodes node_name  
kubectl describe pods pod_name  
kubectl describe deployments deployment_name  
kubectl describe service service_name


Create command is used to create Kubernetes resource. Here, we  
will explain command for deployment creation. We have two  
options, we can specify type of resource (deployment in our case)  
and pass deployment name and Docker image as parameters or  
we can specify everything that we need in ﬁle and use create

command with -f parameter.

kubectl create deployment deployment_name image  
kubectl create -f ﬁle_path

It is always useful to check logs. On Kubernetes all containers are  
run inside pod, so we will use pod name to get logs from  
desired container.

kubectl logs pod_name

Opposite of create command is delete command. We have three  
options for deletion. We can delete only speciﬁc resource, all  
resources of same type or we can use same ﬁle that we used for  
creation to delete resource. Here are examples oﬀ all delete

options for service.

kubectl delete service -l name=service_name  
kubectl delete service --all  
kubectl delete service -f ﬁle_path

We mentioned that in order to access our application from  
outside we must expose our deployment. Command expose will


expose our deployment and essentially create service. We must  
provide deployment name, service type and ports to command.

kubectl expose deployment deployment_name –type=service_type --  
port:port

In Kubernetes, we have couple of service types:

Default type, service will be reachable only from within a cluster.

Expose all cluster nodes on same port. Service can be reachable  
outside cluster with node IP and port.

Creates load balancer and assign ﬁxed IP to the service.

Service will be exposed with arbitrary name.

We can also scale our deployment with scale command. We  
should provide the deployment name and new number of replicas  
as parameters. If new number of replicas is greater than previous,  
we will scale up our deployment, if new number of replicas is  
less than previous we will scale down our deployment.

kubectl scale deployment deployment_name --  
replicas=number_of_replicas

If work from command line is not something that we prefer,  
Minikube provides us with graphical environment where we can


manage our Kubernetes resources Minikube Dashboard will be  
opened in our web browser after we execute dashboard  
command:

minikube dashboard

Figure 9.3: Minikube dashboard

When we ﬁnish work with our Kubernetes cluster we can stop  
Minikube. Optionally, we can delete our Kubernetes cluster with  
delete command, but be warned, this can also delete all locally  
stored Docker images created during running of Minikube.

minikube stop  
minikube delete

Now when we are familiar with basic command we can deploy  
our microservices with Kubernetes.


9.4.2 Deploying microservices with Kubernetes

Before we can deploy our microservices we must adjust database

and RabbitMQ instances that we run locally. Without, this  
adjustment we will not be able to access database and queue  
from Kubernetes cluster.

First, we can change setup for our database. Kubernetes cluster

will be run on IP address diﬀerent from our internal IP address.  
We cannot access database with localhost as host address,  
because Kubernetes cluster and our machine do not belong to

same network.

We must change database connection string and use internal IP  
address of our computer (we can ﬁnd it on Network Settings).  
Our conﬁguration ﬁles will be used inside Docker container so we

must apply changes before we build Docker image. We must  
change conﬁguration ﬁles for all of our microservices.

connection_string = "host=192.168.0.17 port=5432 user=postgres   
password=postgres dbname=book_db sslmode=disable"

There is a chance that our microservice still will not be able to  
access our local database. Reason for that can be that our  
database is not set up to accept external access requests. This  
problem is easily solvable. Inside PostgreSQL conﬁguration ﬁle


postgresql.conf (ﬁle can be found using search functionality on all

Operating Systems) we must locate line:

listen_addresses = 'localhost'

We replace this line with:

listen_addresses = '*'

At the end of PostgreSQL conﬁguration ﬁle we should add these  
lines:

host all all 0.0.0.0/0 md5  
host all all ::/0 md5

When we restart our PostgreSQL server changes will be  
propagated and our database will accept remote connections.

We can connect to RabbitMQ with guest user only from  
localhost. In order to connect from Kubernetes cluster we must  
create new user. This can easily be done on RabbitMQ  
Dashboard inside Admin section (we must be logged as guests).  
We just need to ﬁll username and password inputs and click on  
button.

In our case, we will use micro/microcredentials. Also we should  
replace localhost with internal IP address for RabbitMQ host


address. RabbitMQ connection string will now look similar to  
this:

connection_string = "amqp://micro:micro@192.168.0.17:5672/"

Now, we will create yaml conﬁguration ﬁle that will be used to  
create deployment. We must repeat this task for all of our  
microservices. Here, we will discuss only about deployment yaml  
ﬁle for user microservice.

apiVersion: apps/v1  
kind: Deployment  
metadata:  
name: user-ms  
spec:  
selector:  
matchLabels:  
app: user-ms  
replicas: 1  
template:  
metadata:

labels:  
app: user-ms  
spec:  
containers:  
\- image: userservice:1.0.0  
name: user-ms  
imagePullPolicy: Never  
ports:


\- containerPort: 8080

\- containerPort: 8081

Inside yaml ﬁle we provide some basic object information like  
name (user-ms in our case) and speciﬁcation of desired object  
state. For apiVersion we will always use apps/v1 except in some

older Kubernetes versions where we will use Kind will deﬁne type  
of object (deployment in our case).

Under spec we will deﬁne state of our object, for example, how  
many replicas we will have (for now one is enough, we will  
change that when we talk about scaling), which Docker image to  
use and which containers ports will be exposed.

In our case, we set image pull policy to Never. We use locally  
stored Docker images, without this line Kubectl will try to pull  
image from Docker Registry. Our images are not available on  
Docker Registry and our Deployment will fail. If we use image  
from Docker Registry we should set image pull policy to Always  
to ensure that we will always use the latest version of image.

With completed conﬁguration yaml ﬁle we can use Kubectl create  
command to create our deployment.

kubectl create -f user-ms/kubernetes/user-ms-deployment.yaml

If our deployment was successful, everything should be when we  
take a look on Minikube Dashboard When we create deployments


for all microservices, next step is to expose them to receive  
external requests.


9.4.3 Exposing Kubernetes deployment

As we mentioned, our microservices can receive external request

only if there are exposed. Creation of Kubernetes service will  
expose our deployments to outside world. We will use same  
approach that we used for deployment, create command and

yaml conﬁguration ﬁle.

apiVersion: v1  
kind: Service  
metadata:

name: user-ms-service  
spec:  
selector:  
app: user-ms

type: LoadBalancer  
ports:  
\- name: http  
protocol: TCP  
port: 8080

targetPort: 8080  
\- name: grpc  
protocol: TCP  
port: 8081  
targetPort: 8081

Here, we will use Service for object type and deﬁne service name  
(user-ms-service). All pods with label user-ms will belong to


service. Out of all microservices will use port 8080 for HTTP

Request and port 8081 for gRPC requests.

Note: Our system will work only if have conﬁgured in toml ﬁle

port 8080 for HTTP Server and port 8081 for gRPC Server.

For service type we will use LoadBalancer because it is most  
similar with scenario that we described when we discusses about  
scaling of microservices. All replicas will be exposed on same  
address and local Load Balancer will deliver requests to pods.  
Now we can use create command and create our service.

kubectl create -f user-ms/kubernetes/user-ms-service.yaml

Here we have small problem, Minikube does not support  
LoadBalancer by default. If we create LoadBalancer service, service  
will be in pending state forever. To use this type in we should  
execute tunnel command in separate terminal.

minikube tunnel

After starting of tunnel on our Kubernetes Dashboard we should  
have three running services with exposed external addresses that  
we can use for API calls in Postman


Figure 9.4: Services on Minikube dashboard

For inter-service communication, inside same namespace, we can  
use service name. We should put user service name in  
conﬁguration for book and report microservice. Without that our  
system will not work properly.

user_grpc_connection_string = "user-ms-service:8081"

Now we know how to run our microservices inside Kubernetes  
cluster. We have only one more topic to cover, how to scale our  
microservices.


9.4.4 Scaling Kubernetes deployment

We created deployment with only one replica for all microservices.

After some amount of time, it turned out that one instance of  
user microservice is not enough. Increasing number of HTTP  
Request requires from us to scale up our microservice. We will

use scale command to scale up our microservice to ﬁve  
instances.

kubectl scale deployment user-ms --replicas=5

If we now take a look on Minikube Dashboard, we should see  
ﬁve pods for user-ms deployment. After some performance test,

we ﬁnd out that there is no need for ﬁve instances of user  
microservice, three is enough. Same command can be used for  
scaling down.

kubectl scale deployment user-ms –replicas=3

Now we should see three user-ms pods on Minikube Dashboard.

Kubernetes oﬀers one quite useful concept, horizontal autoscaling.  
Horizontal autoscaler will automatically scale pods based on  
some metrics (CPU utilization, memory utilization, number of  
requests, and so on). We can also specify minimum and  
maximum number of replicas for our deployment.


Autoscaling algorithm will calculate number of desired replicas  
based on this simple formula:

DR = CR * (mc/mt)

where DR represents desired number of replicas, CR current

number of replicas, mc current value of the metrics and mt  
represents targeted value of the metrics.

Here is an example of autoscaler that will use CPU utilization as  
metrics. In our example, autoscaler will maintain average CPU  
utilization across all pods of 50%.

kubectl autoscale deployment user-ms --cpu-percent=50 --min=1 –  
max=10

Horizontal autoscaling will not work if metrics-server addon is not  
enabled in Minikube. We can use addon list command to check  
enabled/disabled addons. If metrics-server addon is not enabled  
we can enable it with addon enable command.  
minikube addons list  
minikube addons enable metrics-server

We a little bit. We use same database for all instances of user  
microservice and that does not follow microservice concepts. Each

microservice should have its own database. Database problems  
are out of the scope of this book but we will issue some


problems and discuss potential solutions without implementation  
details.

Obviously, simplest solution is to create new database for each  
microservice instance and change value in conﬁguration ﬁle (we  
have enough knowledge to know how to do that). Second a little

bit more complex solution is to run database inside same pod  
where we run our microservice. This solution requires from us to  
Dockerize our database.

The real problem here is how to maintain consistency between  
databases? For example, we have ﬁve instances of user  
microservice. One of the instances adds new user to her  
database. If that user tries to login and Load Balancer deliver  
login request to instance that does not have information about

new user, login attempt will be rejected.

All database solution provides way how to set up to propagate  
changes (insertion, deletion, update) between each other. This is  
complex topic, but problem is not unsolvable.

Scaling microservices without database is simple straightforward  
job. Microservices with databases are little bit more complex to

scale but it’s manageable. We should always follow our system  
metrics and scale up or down based on them.


9.5 Summary

We learned how to deploy and scale our microservices using

modern tools. With topics covered in this chapter, we should be  
able to deploy eﬃcient microservice system. Now it is a perfect  
time to summarize everything that we learned in previous

chapters. System that we developed is not perfect and we will  
also discuss about some potential improvements.


9.5.1 REST server application

We started this book with some basics of Go programming

language. Our plan was to use this knowledge and create small  
REST Server Application for library. Nowadays, everything is on  
internet, so client-server approach is perfect for modern

applications.

REST Server Applications are quite simple. Send HTTP Request  
from Client Application to Server Application. Server Application  
will process HTTP Request, create HTTP Response and send it to

Client Application.

Go is a pretty light, has native support for server applications  
and does not require additional resources (like JVM for Java).  
With all positives, Go is a good candidate for developing Server

Applications. For a ﬁrst version of our system we create single,  
monolith application with basic functionalities.


9.5.2 Microservices

Microservice architecture is architectural style that represents

collection of services. Each service performs speciﬁc (small set) of  
actions. Services are loosely bounded(communicate with each  
other) in order to provide desired system functionality.

We have developed monolith application for library. With key parts

deﬁned we can break our monolith application into several  
microservices. Generally, this is a good approach, especially for  
small start-ups, create monolith version with basic functionality

and then broke it to microservices.

Breaking microservices can be complex task. We should have  
concepts of microservice architecture on our mind when we  
design initial, monolith, version of our application. This will help

us to develop application and quite simplify complex task.

It’s much easier to add new functionality into microservice  
architecture. We just need to develop new microservice and  
import it into system. In monolith application, we must change  
and recompile whole code and that can be a quite of a problem.

Microservices are useful concept. We can use it to develop faster  
and better systems with good performances.


9.5.3 Future of microservices

In last decade microservices become one of the most popular

software architectural style. A lot of big information technologies  
players like Netﬂix, Amazon and eBay upgraded their solutions to  
microservices. Monolith application will not become deprecated

(some applications will still perform better as monolith) but will  
become minority.

Microservices are now in speciﬁc phase. This architecture  
becomes more and more popular and a lot of applications are

currently in redesign, from monolith to microservices. Number of  
applications that are developed from start as microservices grows  
on daily base.

If this trends continues, microservices will be dominant software

architecture. Increasing speed of network will further amplify this  
trend. Developers who want to stay in with current trends should  
become familiar with microservice architecture a soon as possible.

Microservice architecture is improvement of Service Oriented  
Architecture Maybe microservices will not be present in this form  
in ten year. Someone can create something new, improvement of  
microservices, some sort of One thing is sure, microservices are

here, and will stay here in years to come.


9.5.4 What next?

Application that we developed through this book is not absolute

perfection. There is a lot of space for improvement so here we  
will point some of them.

Conﬁguration is not ﬂexible. If we want to change something we  
need to change conﬁguration ﬁle and restart application. We can

create some conﬁguration “proﬁles” and generic template for toml  
ﬁle. Based on required speciﬁc conﬁguration ﬁle will be created  
and used in application.

We need to add unit and integration tests for our applications.

Good test can provide better code quality and help us to spot  
mistakes that can break functionality of our system. With properly  
created test a lot of problems can be prevented.

Email functionality can be introduced into current system. With

this functionality librarian can communicate with member. All kind  
of information can be propagated via email. For example, new  
book arrived into library, librarian can send broadcast email and  
inform all members.

Most important functionality that can be added ability to create  
new system user. Now we can only add user with direct insert  
into database. If we expand our user microservice, user can ﬁll


form on client-side application and create account. This is an

essential feature and all modern applications provide it.

If you have some other ideas how library application can be  
improved, try it. Always it is good to practice our skills, with  
practice we all becomes better developers.


Conclusion

We passed a long road. At the end, we have library solution, that

can be (with small improvements) used in real life.

In this book we followed complete development process. We  
learned new programming language, become familiar with some  
concepts (REST and Microservices), designed and developed two

diﬀerent version of system (Monolith and Microservice). At the  
end we learned how to deploy and scale our application.

Now, we can continue to learn and improve skills learned in this  
book. Continual learning will help us to become better software

developers.


Points to remember

We should avoid deployment on local machine, and use

specialized host services.

We should analyze performance of our system to decide when  
and which microservice to scale.

We should always Dockerize our applications.

We should use Kubernetes in combination with Docker.

We should scale our system based on some metrics.

Client-server approach is perfect for modern applications.

We should have concepts of microservice architecture on our

mind during development of monolith application.

Microservice architecture will probably become dominant software  
architecture.

We should practice our skills.

adding new resources.

Docker Image.
